Topic 01 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Multinomial Logistic Regression

Logistic Regression for Data with Polychotomous (Nominal) Outcomes · Kamarul Imran Musa · USM 2022

Learning Objectives

  1. Understand the concept of logistic regression model to analyze data with polychotomous (multinomial) outcome
  2. Estimate parameters of interest in a logistic regression model from data with polychotomous (multinomial) outcome
  3. Make inference based on a logistic regression model from data with polychotomous (multinomial) outcome
  4. Predict the outcome based on a logistic regression model from data with polychotomous (multinomial) outcome
  5. Perform model checking of logistic regression model from data with polychotomous (multinomial) outcomes

Motivation

Some data come with multinomial outcomes in which case the outcome variable is a nominal or polychotomous variable with more than two levels. In multinomial outcome data, the outcome has no natural ordering. If it has ordering, then it is best treated as an ordinal outcome. For these data, we can modify the binary logistic model to make estimation and inference (Kleinbaum 2010; Lemeshow and Hosmer Jr. 2013).

Terminology

Variables with more than two levels are known as either:

  • Multinomial data
  • Polychotomous data
  • Polytomous data

If we employ logistic regression to such data, then the analysis is known as polytomous or multinomial logistic regression. The polychotomous outcome does NOT have any natural order. When categories DO have a natural order, ordinal logistic regression is preferred.

Examples of Multinomial Outcome Data

  • Disease symptoms classified by subjects as being absent, mild, moderate, or severe
  • Tumour invasiveness classified as in situ, locally invasive, or metastatic
  • Patient preferred treatment regimen selected from three or more options: oral medication only, oral + injection, or injection only
  • Program type chosen by high school students: general, vocational, and academic programs
  • Fetal health classification: Normal, Suspect, Pathological (from Cardiotocogram CTG exams)
Important Distinction

A numerical outcome can be categorised based on different cut-off points. The newly created categorical variable is then either treated as a nominal/polychotomous/multinomial outcome OR as an ordinal outcome. That justifies the use of multinomial logistic regression.

Multinomial Logit Model — Key Concept

With multinomial outcome data, an extension of logistic regression known as the multinomial logit or multinomial logistic regression can be performed.

  • One of the categories of the outcome variable is designated as the reference category and each of the other levels is compared with this reference
  • The choice of reference category can be arbitrary and is at the discretion of the researcher
  • Most software sets the first category as the reference (also known as the baseline category) by default
  • Multinomial logistic regression can be thought of as simultaneously fitting binary logits for all comparisons among the alternatives

Other Models for Polychotomous Outcomes

  • Stereotype logistic regression — each independent variable has one value for each individual
  • Alternative-specific variables
  • (These are beyond the scope of this lecture)

Research Question Justification (from practical)

When high school students choose the program (general, vocational, and academic programs), how do their gender, math and science scores and their social economic status (SES) affect their decision? Justification for multinomial logistic regression:

  • The researcher would like to study the influence of several factors on the outcome variable
  • The outcome is a polychotomous categorical variable with more than two levels and has no natural ordering
  • Multinomial logistic regression allows for modeling the probabilities of the outcome categories relative to a chosen reference category

Why not alternatives?

  • Multinomial probit regression — similar to multinomial logistic regression with independent normal error terms
  • Multiple-group discriminant function analysis — a multivariate method for multinomial outcome variables
  • Multiple logistic regression analyses (one for each pair of outcomes) — problem: each analysis potentially runs on a different sample; without constraining the logistic models, probability of all outcome categories could exceed 1
  • Collapsing number of categories to two — suffers from loss of information and changes the original research questions
  • Ordinal logistic regression — if the outcome variable is truly ordered AND satisfies the assumption of proportional odds, switching to ordinal logistic regression will make the model more parsimonious
  • Alternative-specific multinomial probit regression — allows different error structures, relaxing the IIA assumption; requires choice-specific data structure
  • Nested logit model — another way to relax the IIA assumption; also requires choice-specific data structure
Core Assumptions of Multinomial Logistic Regression
  1. Outcome is nominal (no ordering) — The dependent variable is categorical with more than two unordered categories. If ordering exists, use ordinal logistic regression.
  2. Independence of irrelevant alternatives (IIA) — The odds of choosing one category over another are not affected by the presence or absence of other categories.
  3. No multicollinearity — Independent variables should not be highly correlated with each other.
  4. Independence of observations — Each observation is independent.
  5. No perfect separation — Predictors should not perfectly predict the outcome.
  6. Large sample size required — MLE requires adequate sample size. Rule of thumb: at least 10–15 cases per parameter estimated.
Note on Estimation

The dependent variable is a discrete variable and the model is estimated using maximum likelihood. The actual values taken by the dependent variable are irrelevant — only their labels/categories matter. In Stata, the exponentiated beta \(\exp^{\beta}\) generates the relative-risk ratio (RRR).

Log Odds (Logit Functions)

For a dataset with the outcome variable \(Y\) coded as \(0, 1,\) or \(2\), with \(Y=0\) as the reference:

Log odds for comparison between 0 and 1:

\[g_1(x) = \ln\frac{P(D=1|X_1)}{P(D=0|X_1)} = \alpha_1 + (\beta_{11}X_1)\]

Log odds for comparison between 0 and 2:

\[g_2(x) = \ln\frac{P(D=2|X_1)}{P(D=0|X_1)} = \alpha_2 + (\beta_{21}X_1)\]

Odds Ratios

The odds ratio of outcome \(Y = j\) versus \(Y = 0\) for covariate values \(x = a\) versus \(x = b\) is:

\[OR_{j}(a,b) = \frac{Pr(Y=j|x=a)/Pr(Y=0|x=a)}{Pr(Y=j|x=b)/Pr(Y=0|x=b)}\]

Each odds ratio is calculated as:

\[OR_1(X=1,X=0) = \frac{Pr(D=1|X=1)/Pr(D=0|X=1)}{Pr(Y=1|X=0)/Pr(D=0|X=0)} = \exp^{\beta_{11}}\] \[OR_2(X=2,X=0) = \frac{Pr(D=2|X=1)/Pr(D=0|X=1)}{Pr(D=2|X=0)/Pr(D=0|X=0)} = \exp^{\beta_{21}}\]

Conditional Probabilities

For an outcome with 3 levels (0, 1, 2), the conditional probabilities are:

\[P(D = 0 | x) = \frac{1}{1+e^{g_1(x)} + e^{g_2(x)}}\] \[P(D = 1 | x) = \frac{e^{g_1(x)}}{1+e^{g_1(x)} + e^{g_2(x)}}\] \[P(D = 2 | x) = \frac{e^{g_2(x)}}{1+e^{g_1(x)} + e^{g_2(x)}}\]

Note: The sum \(P(D=0|X) + P(D=1|X) + P(D=2|X) = 1\)

General Form for Multiple Covariates

\[h_1(X) = \alpha_1 + \sum_{i=1}^{k}\beta_{1i}X_i\] \[h_2(X) = \alpha_2 + \sum_{i=1}^{k}\beta_{2i}X_i\] \[P(D=0|X) = \frac{1}{1 + \exp[h_1(X)] + \exp[h_2(X)]}\] \[P(D=1|X) = \frac{\exp[h_1(X)]}{1 + \exp[h_1(X)] + \exp[h_2(X)]}\] \[P(D=2|X) = \frac{\exp[h_2(X)]}{1 + \exp[h_1(X)] + \exp[h_2(X)]}\]
Important Note on Probabilities

In multinomial logistic regression for data with three categories, the sum of the probabilities for the three outcome categories must equal 1. However, each comparison considers only two probabilities, and those two probabilities do not sum to 1. Thus, the two odds-like expressions in multinomial logistic regression are not true odds.

Model with Interaction

If covariates \(X_1\) and \(X_2\) interact meaningfully, then the log odds become:

\[\text{log-odds} = a_g + (\beta_{g1} \times X_1) + (\beta_{g2} \times X_2) + (\beta_{g3} \times X_1X_2)\]

where \(g = 1, 2\)

Key Interpretive Concepts

In Stata, the exponentiated beta \(\exp^{\beta}\) generates the Relative-Risk Ratio (RRR). Interpreting and assessing the significance of estimated coefficients are the main objectives in regression analysis.

Example 1: Placement for Hospitalized Adolescents

Taken from Chapter 8, Applied Logistic Regression (3rd Edition) — Lemeshow and Hosmer Jr. 2013

  • Outcome = Placement, coded as: 0 = Outpatient, 1 = Intermediate, 2 = Residential
  • Covariate = History of VIOLENCE (VIOL), coded as 0 = No, 1 = Yes
  • Research Question: Does placement for hospitalized adolescents depend on history of violence?
LogitVarCoefSERRR95% OR
1 (Intermediate vs Outpatient)VIOL0.5810.261.791.08, 2.96
CONS-1.1240.23——
2 (Residential vs Outpatient)VIOL1.1310.313.11.70, 5.66
CONS-1.6740.28——
Interpretation — Log Odds
  • The estimated log odds among adolescents with history of violence (VIOL=1) to be placed in an intermediate facility (Placement=1) is \(-1.124 + 0.581 \times 1 = -0.543\)
  • The estimated log odds among adolescents without history of violence (VIOL=0) to be placed in an intermediate facility (Placement=1) is \(-1.124 + 0.581 \times 0 = -1.124\)
Interpretation — Odds Ratio (RRR)

The estimated odds among adolescents with history of violence (VIOL=1) to be placed in an intermediate facility (Placement=1) is \(\exp(0.581) = 1.79\) times greater than the odds among adolescents without a history of violence. The confidence interval indicates that the odds could be as little as 1.1 times or as much as 3 times larger with 95% confidence.

Example 2: Cancer Type (from Kleinbaum, page 439)

With polytomous logistic regression, one of the categories is the reference and each other level is compared with this reference.

  • Outcome = Cancer type: AdenoCA = 0, AdenoSQ = 1, Other = 2 (Reference = Adenocarcinoma)
  • Covariate = AGEGP: older subjects (65–79) = 1, younger subjects (50–64) = 0
VariableEstimateS.E.Symbol
Intercept1-1.45340.2618\(\alpha_2\)
Intercept2-1.94590.3223\(\alpha_1\)
AGEGP (Other vs AdenoCA)0.42560.3215\(\beta_{21}\)
AGEGP (AdenoSQ vs AdenoCA)0.78090.3775\(\beta_{11}\)

Note: The Adenocarcinoma is the reference group. This table comes from SAS software. In SAS, if outcome=0 is the reference, the output is listed in descending order.

Interpretation — Cancer Example
  • The estimated log odds of Other (category 2) vs. Adenocarcinoma (category 0): \(-1.4534 + 0.4256 \times age\). Exponentiating the beta for age gives an estimated OR of 1.53.
  • The estimated log odds of Adenosquamous (category 1) vs. Adenocarcinoma (category 0): \(-1.9459 + 0.7809 \times age\). Exponentiating the beta for AGEGP gives an estimated OR of 2.18.

Full interpretation: For women diagnosed with primary endometrial cancer, older subjects (aged 65–79) relative to younger subjects (aged 50–64) were more likely to have their tumors categorised as Other than as Adenocarcinoma (\(\widehat{OR}_2 = 1.53\)) and were even more likely to have their tumors classified as Adenosquamous than as Adenocarcinoma (\(\widehat{OR}_1 = 2.18\)).

Interpretation: Numerical vs. Categorical Covariates

Continuous Covariate
Any continuous covariate modeled as linear in the logit has a single estimated coefficient for each logit function. Exponentiation gives the estimated OR for a change of one unit in the variable.
Binary Covariate
For binary covariates coded (0,1): \(OR_j = OR_j(1,0) = \exp(\beta_j)\). Estimation of ORs for clinically meaningful changes applies directly to each logit function.

Example Interpretation from CTG Dataset (Student Practical)

Baseline Variable — Pathological vs Normal

Every increment of 1 unit in the baseline variable, controlling for other factors, the odds of being in the pathological group (in comparison to being in the normal group) change by 0.98 (95% CI: 0.96, 0.99, p-value 0.004). The 95% CI does not include 1, indicating a statistically significant decrease in the odds of being in the pathological group as baseline increases.

Baseline Variable — Suspect vs Normal

Every increment of 1 unit in the baseline variable, controlling for other factors, the odds of being in the suspect group change by 1.11 (95% CI: 1.08, 1.11, p-value <0.001). The 95% CI does not include 1, indicating a statistically significant increase in the odds of being in the suspect group as baseline increases.

Hosmer–Lemeshow Model Building Steps
  1. Univariable analysis: Select variables with p-values of 0.25 or below for the next step. Identify covariates that need recategorisation.
  2. Fit multivariable model: Assess Wald statistics and likelihood ratio to add or eliminate covariates. Identify variables that change other coefficients by 20 percent or more if excluded from the model — these might be confounders.
  3. Check linearity of logits for numerical covariates. This can be done using fractional polynomials or design variables.
  4. Add interaction variables to the model. Choose based on clinical importance or p-values (based on LR test). At this stage it is known as the preliminary final model.
  5. Assess model fitness. This can be done by doing separate binary logistic regression and testing the fitness for each regression.

General Workflow

  • Read data
  • Explore data (EDA: summaries, plots)
  • Build model
  • Assess model
  • Make prediction
  • Interpret

Always start your analysis with a new project in RStudio.

Model Comparison Strategy

Results from VGAM::vglm and nnet::multinom are similar. In Stata, run mlogit place3 age race dangerd behav los custd. The results are easier to look at in Stata. The mlogit package in R requires data to be in wide format. nnet::multinom does not provide p-values directly.

Types of Inferences

  1. Hypothesis testing
  2. Interval estimation around parameters

Hypothesis Testing

Hypothesis testing can be done by:

  • Examining the p-values based on Wald statistics. If the level of significance is set at 0.05, then p-values less than 0.05 indicate statistically significant association between the covariate and the outcome.
  • Calculating the confidence intervals. CIs for the estimated log odds that do not include 0 indicate statistical significance at 0.05 level. CIs for estimated ORs that do not include 1 indicate a significant association.

Confidence Intervals

95% CI for estimated \(\hat\beta\):

\[\hat\beta \pm 1.96 \times SE(\hat\beta)\]

95% CI for estimated \(\widehat{OR}\):

\[\exp^{\hat\beta \pm 1.96 \times SE(\hat\beta)}\]

Likelihood Ratio Test (LR Test)

Adding or removing a covariate from a model can be assessed using the likelihood ratio test. We calculate minus twice the change in the log-likelihood between two models.

\[LR_{test} = -2 \ln L_{reduced} - (-2 \ln L_{full}) \sim \chi^2\]
Worked Example — LR Test

If model A has log-likelihood = −524.371 and model B has log-likelihood = −510.213:

\[G = -2 \times [-524.371 - (-510.213)] = 28.316\]

The null hypothesis is that the two models are not different. The test statistic follows the chi-square distribution with degrees of freedom equal to the number of parameters set to zero under the null hypothesis.

Wald Test

A Wald test can also be performed. The null hypothesis for each level of interest is that the beta coefficient equals zero.

\[H_0: \beta_{11} = 0, \quad H_0: \beta_{21} = 0\] \[Z = \frac{\hat\beta_{g1}}{SE_{\hat\beta_{g1}}}\]
Example Wald Statistics (Cancer Example)
\[Z_{1-0} = \frac{0.7809}{0.3775} = 2.07\] \[Z_{2-0} = \frac{0.4256}{0.3215} = 1.32\]

Model Assessment

Assessment can be done via a few methods:

  • Assess the overall model fitness (see page 284–290 Applied Logistic Regression) — compare observed and predicted frequencies
  • Assess the estimated probabilities
  • Check the value of delta beta, delta \(\chi^2\)
  • Assess the effect of removing subjects with high value of delta beta
Prediction Options
  • The expected number of outcome
  • Probability for each outcome
  • Delta chi square
  • Delta beta
  • ROC curve is NOT appropriate for multinomial outcomes

Key R Packages for Multinomial Logistic Regression

VGAM::vglm()
Main package. Reference = largest level. Provides z-values and p-values. Results identical to Stata's mlogit.
nnet::multinom()
Easier output but does NOT provide p-values directly. Reference = smallest level (by default).
haven
Read .dta (Stata) files. Use read_dta()
tidyverse
Data wrangling and plots
gtsummary
Publication-quality summary tables
broom
Tidy model output

Practical 1: Mammography Experience Dataset (mammog9.dta)

Source: UCLA IDRE — Applied Logistic Regression Chapter 8

Dataset Variables — mammog9.dta (n=412)
  • me: outcome — mammography experience (0 = never, 1 = within a year, 2 = over a year ago)
  • sympt: "You do not need a mammogram unless you develop symptoms." (1=Strongly Agree, 2=Agree, 3=Disagree, 4=Strongly Disagree)
  • pb: Perceived benefit of mammography. Score 5–20
  • hist: Mother or Sister with history of breast cancer (0=No, 1=Yes)
  • bse: Has anyone taught you BSE? (0=No, 1=Yes)
  • detc: "How likely is it that a mammogram could find a new case of breast cancer?" (1=Not likely, 2=Somewhat likely, 3=Very likely)

Step 1: Load Libraries and Read Data

Load libraries and read data
# General packages
library(here)

dat.m1 <- read_dta('mammog9.dta')

# Check data structure

Step 2: Data Wrangling — Convert to Factor Variables

Create factor variables and relevel for reference category
# Convert me to factor with labels
dat.m1 <- 
  dat.m1 %>% 
  mutate(me2 = factor(me, 
                      labels = c("never", "within.a.year", "over.a.year.ago")))

# Check the count by level
dat.m1 %>% count(me2)

# Convert hist to factor
dat.m1 <- 
  dat.m1 %>% 
  mutate(hist2 = factor(hist, labels = c("no", "yes")))

# For VGAM, we need to relevel: the LAST group becomes the reference
# Relevel me2 so that 'never' is the last level (= reference)
dat.m1 <- 
  dat.m1 %>% 
  mutate(me3 = fct_relevel(me2, c("over.a.year.ago", 'within.a.year', 'never')))

# Verify levels
levels(dat.m1$me2) # "never" "within.a.year" "over.a.year.ago"
levels(dat.m1$me3) # "over.a.year.ago" "within.a.year" "never"

# Descriptive table
dat.m1 %>%
  tbl_summary(by = me2,
              statistic = list(all_continuous() ~ "{mean} ({sd})"))
Stata data wrangling
* Label variables (if not already labelled)
label define melab 0 "never" 1 "within.a.year" 2 "over.a.year.ago"
label values me melab
tab me

Step 3: Estimation using VGAM::vglm()

Reference Category — VGAM::vglm

In VGAM::vglm, the reference or base outcome is the LAST (largest) level. The comparisons are:

  • Group 1 vs Group 3: over a year ago vs never
  • Group 2 vs Group 3: within a year vs never

So, the reference group is "never".

Fit simple multinomial model with VGAM::vglm
library(VGAM)

# Fit model: me3 (over.a.year.ago, within.a.year, never) ~ hist2
# Reference = "never" (last level)
fitmlog1 <- vglm(me3 ~ hist2, multinomial, data = dat.m1)
summary(fitmlog1)

# Output interpretation:
# (Intercept):1  → outcome me = over a year ago vs never
# (Intercept):2  → outcome me = within a year vs never
# hist2yes:1     → over a year ago vs never
# hist2yes:2     → within a year vs never
Stata: mlogit
* In Stata, run mlogit
mlogit me hist, baseoutcome(0)
mlogit place3 age race dangerd behav los custd
SPSS
* Not covered in lecture notes for this step

Step 4: Inferences — CIs and RRR

Calculate CIs and Relative-Risk Ratios
# Get beta coefficients
b_fitmlog2 <- coef(fitmlog2)

# Get 95% confidence intervals
ci_fitmlog2 <- confint(fitmlog2)

# Combine beta and CIs
b_ci_fitmlog2 <- cbind(b_fitmlog2, ci_fitmlog2)

# Exponentiate to get Relative-Risk Ratios (RRR)
rrr_fitmlog2 <- exp(b_ci_fitmlog2)

# Combine all
tab_fitmlog2 <- cbind(b_ci_fitmlog2, rrr_fitmlog2)
colnames(tab_fitmlog2) <- c('b', 'lower b', 'upper b', 
                            'rrr', 'lower rrr', 'upper rrr')
tab_fitmlog2

# CI for vglm (separate call)
confint(fitmlog1)
#                    2.5 %     97.5 %
# (Intercept):1 -1.5305586 -0.9704270
# (Intercept):2 -1.2012851 -0.7006674
# hist2yes:1     0.1714464  1.8472152
# hist2yes:2     0.5220375  1.9906784
Stata: RRR and CIs
* mlogit shows RRR automatically with rrr option
mlogit me hist, baseoutcome(0) rrr
mlogit place3 age race dangerd behav los custd, rrr

Step 5: Multivariable Model with Multiple Covariates (APS.dta)

Dataset: APS.dta — Adolescent Placement Study

Outcome: place3 (zero = outpatient, one = intermediate, two = residential). Predictors: age, race, dangerd, behav, custd.

Multivariable VGAM model
library(foreign)
dat.m2 <- read.dta('APS.dta', convert.factors = TRUE)

# Change levels: one, two VS zero (base outcome)
dat.m2$place3b <- factor(dat.m2$place3, c("one", "two", "zero"))
levels(dat.m2$place3b) # "one" "two" "zero"

# Fit multivariable multinomial model
fit.m2 <- vglm(place3b ~ age + race + dangerd + behav +
                 custd, multinomial, data = dat.m2)
summary(fit.m2)

# Get Relative-Risk Ratios
exp(coef(fit.m2))
Stata multivariable mlogit
* Full multivariable model
mlogit place3 age race dangerd behav los custd
mlogit place3 age race dangerd behav los custd, rrr
SPSS
* Not covered in lecture notes for this step

Step 6: Using nnet::multinom()

nnet::multinom — Reference Category

Unlike VGAM::vglm (reference = last/largest level), nnet::multinom uses the smallest level as the reference or base outcome. Use relevel() to change the reference category.

nnet::multinom — including p-values
library(nnet)

# Change reference category using relevel()
dat.m1 <- dat.m1 %>%
  mutate(me3_relev = relevel(me3, ref = "never"))

# Fit model
mlog_nnet_rel <- multinom(me3_relev ~ hist2, data = dat.m1)
summary(mlog_nnet_rel)

# nnet::multinom does NOT provide p-values directly
# Calculate p-values manually (Wald z-test):
z.test <- summary(mlog_nnet_rel)$coefficients / 
           summary(mlog_nnet_rel)$standard.errors
# Two-tailed p-value
p.val <- (1 - pnorm(abs(z.test), 0, 1)) * 2
p.val

# CI for nnet::multinom
confint(mlog_nnet_rel, level = 0.95)

# Full model (APS.dta) with multinom
fit.m3 <- multinom(place3 ~ age + race + dangerd + behav +
                 custd, data = dat.m2)
summary(fit.m3)
Stata — equivalent
* Stata mlogit automatically provides p-values
mlogit place3 age race dangerd behav custd, baseoutcome(0)
SPSS
* Not covered in lecture notes for this step

Step 7: Prediction

Prediction — probabilities and probability plots
# Predicted probabilities for first 6 observations
head(predict.vgam(fitmlog1, type = 'response'))
#   over.a.year.ago within.a.year     never
# 1       0.1711957     0.2309783 0.5978261
# 3       0.2500000     0.4318182 0.3181818

# Manual calculation for 1st observation:
# log odds: over.a.year.ago = -1.2504928, within.a.year = -0.9509763

# P(never) = reference group
1/(1 + exp(-1.2504928) + exp(-0.9509763))
# [1] 0.5978261

# P(over.a.year.ago)
exp(-1.2504928)/(1 + exp(-1.2504928) + exp(-0.9509763))
# [1] 0.1711957

# P(within.a.year)
exp(-0.9509763)/(1 + exp(-1.2504928) + exp(-0.9509763))
# [1] 0.2309783

# Probability plot (hsbdemo dataset)
pp <- cbind(hsbdemo1, predict(mn1, newdata = hsbdemo1, type = "response"))
pp_long <- pp %>%
  pivot_longer(cols = c("vocation", "academic", "general"),
               names_to = "prog3", values_to = "predicted_probability")
ggplot(pp_long, aes(x = math, y = predicted_probability)) +
  geom_smooth(method = "loess", se = FALSE) +
  facet_grid(vars(prog3), scales = "free")

Practical 2: hsbdemo Dataset (High School & Beyond)

Dataset: hsbdemo.dta (n=200)

Outcome: prog (program type: 1=general, 2=academic, 3=vocational)

Predictors: ses (social economic status: 1=low, 2=middle, 3=high), math score (continuous), science score (continuous), female (0=male, 1=female)

Source: UCLA IDRE hsbdemo.dta

Full hsbdemo practical
# Load data from URL
hsbdemo <- read_dta("https://stats.idre.ucla.edu/stat/data/hsbdemo.dta")

# Convert labelled to factors
hsbdemo <- hsbdemo %>%
  mutate(across(where(~is.labelled(.x)), as_factor))

# Select variables of interest
hsbdemo1 <- hsbdemo %>%
  select(prog, female, math, science, ses)

# Exploratory plots
ggplot(hsbdemo1, aes(x = prog, fill = female)) + geom_bar()
ggplot(hsbdemo1, aes(x = prog, fill = ses)) + geom_bar()

# Descriptive table
hsbdemo1 %>% tbl_summary(by = prog,
              statistic = list(all_continuous() ~ "{mean} ({sd})"))

# Confirm levels
levels(hsbdemo1$prog) # "general" "academic" "vocation"

# Relevel for VGAM: last group = reference = general
hsbdemo1 <- hsbdemo1 %>%
  mutate(prog2 = fct_relevel(prog, c("vocation", 'academic', 'general')))
levels(hsbdemo1$prog2) # "vocation" "academic" "general"

# Fit null model (female only)
mn.gen <- vglm(prog2 ~ female, multinomial, data = hsbdemo1)

# Fit full model
mn1 <- vglm(prog2 ~ female + math + science + ses, 
             multinomial, data = hsbdemo1)
summary(mn1)

# Likelihood Ratio Test
library(lmtest)
lrtest(mn.gen, mn1)
# Log likelihoods: model 1 = -204.0703, model 2 = -169.5161
# LRT = 69.1084, df=8, p-value = 7.394e-12
Stata — hsbdemo

estimates store full_model

Quick Reference — Multinomial Logistic Regression

When to useNominal outcome with 3+ unordered categories
Reference categoryArbitrary; researcher's choice. VGAM = last level; nnet = first level
Effect measureRelative-Risk Ratio (RRR) = exp(β)
Logit functionsOne per comparison (J-1 logit functions for J categories)
Probabilities sumAll J probabilities sum to 1
Hypothesis testsWald test, Likelihood Ratio Test
Model comparisonLR test: −2(logL_reduced − logL_full) ~ χ²
ROC curveNOT appropriate for multinomial outcomes
R packagesVGAM::vglm() [preferred], nnet::multinom()
Stata commandmlogit outcome predictors, rrr
Key referencesKleinbaum 2010 (Ch.12); Hosmer & Lemeshow 2013 (Ch.8)
Software Comparison
  • VGAM::vglm and nnet::multinom: Results are similar; reference category differs
  • Stata mlogit: Results are easier to interpret; provides RRR directly
  • mlogit package (R): Requires data in wide format
  • nnet::multinom: Does NOT provide p-values — calculate manually using Wald z-test
Topic 02 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Ordinal Logistic Regression

Logistic Regression for Data with Ordinal Outcomes · Kamarul Imran Musa · USM 2022

Learning Objectives

  1. Understand the concept of logistic model for data with ordinal outcome
  2. Perform estimation using univariable and multivariable logistic model for data with ordinal outcome
  3. Perform inferences using univariable and multivariable logistic model for data with ordinal outcome
  4. Make prediction using univariable and multivariable logistic model for data with ordinal outcome
  5. Check for model adequacy and perform model diagnostics
  6. Test the proportional odds assumption

Motivation

Some data come with ordinal outcomes. This means that the outcome variable:

  • Has more than 2 categories or levels
  • Has categories in an order or rank that is important to the analyst

To analyse data with ordinal outcomes, you may use multinomial logistic model. The caveat is that such analysis (multinomial) does not address the ordinal nature of outcomes. One of the better analyses is ordinal logistic regression. For ordinal response variable \(y\) with \(c\) categories, our focus is on modelling how \(P(y=j), j=1,2,...,c,\) depends on explanatory variable(s) \(x\).

Conditions for Using Ordinal Logistic Regression
  • Outcome is categorical
  • Outcome has more than 2 categories or levels
  • Outcome is ordinal — the order or rank is important
  • Multinomial logistic regression does not address the ordinality

Research Question Examples

  • Disease symptoms classified as absent, mild, moderate, or severe
  • Birth weight categories: <2500g, 2501–3000g, 3001–3500g, >3500g
  • Wine bitterness rated on a scale of 1 to 5
  • Blood pressure: <120, 121–140, 141–160, >160 mmHg
  • Decision to apply to graduate school: unlikely, somewhat likely, very likely

Research Question (Practical Example)

Example Study — Graduate School Application

A study looks at factors that influence the decision of whether to apply to graduate school. College juniors are asked if they are unlikely, somewhat likely, or very likely to apply to graduate school. Hence, our outcome variable has three categories. Data on parental educational status, whether the undergraduate institution is public or private, and current GPA is also collected.

The researchers have reason to believe that the "distances" between these three points are not equal. For example, the "distance" between "unlikely" and "somewhat likely" may be shorter than the distance between "somewhat likely" and "very likely".

Justification for Ordinal vs Multinomial

  • If the outcome variable is truly ordered AND satisfies the assumption of proportional odds, ordinal logistic regression will make the model more parsimonious
  • Ordinal logistic regression exploits the ordered nature — multinomial ignores it
  • For ordinal logistic regression, estimation is performed using maximum likelihood
Key Assumptions of Ordinal Logistic Regression
  1. Outcome is ordinal — categories have a meaningful order
  2. Proportional Odds Assumption (most critical) — the relationship between each pair of outcome groups is the same; the odds ratio is invariant to where categories are dichotomised
  3. Independence of observations
  4. No perfect multicollinearity among predictors
  5. No perfect separation
  6. Adequate sample size — at least 10 cases per predictor

Proportional Odds Assumption — Explained

The odds ratio is invariant to where the outcome categories are dichotomised. For example, for a data with outcomes 0 to 4 and exposure groups E=1 vs E=0:

\[OR(D \geq 1) = OR(D \geq 4)\] \[OR_{D \geq 1} = \frac{\text{odds}[(D \geq 1) | E = 1]}{\text{odds}[(D \geq 1) | E = 0]}\] \[OR_{D \geq 4} = \frac{\text{odds}[(D \geq 4) | E = 1]}{\text{odds}[(D \geq 4) | E = 0]}\]

Under the proportional odds assumption, both ORs are equal.

Implication of Proportional Odds

If there are \(G\) outcome categories, there is only one parameter \(\beta\) for each predictor (e.g., \(\beta_1\) for predictor \(X_1\)). However, there is a separate intercept term \(\alpha_g\) for each of the \(G-1\) comparisons. This is more parsimonious than multinomial logistic regression.

When Proportional Odds Fails

If the proportional odds assumption is violated, the simplest approach is to fall back to multinomial logistic regression. Other options include the partial proportional odds model.

The failure to reject the null hypothesis does NOT necessarily mean the proportional odds assumption is reasonable — it could be that there are not enough data to provide statistical evidence to reject the null.

Three Models for Ordinal Outcomes

1. Adjacent-Category Logit
Compares each response to the next larger response. Log-odds does not depend on the response; is linear in coefficients. Uses local odds ratios.
2. Continuation-Ratio Logit
Compares each response to ALL LOWER responses: Y=k versus Y<k. Has different constant terms AND different slopes for each logit. Often used in survival/disease progression.
3. Proportional Odds Model
Most widely used. Cumulative logit model. Compares probability of equal or smaller response Y≤k to probability of larger response Y>k. One β per predictor.

Adjacent-Category Logit Model

\[\log \frac{P(y_i = j)}{P(y_i = j+1)} = \alpha_j + \beta x_i\]

Interpretation uses local odds ratios instead of cumulative odds ratios. Can have either the proportional odds form or the non-proportional odds form. In VGAM::vglm, the parallel argument can be set to TRUE (proportional) or FALSE (non-proportional).

Example (SMOKE covariate, from Applied Logistic Regression page 294):

First AC model: \(\alpha_1(SMOKE) = -0.110 + 0.370 \times SMOKE\)

Second AC model: \(\alpha_2(SMOKE) = -0.441 - (-0.110) + 0.370 \times SMOKE\)

Third AC model: \(\alpha_3(SMOKE) = -0.175 - (-0.441) + 0.370 \times SMOKE\)

Estimated OR: \(\widehat{OR}(k, k-1) = \exp(0.370) = 1.45\)

Interpretation — Adjacent-Category

The odds of a birth in the next lower weight category among women who smoke during pregnancy are 1.45 times the odds among women who do not smoke.

Continuation-Ratio Logit Model

Compares each response to ALL LOWER responses: \(Y=k\) versus \(Y

Interpretation — Continuation-Ratio

Unconstrained model: The three estimated coefficients are quite similar (all approximately 0.6). The odds of a birth in the next lower weight category relative to higher weight categories among women who smoked during pregnancy is about \(1.8 = \exp(0.6)\) times that of women who did not smoke.

Constrained model: OR for smoking = \(\exp(0.627) = 1.87\)

Proportional Odds Model (Cumulative Logit Model)

The most commonly used method to analyze data with ordered outcome categories. Describes a "less than or equal to versus more" comparison.

The model is \(P(y \leq j), j=1,2,...,c-1\), using logits:

\[\text{logit} \; P(y \leq j) = \log\left[\frac{P(y \leq j)}{P(y > j)}\right] = \alpha_j + \beta x, \quad j=1,...,c-1\]

The model satisfies:

\[\log\left[\frac{P(y \leq j | x_1)/P(y > j | x_1)}{P(y \leq j | x_2)/P(y > j | x_2)}\right] = \beta(x_1 - x_2)\]

where \(\beta\) is the cumulative log odds ratio for a 1-unit increase in predictor

Probabilities in Proportional Odds Model

\[P(D \geq g | X_1) = \frac{1}{1 + \exp[-(\alpha_g + \beta_1 X_1)]}\]

where \(g = 1, 2, ..., G-1\)

\[P(D < g | X_1) = 1 - P(D \geq g | X_1) = \frac{\exp[-(\alpha_g + \beta_1 X_1)]}{1 + \exp[-(\alpha_g + \beta_1 X_1)]}\]
STATA/SPSS vs SAS Formulation

STATA and SPSS use the alternative model formulation (compares \(Y \leq k\) to \(Y > k\)):

\[\ln \frac{P(Y \leq g | X)}{P(Y > g | X)} = \exp{-[\alpha_g - \sum_{j=1}^{p} \beta_i X_i]}\]

SAS uses the formulation WITHOUT negating the coefficient:

\[\ln \frac{P(Y \geq g | X)}{P(Y < g | X)} = \alpha_g + \sum_{j=1}^{p} \beta_i X_i\]

This means that betas from Stata/SPSS have the opposite sign to betas from SAS for the same model. Read page 469 in Kleinbaum (Logistic Regression: A Self-learning Text) and equation 8.25 (page 300) in Hosmer book.

Ordinal Logit Model (Full formulation)

For a subject \(i\) with values \(x_i\) on a set of independent variables, the estimated conditional distribution function is:

\[\hat{P}(y_i \leq j) = \frac{\exp(\alpha_j + \beta x_i)}{1 + \exp(\alpha_j + \beta x_i)}\]

The estimated probability of outcome \(j\) is:

\[\hat{P}(y_i = j) = \hat{P}(y_i \leq j) - \hat{P}(y_i \leq j-1)\]

The logit form (as used by R's ordinal/MASS packages):

\[\text{logit}(P(Y \leq g)) = \ln \frac{P(Y \leq g)}{P(Y > g)} = \beta_{0_g} - (\beta_1 X_1 + \dots + \beta_p X_p)\]

The predicted cumulative probabilities:

\[\hat{P}(Y \leq g) = \frac{e^{\hat{\beta}_{0_g} - (\hat{\beta}_1 X_1 + \dots + \hat{\beta}_p X_p)}}{1 + e^{\hat{\beta}_{0_g} - (\hat{\beta}_1 X_1 + \dots + \hat{\beta}_p X_p)}}\]

The Intercept (Threshold / Cutpoints)

The intercept \(\alpha_g\) is the log odds of \(D \geq g\) where all independent variables are equal to zero. The log odds of \(D \geq 1\) is greater than the log odds of \(D \geq 2\), since for \(D \geq 1\) the outcome can be in categories 1, 2, 3, or 4, whereas for \(D \geq 2\) the outcome can only be in categories 2, 3, or 4.

Note: There are no p-values displayed for the threshold coefficients because it usually does not make sense to test if they equal zero.

Interpretation: Numerical Covariate

Example — Numerical Covariate (Age)

Data with: outcome \(D\) of low, median and high (coded as \(g=0,1,2\)); covariate age (numerical variable). Estimated coefficients:

  • Intercept (threshold): \(\alpha_j\)
  • \(\beta_{age} = 0.20\), \(SE_{age} = 0.05\)

Then: \(OR = \exp(0.20) = 1.22\)

Interpretation — Numerical Covariate

For a one-year increase in age, the estimated odds ratio of being in a higher (vs lower) outcome category is exp(β̂) = 1.22 times the estimated odds at the previous age value, controlling for other covariates in the model.

For a 10-year increase in age: exp(10 × β̂) = exp(0.20 × 10) = 7.38 → the estimated odds ratio of a higher outcome category is 7.38 (c = 10 units), controlling for other covariates in the model.

Note: The interpretation for intercept is not important when interpreting odds ratios.

Interpretation: Categorical Covariate

Example 1 — Binary Categorical Covariate

Outcome: low, median, high (\(g={0,1,2}\)). Covariate: sex coded male=1, female=0 (female is baseline). Estimated: \(\beta_{sex} = -0.285\)

\(\widehat{OR} = \exp(-0.285) = 0.752\)

Interpretation — Categorical Covariate

The odds of being in a higher (vs lower) category of outcome are 24.8% less for men than for women.

Example 2: Tumour Grade (from Kleinbaum, page 477)

Results produced by SAS. Equivalent model and alternative formulation differ — see page 469 Kleinbaum and Eq 8.25 (page 300) in Hosmer.

Data:

  • Outcome: Grade (D): 0=well differentiated, 1=moderately differentiated, 2=poorly differentiated
  • \(X_1 = RACE\): 0=white, 1=black
  • \(X_2 = ESTROGEN\): 0=never use, 1=ever use
VariableEstimateS.E.Symbol
Intercept1-1.2740.229\(\alpha_2\)
Intercept20.5110.215\(\alpha_1\)
RACE0.4270.272\(\beta_1\)
ESTROGEN-0.7760.249\(\beta_2\)

Odds that tumor grade is ≥2 (poorly differentiated) vs <2:

\[\text{odds} = \frac{P(D \geq 2 | X)}{P(D < 2 | X)} = \exp(\alpha_2 + \beta_1 X_1 + \beta_2 X_2)\]

Odds that tumor grade is ≥1 (moderately or poorly) vs <1:

\[\text{odds} = \frac{P(D \geq 1 | X)}{P(D < 1 | X)} = \exp(\alpha_1 + \beta_1 X_1 + \beta_2 X_2)\]

Estimated OR for RACE (controlling for ESTROGEN):

\[\widehat{OR} = \exp(\hat\beta_1) = \exp(0.4270) = 1.53\] \[95\% \; CI: \exp[0.427 \pm 1.96(0.272)] = (0.90, 2.61)\]

Example 3: LWT (Mother's Weight) Effect on Birth Weight

From Applied Logistic Regression Eq 8.25. The negative sign in Hosmer reflects that under a positive association, the covariate is protective (negatively associated with smaller values of ordinal outcome).

Interpretation — LWT Example
  • Estimate of the effect of a 10 pound increase in LWT on odds for "as light or lighter" baby: \(\widehat{OR} = \exp(-0.013 \times 10) = 0.88\) → 12% reduction in the odds for a lower weight baby per 10 pound increase in weight.
  • If we are interested in modeling heavier vs lighter babies: \(\widehat{OR} = \exp(0.013 \times 10) = 1.14\) → 14% increase in odds of a heavier baby per 10 pound increase in weight.

Key Feature: One feature of the proportional odds model identical to the binary logistic model is that we can reverse the direction of the model by simply changing the signs of the coefficients.

Confidence Intervals

95% CI for betas:

\[\hat\beta \pm 1.96 \times \widehat{SE}_{beta}\]

95% CI for OR (exponentiate the CI for beta):

\[95\% \; \widehat{OR} = \exp(0.20 \pm 1.96 \times 0.05)\]

Interpretation from Student Practical (ordinal.html — Graduate School)

Full Model Interpretation — Graduate School Dataset

Parental education (pared1): The odds of being in a higher category (vs lower) of decision to apply to graduate school are approximately 2.85 (95% CI: 1.70, 4.82, p-value <0.001) times higher for students whose parents have a degree compared to those whose parents do not, controlling for type of undergraduate institution and GPA.

Public institution (public1): The estimated odds of a higher application likelihood category for students from a public undergraduate institution is Ψ̂ = 0.94 (95% CI: 0.52–1.68, p = 0.843) times the estimated odds for students from a private institution, controlling for parental education and GPA in the model. This was not statistically significant.

GPA: One year increase in GPA, the odds to be more likely to apply for graduate school becomes 1.85 (95% CI: 1.11, 3.10, p-value = 0.018) higher controlling for parental degree and type of undergraduate institution.

Sign Interpretation Rule
  • Positive sign → higher vs lower → increases odds of being in a higher category
  • Negative sign → lower vs higher → reduces odds of being in a higher category

Example: \(\exp(1.04766) = 2.85\) → having educated parent increases odds for higher categories (more likely to apply for grad school) by 2.85

\(\exp(-1.04766) = 0.35\) → having educated parent reduces odds for lower categories (less likely to apply for grad school) by 65%

Model building is similar to other logistic regression approaches. Always start with exploratory data analysis.

  1. Start with exploratory data analysis (EDA)
  2. Run bivariate analysis among predictors and with outcome
  3. Fit univariable models first (simple models) — select variables with p < 0.25
  4. Fit multivariable model — use LRT and Wald test to add/remove variables
  5. After model building, assess goodness of fit
  6. Check the proportional odds assumption
For Proportional Odds Model

Check the proportionality odds assumption. For adjacent-category (non-proportional) and continuation-ratio models, one may assess fit via separate binary logistic regressions.

Tests include: Brant's test, approximate likelihood ratio, Fagerland-Hosmer test, and Lipsitz test.

Methods to Check Model Fitness

  • Pearson \(\chi^2\): used to perform the Pearson test statistic
  • Deviance \(G^2\): used to perform the likelihood ratio test
  • Compare observed and predicted frequencies
  • Casewise diagnostics is problematic — the next best thing is to perform separate logistic regressions and perform model diagnostics afterwards

Likelihood Ratio Test for Model Comparison

LRT for ordinal models
# Compare two clm models
o1b <- clm(rating ~ temp, data = dat.o)
o1 <- clm(rating ~ temp + contact, data = dat.o)

# LRT: 'test' argument is ignored in anova.clm
anova(o1b, o1, test = 'Chisq')

# Output:
# Likelihood ratio tests of cumulative link models:
#     no.par    AIC  logLik LR.stat df Pr(>Chisq)
# o1b      5 194.03 -92.013
# o1       6 184.98 -86.492  11.043  1  0.0008902 ***

# For graduate school example (ordinal.html):
ord1 <- clm(apply ~ pared + public + gpa, data = college)
ord2 <- clm(apply ~ public, data = college)
anova(ord1, ord2, test = 'Chisq')
# LRT = 23.766, df=2, p-value = 6.906e-06 ***
# More complex model (ord1) is significantly better
Stata
* Fit models and compare
ologit rating temp
estimates store m_reduced
ologit rating temp contact
estimates store m_full
lrtest m_reduced m_full
SPSS
* Not covered in lecture notes for this step

Methods to Test Proportional Odds

  • Brant's test — formal test of parallel regression assumption
  • nominal_test() in the ordinal package
  • Approximate likelihood ratio test
  • Fagerland-Hosmer test
  • Lipsitz test
H0 for Proportional Odds Tests

H0: Parallel Regression Assumption holds (i.e., proportional odds assumption is satisfied)

If p-value > 0.05 → fail to reject H0 → proportional odds assumption is plausible

If p-value < 0.05 → reject H0 → proportional odds assumption may be violated → consider multinomial logistic regression

Testing proportional odds assumption
# Method 1: nominal_test() from ordinal package
nominal_test(o1)

# Output (wine dataset):
# Tests of nominal effects
# formula: rating ~ temp + contact
#         Df  logLik    AIC    LRT Pr(>Chi)
# <none>     -86.492 184.98                
# temp     3 -84.904 187.81 3.1750   0.3654  (not significant → PO holds)
# contact  3 -86.209 190.42 0.5667   0.9040  (not significant → PO holds)

# Fit model with non-proportional effect for contact
o1.nominal <- clm(rating ~ temp, nominal = ~contact, data = wine)

# Method 2: Brant test (requires MASS::polr)
library(brant)
polr_cr3 <- MASS::polr(bwt4 ~ lwt, data = data.o3, Hess = TRUE)
brant(polr_cr3)

# Output:
# -------------------------------------------- 
# Test for X2  df  probability 
# -------------------------------------------- 
# Omnibus      1.13    2   0.57  (not significant)
# lwt          1.13    2   0.57  (not significant)
# H0: Parallel Regression Assumption holds

# Method 3: LRT comparing constrained vs unconstrained (ordinal package)
# For graduate school dataset:
nominal_test(ord1)
# Tests of nominal effects
# formula: apply ~ pared + public + gpa
#        Df  logLik    AIC    LRT Pr(>Chi)  
# <none>    -358.51 727.02                  
# pared   1 -358.50 729.00 0.0247  0.87510  
# public  1 -356.57 725.14 3.8833  0.04877 *  ← borderline!
# gpa     1 -358.11 728.22 0.8016  0.37061  

# Alternative: compare with nominal gpa model
ord.gpa <- clm(apply ~ pared + public, nominal = ~gpa, data = college)
anova(ord1, ord.gpa)
# LRT = 0.8016, df=1, p=0.3706 → PO assumption holds for gpa
Stata — test of parallel lines
* After ologit, test proportional odds assumption
ologit rating temp contact
* Approximate likelihood ratio test of proportionality of odds
brant

* Or use omodel package
omodel logit rating temp contact
* Brant test
brant, detail
SPSS
* In SPSS, the test of parallel lines is available
* in Analyze > Ordinal Regression
PLUM rating WITH temp contact
  /CRITERIA=CIN(95) DELTA(0) LCONVERGE(0) MXITER(100) MXSTEP(5) PCONVERGE(1.0E-6) SINGULAR(1.0E-8)
  /LINK=LOGIT
  /PRINT=FIT PARAMETER SUMMARY TPARALLEL.
Interpreting the Test Result

public variable (p=0.04877): This is borderline significant, suggesting the proportional odds assumption may not hold perfectly for the public variable. The p-value is just below 0.05.

Approach: For gpa (p=0.3706) → proportional odds assumption cannot be rejected → gpa satisfies proportional odds assumption.

R Packages for Ordinal Logistic Regression

ordinal::clm()
Main package. Cumulative Link Models. Provides z-values, p-values, AIC, logLik. Supports multiple link functions. Use for both estimation and PO testing.
MASS::polr()
Standard CLMs. 5 standard link functions. Use with brant package for PO testing. Results equivalent to clm().
VGAM::vglm(cumulative)
Also implements CLMs via vglm() function using cumulative link.
rms::lrm / orm
Also implements CLMs but without scale effects or partial/structured thresholds.
brant
Brant test for the parallel regression assumption (requires polr object).
broom::tidy()
Tidy output for clm() — easy to extract and exponentiate for OR.

Practical 1: Wine Dataset (wine.dta)

Wine Dataset — Description

From a factorial experiment on factors determining the bitterness of wine. Two treatment factors (temperature and contact) each have two levels. 9 judges assessed wine from 2 bottles from each of 4 treatment conditions → 72 observations.

  • response: scorings of wine bitterness on 0–100 continuous scale
  • rating: ordered factor with 5 levels (grouped version of response) — 1=least bitter, 5=most bitter
  • temp: temperature (cold/warm)
  • contact: contact between juice and skins (no/yes)
  • bottle: factor with 8 levels
  • judge: factor with 9 levels

Step 1: Load Libraries and Read Data

Load libraries and wine data
library(ordinal)  # for clm() and wine dataset

library(broom)     # to tidy results
library(gtsummary) # summary tables

# Read wine data from Stata file
dat.o <- read_dta('wine.dta')
glimpse(dat.o)
summary(dat.o)

# Convert labelled variables to factors
dat.o <- 
  dat.o %>%
  mutate(across(where(is.labelled), ~as_factor(.x)))

Step 3: Estimation using ordinal::clm()

# Model: logit P(Y_i <= j) = θ_j - β1(TEMP) - β2(CONTACT)

# Tidy output (log odds)

# Tidy output (odds ratios)
tidy(o1, exponentiate = TRUE)

Interpretation — Wine Dataset
  • tempwarm (OR = 12.2, 95% CI: 4.53, 36.4, p < 0.001): Warm temperature associates with higher categories of outcome (more bitter wine). With warm temperature, the odds ratio of bitterness being rated in category j or above is 12.2, controlling for CONTACT.
  • contactyes (OR = 4.61, 95% CI: 1.85, 12.1, p = 0.00135): The estimated odds of wine bitterness being rated in category j or above for contact = yes is Ψ̂ = 4.61 (95% CI: 1.85–12.1, p = 0.001) times the estimated odds for contact = no, controlling for TEMP in the model.

Practical 2: lowbwt.dta — Birth Weight Dataset

lowbwt.dta Dataset

Outcome: bwt4 (birth weight in 4 categories: ≤2500g, 2501–3000g, 3001–3500g, >3500g). Predictor: lwt (mother's weight in pounds).

lowbwt analysis — lwt predictor
# Read lowbwt data
data.o3 <- read_dta('lowbwt.dta')

# Create ordered 4-category birth weight variable
# Categories: =<2500, 2501-3000, 3001-3500, >3500
# (see practical for cut() function details)

# Fit model: bwt4 ~ lwt
o.lwt <- clm(bwt4 ~ lwt, data = data.o3)
summary(o.lwt)

# Output:
# Coefficients:
#     Estimate Std. Error z value Pr(>|z|)
# lwt 0.012737   0.004317   2.951  0.00317 **
#
# Threshold coefficients:
#                     Estimate Std. Error z value
# =<2500|2501-3000      0.8316     0.5686   1.462
# 2501-3000|3001-3500   1.7070     0.5782   2.952
# 3001-3500|>3500       2.8311     0.6027   4.697

# Log odds and OR with CI
tidy(o.lwt, conf.int = TRUE)
tidy(o.lwt, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)
# OR = 1.01 (95% CI: 1.00, 1.02)

# Manual interpretation calculations
exp(1 * -0.013)   # 0.987 → 1.3% reduction per 1 pound increase LWT
exp(10 * -0.0127)  # 0.881 → 12% reduction per 10 pound increase LWT
exp(1 * 0.013)    # 1.013 → 1.3% increase in odds of HEAVIER baby
exp(10 * 0.0127)   # 1.135 → 14% increase per 10 pound increase LWT
Stata: lowbwt
use lowbwt.dta, clear
* See practical_ordinal_log_2022 for Stata bwt4 variable creation
ologit bwt4 lwt
ologit bwt4 lwt, or
SPSS
* Not covered in lecture notes for this step
Interpretation — lowbwt lwt predictor

The coefficient \(\hat\beta_{lwt} = 0.0127\). This shows that the heavier the mother's weight (lwt), then the birth weight (bwt) also tends to increase.

  • For every 1 pound increase in lwt, the odds to get a LIGHTER baby reduced by about 2% [\(\exp(1 \times -0.013) = 0.987\)]
  • For every 10 pound increase in lwt, the odds to get a LIGHTER baby reduced by about 12% [\(\exp(10 \times -0.013) = 0.88\)]
  • Alternatively, for every 1 pound increase in lwt, the odds to get a HEAVIER baby is 1.01 or increased by 1%
  • For every 10 pound increase in lwt, the odds to get a HEAVIER baby is 1.14 or increased by 14%

Using polr() with Brant Test (lowbwt)

polr() + Brant test
polr_cr3 <- MASS::polr(bwt4 ~ lwt, data = data.o3, Hess = TRUE)
summary(polr_cr3)

# Brant test for proportional odds
library(brant)
brant(polr_cr3)
# -------------------------------------------- 
# Test for X2  df  probability 
# -------------------------------------------- 
# Omnibus      1.13    2   0.57  (p > 0.05 → PO holds)
# lwt          1.13    2   0.57  (p > 0.05 → PO holds)
# H0: Parallel Regression Assumption holds

Practical 3: warmth Dataset (ordwarm2b.dta)

warmth Dataset — Opinions on Women's Roles

Source: Chapter 5, Regression Models for Dependent Categorical Variables Using Stata

  • warm: outcome — SD (Strongly Disagree), D, A, SA (Strongly Agree)
  • yr89: 1977 or 1989
  • male: Men or Women
  • white: White or NotWhite
  • age, ed, prst: continuous variables

Model: \(Pr(warm = m | x_i) = F(\tau_m - x\beta) - F(\tau_{m-1} - x\beta)\)

warmth dataset — full multivariable model
warm <- 
  read_dta('ordwarm2b.dta') %>%
  mutate(across(where(is.labelled), ~as_factor(.x)))

# Using MASS::polr

                  ed + prst, data = warm, Hess = TRUE)
summary(polr_warm)

# Using ordinal::clm
library(ordinal)
clm_warm <- clm(warm ~ yr89 + male + white + age +
                  ed + prst, data = warm)
summary(clm_warm)

# Predict using polr
pred_grp <- predict(polr_warm)
pred_prob <- predict(polr_warm, type = 'probs')

# Predict using clm (must use newdata without outcome)
new_data_warm <- warm %>% select(-warm)
pred_prob_clm <- predict(clm_warm, new_data_warm)

newData = data.frame(yr89 = '1977',

Practical 4: Graduate School Application Dataset (ordinal.html)

Dataset — ologit.dta (n=400)
  • apply: outcome — unlikely (0), somewhat likely (1), very likely (2)
  • pared: 0/1 whether at least one parent has graduate degree
  • public: 0/1 public (1) or private (0) undergraduate institution
  • gpa: student's grade point average (continuous)
Graduate school application — full analysis
# Load data
college <- read_dta("https://stats.idre.ucla.edu/stat/data/ologit.dta")

# Convert labelled to factors
college <- college %>%
  mutate(across(where(~is.labelled(.x)), as_factor)) %>%
  mutate(pared = as.factor(pared), public = as.factor(public))

# EDA
hist(college$gpa)
ggplot(college, aes(x = apply, y = gpa)) +
  geom_boxplot(size = .5) + geom_jitter(alpha = .5) +
  facet_grid(pared ~ public, margins = TRUE) +
  theme(axis.text.x = element_text(angle = 45, hjust = 1))

# Descriptive summary
college %>% tbl_summary(by = apply,
              statistic = list(all_continuous() ~ "{mean} ({sd})"))

# Fit proportional odds model
ord1 <- clm(apply ~ pared + public + gpa, data = college)
summary(ord1)

# Log odds
tidy(ord1)
tidy(ord1, conf.int = TRUE)

# Odds ratios
tidy(ord1, exponentiate = TRUE)
tidy(ord1, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)

# Combined table of LO and OR
lo_ord1 <- tidy(ord1, conf.int = TRUE) %>% select(term, estimate, conf.low, conf.high)
odd_ord1 <- tidy(ord1, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE) %>%
  select(estimate, conf.low, conf.high, p.value)
cbind(lo_ord1, odd_ord1)

# Simpler comparison model
ord2 <- clm(apply ~ public, data = college)

# LRT comparison
anova(ord1, ord2, test = 'Chisq')
# LRT = 23.766, df=2, p = 6.906e-06 *** → full model better

# Prediction
college1 <- college %>% select(-apply)
prob.ord1 <- predict(ord1, newdata = college1)
collegepp <- cbind(college, prob.ord1)
class.ord1 <- predict(ord1, type = 'class')

# Test proportional odds
nominal_test(ord1)
ord.gpa <- clm(apply ~ pared + public, nominal = ~gpa, data = college)
anova(ord1, ord.gpa)

Full Stata ordinal logistic workflow
* ============================================
* Stata Ordinal Logistic Regression
* ============================================

* Basic ologit
ologit apply2 pared public

* With odds ratios
ologit apply2 pared public, or

* Prediction
predict probunlike probsomewhat probverylike, p

* Linear prediction (xb)
predict linpred, xb

* Brant test (requires omodel or brant package)
brant
brant, detail

* See file: stata_manual_13_handson.do

* STATA Logit formula (alternative formulation):
* ln[ P(Y<=g|X) / P(Y>g|X) ] = exp{-[αg - Σβi*Xi]}

* STATA Probability formula:
* P(Y<=g|X) = 1 / (1 + exp{-[αg - Σβj*Xj]})
* where αg satisfies: α1 < α2 < α3 ... < α(g-1)

* Model comparison
ologit apply2 pared
estimates store m_reduced
ologit apply2 pared public gpa
estimates store m_full
lrtest m_reduced m_full
SPSS Full Ordinal Workflow
* Ordinal regression in SPSS
* Analyze → Regression → Ordinal
PLUM apply WITH pared public gpa
  /CRITERIA=CIN(95) DELTA(0) LCONVERGE(0) MXITER(100) MXSTEP(5) 
    PCONVERGE(1.0E-6) SINGULAR(1.0E-8)
  /LINK=LOGIT
  /PRINT=FIT PARAMETER SUMMARY TPARALLEL.

* Note: SPSS uses the alternative formulation
* P(Y<=g|X) = 1 / (1 + exp{-(αg - Σβj*Xj)})
* Negative coefficients indicate HIGHER category

Software Formulation Comparison (Critical!)

SoftwareLogit FormulaProbability FormulaIntercept Direction
SAS (PROC Logistic)\(\ln\frac{P(Y \geq g|X)}{P(Y < g|X)} = \alpha_g + \sum\beta_i X_i\)\(P(Y \geq g|X) = \frac{1}{1+\exp[-(\alpha_g + \sum\beta_j X_j)]}\)\(\alpha_1 > \alpha_2 > ...\)
Stata (ologit)\(\ln\frac{P(Y \leq g|X)}{P(Y > g|X)} = \exp{-[\alpha_g - \sum\beta_i X_i]}\)\(P(Y \leq g|X) = \frac{1}{1+\exp[-(\alpha_g - \sum\beta_j X_j)]}\)\(\alpha_1 < \alpha_2 < ...\)
R (clm/polr)\(\text{logit}(P(Y \leq g)) = \beta_{0g} - \sum\beta_j X_j\)\(\hat{P}(Y \leq g) = \frac{e^{\hat{\beta}_{0g} - \sum\hat{\beta}_j X_j}}{1 + e^{\hat{\beta}_{0g} - \sum\hat{\beta}_j X_j}}\)\(\alpha_1 < \alpha_2 < ...\)

Manual Probability Calculation (ordwarm2b example)

Manual probability calculation
# Manual calculation for full_auto dataset (STATA replication)
# Model: rep77 ~ foreign, thresholds: Poor|Fair=-2.764, Fair|Average=-0.996
#        Average|Good=0.942, Good|Excellent=3.12
#        foreign coefficient = 1.456

# Case 1: domestic car (foreign=0)
case1_poor <- 1/(1 + exp(-(-2.764 - (1.456*0))))
case1_poor   # 0.0593

case1_fair <- 1/(1 + exp(-(-0.996 - (1.456*0)))) - case1_poor
case1_fair   # 0.2104

case1_average <- 1/(1 + exp(-(0.942 - (1.456*0)))) - case1_fair - case1_poor
case1_average  # 0.4498

case1_good <- 1/(1 + exp(-(3.12 - (1.456*0)))) - case1_average - case1_fair - case1_poor
case1_good     # 0.2382

# Graduate school dataset - manual probability for 1st observation
# Thresholds: unlikely|somewhat = 2.2033, somewhat|very = 4.2988
# pared=0, public=0, gpa=3.26; linear predictor = 2.2033 - (1.048*0 + (-0.059)*0 + 0.616*3.26)
# = 2.2033 - 2.008 = 0.1959919
exp(0.1959919) / (1 + exp(0.1959919))  # P(unlikely) = 0.5488417
exp(-0.1959919) / (1 + exp(-0.1959919))  # P(somewhat+very) = 0.4511583
1/(1 + exp(-2.291436))  # P(unlikely+somewhat) = 0.9081653
0.5488417 + 0.3593236    # = 0.9081653 ✓
Stata manual
* Manual calculation in Stata
* After ologit, coefficients stored in e(b)
scalar cut1 = _b[cut1:_cons]
scalar cut2 = _b[cut2:_cons]
scalar b_pared = _b[pared]
display 1/(1 + exp(-(cut1 - b_pared*1)))
SPSS
* Not covered in lecture notes for this step
Blood Pressure Dataset (Student Practical)

Health and lifestyle information for 200 individuals. Key outcome: blood pressure (bp). Variables: id, age, height, weight, cholesterol, smoke, exercise. The study investigates the relationship between blood pressure and factors such as age, cholesterol levels, smoking status, and exercise frequency.

bp4 categories: ≤120, 121–140, 141–160, >160 mmHg. Mean values: ≤120=106.2, 121–140=132.3, 141–160=150.2, >160=170.3.

Three Models Compared

1. Adjacent-Category (Baseline Logit)
Replicates unconstrained baseline logit model. Uses vglm with multinomial family. Gets RRR. Note: constrained AC models could not be reproduced.
2. Continuation-Ratio
Creates 3 comparisons: >160 vs 141-160; then {>160,141-160} vs 121-140; then {>160,141-160,121-140} vs ≤120.
3. Cumulative Logit (Proportional Odds)
Main model. Uses ordinal::clm. One β per predictor. Provides cumulative odds ratios. Uses ordinal::clm() with bp4 ordered factor.
Interpretation — Blood Pressure Age Effect (Cumulative Logit)

For every 1-year increase in age, the odds of being in a lower blood pressure category is 1.01 (increased by 1%)

For every 10-year increase in age, the odds of being in a lower blood pressure category is 1.14 (increased by 14%)

Interpretation using the reverse direction:

  • \(\exp(1 \times 0.013) = 1.013\) → 1 year increase → 1.3% increase in odds of being in LOWER bp category
  • \(\exp(10 \times 0.0135) = 1.145\) → 10 year increase → 14.5% increase in odds of LOWER bp category

Interpretation — Adjacent-Category (Exercise Variable)

Adjacent-Category Interpretation
  • Comparison of ">160" vs. "≤120": When exercise increases, the log odds of having BP ">160" compared to "≤120" changes by 0.02 (95% CI: -0.23, 0.27, p=0.894). Exercise does not significantly affect the odds. Wide CI indicates high uncertainty.
  • Comparison of "141–160" vs. "≤120": Exercise changes log odds by -0.06 (95% CI: -0.35, 0.23, p=0.705). Not significant. Wide CI — could range from reduction to increase in risk.
  • Comparison of "121–140" vs. "≤120": Exercise changes log odds by 0.04 (95% CI: -0.25, 0.32, p=0.803). Not significant.

Plot Interpretation — Blood Pressure Predicted Probabilities

Plot Interpretation
  • Age Effect: Across all panels, the probability of having lower BP (≤120) decreases with age, while the probability of higher BP (>160) increases with age.
  • Smoking Effect: Smokers (smoke=1) generally have lower probabilities of lower BP levels (≤120) and higher probabilities of higher BP levels (>160) compared to non-smokers (smoke=0).
  • Exercise Effect: Higher exercise levels tend to be associated with higher probabilities of lower BP and lower probabilities of higher BP.

Quick Reference — Ordinal Logistic Regression

When to useOrdered categorical outcome with 3+ levels
Most popular modelProportional Odds Model (cumulative logit)
Key assumptionProportional odds assumption — same OR for all cutpoints
Effect measureOR = exp(β). Positive β → higher odds of HIGHER category
ThresholdsG−1 intercepts (cutpoints) for G outcome categories
PO test (R)nominal_test(clm_obj) or brant(polr_obj)
PO test (Stata)brant (after ologit)
PO test (SPSS)Test of Parallel Lines in PLUM output
Model comparisonanova(model1, model2) or LRT
R packagesordinal::clm() [preferred], MASS::polr()
Stata commandologit outcome predictors [, or]
Sign interpretationPositive β → more likely in HIGHER category; Negative β → less likely
Key referencesHosmer & Lemeshow 2013 (Ch.8.2); Kleinbaum (Ch.13)
clm() vs polr() vs vglm(cumulative)
  • ordinal::clm() — Most feature-rich; provides z-values, p-values, supports multiple links; best for testing proportional odds with nominal_test()
  • MASS::polr() — Standard; required for brant() test; similar results to clm()
  • VGAM::vglm(cumulative) — Also implements CLMs via vglm(); less commonly used
  • Results from clm() and polr() should be essentially identical
Topic 03 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Conditional Logistic Regression

Analysis of Matched Case-Control Studies · Kamarul Imran Musa · USM 2022

Learning Objectives

  1. Understand the logistic model for matched case-control studies
  2. Perform cross-classifying tables in a matched case-control study
  3. Develop logistic models from univariable to multivariable using matched data
  4. Perform model assessment using matched case-control data
  5. Implement conditional logistic regression using clogit() in R

Motivation

In some studies, subjects are stratified on the basis of variables believed to be associated with the outcome. Age and gender are examples of commonly used stratification (matching) variables. Within each stratum, samples of cases (\(y=1\)) and controls (\(y=0\)) are chosen. The number of cases and controls need not be constant across strata, but the most common matched designs include one case and between 1 to 5 controls per stratum, referred to as 1-M matched studies.

Why Standard Logistic Regression Fails

In the 1–1 matched design with \(n\) case-control pairs we have only two subjects per stratum. In a fully stratified analysis with \(p\) covariates, we would be required to estimate \(n + p\) parameters — the constant term, the \(p\) slope coefficients, and the \(n + 1\) coefficients for the stratum-specific design variables — using a sample of size \(2n\). This is the incidental parameters problem, leading to biased estimates. Conditional logistic regression solves this by conditioning out the stratum-specific intercepts.

What is Matched Case-Control Analysis?

To analyze case-control data with pairwise matching we use conditional logistic regression. Cases are individually matched to 1 (or up to 4) control subjects based on matching criteria. These matching criteria represent the confounders to be controlled. Each case and matched control(s) are analyzed as separate strata. For example, if there are 150 cases, then there are 150 strata that need to be fitted.

The Matched Pairs Model

With matched pairs data the form of the logistic model involves the probability, \(\phi\), that in matched pair number \(i\), for a given value of the explanatory variable, the member of the pair is a case:

\[ ext{logit}(\phi_i) = lpha_i + \beta x\]

The odds that a subject with \(x = 1\) is a case equals \(\exp(\beta)\) times the odds that a subject with \(x = 0\) is a case.

Software Note

Some software does not provide a specific interface for conditional logistic regression and instead uses the proportional hazards regression function. In R, clogit() from the survival package accomplishes this. In Stata, the clogit command fits conditional (fixed-effects) logistic regression. In economics, this model is known as the fixed-effect logit model for panel data.

Core Assumptions
  1. Matched design — Data arise from a matched case-control study with well-defined strata (matched sets)
  2. Binary outcome within strata — Within each stratum there is at least one case and at least one control
  3. Independence between strata — Matched sets are independent of one another
  4. Linearity in the logit — Continuous covariates have a linear relationship with the log-odds (check with fractional polynomials)
  5. No perfect separation — No covariate or combination of covariates perfectly predicts case/control status
  6. Adequate sample size — At least 10 discordant pairs per covariate in the model is a commonly used rule of thumb
Discordant Pairs

Concordant pairs (where both case and control have the same value of a covariate) contribute no information to the conditional likelihood — they cancel out. Only discordant pairs (where case and control differ) contribute. This is why it is a good practice to form the \(2 imes 2\) cross-classification table of case versus control for all dichotomous covariates to determine the number of discordant pairs.

Conditional Likelihood for a Single Stratum

The most frequently used matched design is the one case to one control (1:1 matched study). Let \(x_{1k}\) denote the data vector for the case and \(x_{0k}\) for the control. The conditional likelihood for the \(k\)th stratum is:

\[l_k(\beta) = \frac{e^{\beta x_{1k}}}{e^{\beta x_{1k}} + e^{\beta x_{0k}}}\]

This can be simplified to:

\[l_k(\beta) = \frac{e^{\beta (x_{1k} - x_{0k})}}{1 + e^{\beta (x_{1k} - x_{0k})}}\]
Worked Numerical Example

Suppose we have a model with a single dichotomous covariate and \(\beta = 0.8\), and the observed data are \(x_{1k} = 1\) (case has the exposure) and \(x_{0k} = 0\) (control does not). Then:

\[l_k(0.8) = \frac{e^{0.8 imes 1}}{e^{0.8 imes 1} + e^{0.8 imes 0}} = \frac{e^{0.8}}{e^{0.8} + 1} = 0.690\]

This means the probability is 0.69 that a subject with \(x = 1\) is the case compared to a subject with \(x = 0\).

Concordant Pairs are Uninformative

When the data for case and control are identical (concordant pair), \(x_{1k} = x_{0k}\), so \(l_k(\beta) = 0.5\) for any value of \(\beta\). The case-control pairs with the same value for any covariate are uninformative and contribute nothing to parameter estimation — they are automatically conditioned out of the likelihood.

The Full Conditional Likelihood

The overall conditional likelihood is the product across all \(K\) strata:

\[L(\beta) = \prod_{k=1}^{K} l_k(\beta) = \prod_{k=1}^{K} \frac{e^{\beta x_{1k}}}{e^{\beta x_{1k}} + e^{\beta x_{0k}}}\]

Maximum likelihood estimation of \(\beta\) maximises \(\log L(\beta)\). The strata-specific intercepts \(lpha_k\) do not appear in the conditional likelihood — they have been conditioned away.

What is Matching?

Matching is a design strategy where each case is paired with one or more controls who share the same values on selected confounding variables (the matching variables). This is done to control for confounding in the design phase rather than the analysis phase.

Design TypeStrata StructureCommon Use
1:1 matched1 case + 1 control per stratumMost common; maximises statistical efficiency per case
1:M matched (M=2–5)1 case + M controls per stratumIncreases power; diminishing returns beyond M=4
Variable matchingVarying number of controls per caseWhen controls are scarce for some cases
Matching Variables vs. Study Variables

Matching variables (e.g., age group, sex, hospital) are used to define strata and are NOT included as predictors in the conditional logistic model — they are controlled for through the stratification itself. Study covariates of interest (potential risk factors) ARE included as predictors.

The 2×2 Cross-Classification Table for Discordant Pairs

For a 1:1 matched study with a dichotomous exposure \(E\), construct:

Control E=1Control E=0
Case E=1Both exposed (concordant) — uninformativeCase exposed, control not — discordant
Case E=0Control exposed, case not — discordantNeither exposed (concordant) — uninformative

Only the discordant cells contribute to the estimate of the odds ratio.

Odds Ratios from Conditional Logistic Regression

The exponentiated coefficient \(e^\beta\) from conditional logistic regression is interpreted as a matched odds ratio — the odds that the case (vs. control) has the exposure, controlled for all other covariates in the model.

Interpretation Rules
  • Binary covariate: \( ext{OR} = e^\beta\) is the odds of being a case for exposure = 1 vs. exposure = 0, controlling for other covariates and matching variables
  • Continuous covariate: \( ext{OR} = e^\beta\) is the OR per 1-unit increase. For \(c\)-unit increase: \( ext{OR} = e^{c\beta}\)
  • OR > 1: the exposure increases the odds of being a case
  • OR < 1: the exposure decreases the odds of being a case (protective)
Example Interpretation (Hosmer & Lemeshow 2013 — Fracture Study)

In the table reported by Hosmer & Lemeshow, for weight (OR for 5 kg increase) and BMI (OR for 5 kg/m² increase), the outcome is fracture vs. no fracture:

  • History of prior fracture: OR > 2, 95% CI: 1.2 to 4-fold increase in odds of fracture
  • Mother's history of fracture: OR could be non-significant; up to 4.6-fold increase or 7% decrease in odds
  • Weight (per 5 kg increase): estimated 38% decrease in odds; could be as small as 16% or as much as 54% decrease
  • BMI (per 5 kg/m² increase): 3-fold increase in odds; could be 1.4-fold to 6.7-fold increase with 95% confidence
Step-by-Step Model Building
  1. Fit univariable conditional logistic regression — one variable at a time; identify variables significant at \(p < 0.25\) or clinically important
  2. Perform variable selection — enter candidate variables; remove non-significant ones sequentially
  3. Check for confounders — use the 20% change rule: if removing a variable changes any remaining coefficient by >20%, retain it as a confounder regardless of its p-value
  4. Check for interaction — explore clinically plausible interactions; compare models with and without interaction using the likelihood ratio test
  5. Perform casewise diagnostic measures

Diagnostic Plots

  • Leverage: plot leverage against estimated probability (fitted values)
  • Lack of fit: plot delta chi-square against estimated probability (fitted values)
  • Influence: plot delta beta (Cook's distance) against estimated probability (fitted values)

The presence of a numerical covariate requires the use of fractional polynomials to check for its scale in the logit. Any important two-way interaction from the main-effect model must be explored and checked. Comparison between models with and without interaction is best done using the likelihood ratio test.

Fractional Polynomials for Linearity Check

Before including a continuous variable as a linear term, check whether its functional form is correctly specified. Fractional polynomials allow non-linear transformations of the form \(X^p\) where \(p\) is chosen from the set \(\{-2, -1, -0.5, 0, 0.5, 1, 2, 3\}\). Compare the model with fractional polynomial transformation against the linear model using LRT.

Key Function

In R, conditional logistic regression is implemented via clogit() from the survival package. Internally it uses the Cox proportional hazards function (coxph()) with the strata() term to define matched sets. The syntax mirrors standard logistic regression except for the strata() argument.

R · Libraries & Data Setup
# Load required libraries
library(tidyverse)
library(broom)       # tidy() for clean output
library(haven)       # read .dta Stata files
library(HSAUR2)      # contains backpain dataset
library(survival)    # clogit() is here
library(gtsummary)   # publication-ready tables

# Built-in example: backpain data (HSAUR2 package)
glimpse(backpain)
# Rows: 434
# Columns: 4
# $ ID       <fct> 1, 1, 2, 2, 3, 3, ...  (matched pair ID)
# $ status   <fct> case, control, ...     (outcome)
# $ driver   <fct> yes, yes, ...          (exposure: professional driver?)
# $ suburban <fct> yes, no, ...           (exposure: suburban residence?)

# Descriptive table
backpain %>%
  select(-ID) %>%
  tbl_summary(by = status)
R · Basic clogit — backpain example
# Fit conditional logistic regression
# Formula: outcome ~ covariates + strata(matched_set_ID)
backpain_glm <- clogit(
  I(status == 'case') ~ driver + suburban + strata(ID),
  data = backpain
)

# Tidy output (log-OR scale)
tidy(backpain_glm)
# term        estimate std.error statistic p.value
# driveryes      0.658     0.294      2.24  0.0252
# suburbanyes    0.255     0.226      1.13  0.258

# Odds ratios with 95% CI
tidy(backpain_glm, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)
# term        estimate conf.low conf.high p.value
# driveryes      1.930    1.086     3.430   0.025
# suburbanyes    1.291    0.827     2.016   0.258
R · Multivariable — MI study (dataclogit.csv)
# Read matched MI data
data_mi <- read_csv('dataclogit.csv')
# Variables: match (strata ID), person, mi (1=case, 0=control),
#            smk (smoking), sbp (systolic BP), ecg (ECG abnormality)

# Recode for clarity
data_mi <- data_mi %>%
  mutate(
    mi  = factor(mi,  levels = c(1,0), labels = c("Case","Control")),
    smk = factor(smk, levels = c(0,1), labels = c("Not current","Current")),
    ecg = factor(ecg, levels = c(0,1), labels = c("Normal","Abnormal"))
  )

# Descriptive table
data_mi %>%
  select(mi, smk, ecg) %>%
  tbl_summary(by = mi)

# Univariable: smoking only
mi.clog <- clogit(I(mi == 'Case') ~ smk + strata(match), data = data_mi)
summary(mi.clog)
tidy(mi.clog)
# smkCurrent: estimate = 0.843, OR = 2.32, p = 0.070

# Multivariable: smoking + ECG + SBP
mi.clog.mv <- clogit(I(mi == 'Case') ~ smk + ecg + sbp + strata(match),
                      data = data_mi)
tidy(mi.clog.mv, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)
R · Prediction & Diagnostics
# Augment — residuals and fitted values
low.bwt.2 %>%
  augment(low.bwt, type.predict = 'response')
# .fitted gives predicted probability of being a case

# Predicted probabilities (risk scores)
odds_low <- predict(low.bwt.2, type = "risk")
(odds_low / (odds_low + 1))[1:10]

# Note from clogit documentation:
# In a conditional logistic model, the "expected number of events"
# in a stratum is 1 (one case per stratum).
# Prediction is via the linear predictor / risk score only.

# Model comparison using LRT
anova(model1, model2, test = "Chisq")

Output from summary()

Reading clogit Summary Output

The summary() of a clogit object shows Cox-style output because clogit internally calls coxph():

## Call:
## coxph(formula = Surv(rep(1, 117L), I(mi == "Case")) ~ smk + strata(match),
##       method = "exact")
##   n= 117, number of events= 39
##
##              coef exp(coef) se(coef)     z Pr(>|z|)
## smkCurrent  0.8434    2.3242   0.4661 1.809   0.0704 .
##
##            exp(coef) exp(-coef) lower .95 upper .95
## smkCurrent     2.324     0.4303    0.9322     5.794
##
## Concordance= 0.577  (se = 0.058)
## Likelihood ratio test= 3.37  on 1 df,   p=0.07
## Wald test            = 3.27  on 1 df,   p=0.07
## Score (logrank) test = 3.43  on 1 df,   p=0.06
  • exp(coef) = odds ratio
  • lower .95 / upper .95 = 95% CI for OR
  • Three tests at the bottom (LRT, Wald, Score) — prefer LRT for inference
Stata Note

Based on the lecture notes: "the best interface to perform matched case-control data analysis using conditional logistic regression is from clogit function in STATA. clogit in software such as STATA fits a conditional logistic regression model for matched case-control data. It fits maximum likelihood models."

Stata · clogit — basic syntax
* Load data
use "dataclogit.dta", clear

* Describe matched structure
tab mi          // outcome: 1=case, 0=control
tab smk         // exposure: smoking

* Conditional logistic regression
* Syntax: clogit outcome predictors, group(stratum_id)
clogit mi smk, group(match)

* Display odds ratios
clogit mi smk, group(match) or

* Multivariable model
clogit mi smk ecg sbp, group(match) or

* LRT-based model comparison
estimates store model1
clogit mi smk ecg sbp age, group(match) or
estimates store model2
lrtest model1 model2
Stata · Multivariable + post-estimation
* Multivariable conditional logistic regression
clogit mi smk ecg sbp, group(match) or

* Salmonella outbreak example
use "sal_outbrk.dta", clear
clogit casecontrol slt_a, group(match_grp) or

* Low birthweight matched study
use "lowbwt.dta", clear
clogit low smoke lwt race ptd ht ui, group(pair) or

* Confidence intervals
clogit mi smk, group(match) or level(95)

* Test of overall model
clogit mi smk ecg, group(match)
estat gof    // goodness of fit (if available)

* Predict linear predictor (xb)
predict xb, xb

This dataset with a count outcome (number of attacks) is more naturally suited to Poisson regression (covered in Topic 4). In conditional logistic contexts, asthma studies typically involve matched case-control designs where cases (asthma patients with attacks) are matched to controls.

R · Salmonella outbreak
library(haven)
salmonella <- read_dta('sal_outbrk.dta')
salmonella <- salmonella %>%
  mutate(across(where(is.labelled), ~as_factor(.x)))

# Descriptive table
salmonella %>% select(-date) %>% tbl_summary(by = casecontrol)

# Conditional logistic — slt_a exposure
salmonella_clogit <- clogit(
  I(casecontrol == 'case') ~ slt_a + strata(match_grp),
  data = salmonella
)

# slt_ayes    1.49     0.518      2.87   0.00415
# OR = exp(1.49) ≈ 4.44 → Attending that salad bar is associated with
