Tutorial 4 — Low Birthweight Study (1:1 Matched)

Data from Hosmer & Lemeshow: 111 rows (55–56 matched pairs). Variables include pair (strata), low (birthweight <2500g: yes/no), lwt (mother's last weight), smoke, race, ptd (preterm delivery), ht (hypertension), ui (uterine irritability).

R · Low birthweight matched study
low.bwt <- read_table('lowbwt11.dat')
colnames(low.bwt) <- c('pair','low','age','lwt','race','smoke','ptd','ht','ui')

low.bwt <- low.bwt %>%
  mutate(
    low   = recode(low,   `0` = 'bwt>2500g', `1` = 'bwt=<2500g'),
    race  = recode(race,  `1` = 'white', `2` = 'black', `3` = 'other'),
    smoke = recode(smoke, `0` = 'no', `1` = 'yes'),
    ptd   = recode(ptd,   `0` = 'no', `1` = 'yes'),
    ht    = recode(ht,    `0` = 'no', `1` = 'yes'),
    ui    = recode(ui,    `0` = 'no', `1` = 'yes')
  )

# Univariable — mother's weight (continuous)
low.bwt.1 <- clogit(I(low == 'bwt=<2500g') ~ lwt + strata(pair),
                     data = low.bwt)
tidy(low.bwt.1)
# term  estimate  OR = 0.991 (95%CI 0.979–1.003), p = 0.151

# Univariable — smoking
low.bwt.2 <- clogit(I(low == 'bwt=<2500g') ~ smoke + strata(pair),
                     data = low.bwt)
tidy(low.bwt.2, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)
# smokeyes: OR = 2.62 (95%CI: 1.16–5.93), p = 0.020 *
# Smoking increases odds of low birthweight by 2.62x

Model Assessment — Diagnostics

Model assessment in conditional logistic regression uses an extension of regression diagnostics for the unconditional logistic regression model (Hosmer & Lemeshow 2013, Chapter 9):

  1. Generate Pearson residuals (page 249, Hosmer book) → calculate Pearson chi-square
  2. Generate standardized Pearson residuals (page 250) → standardized Pearson chi-square
  3. The square of the standardized residual = lack of fit diagnostic (delta chi-square)
  4. The influence diagnostic (delta beta) from the standardized residuals
Diagnostic Plot Interpretation

The most informative way to view diagnostic statistics is via a plot of each statistic versus the fitted values (estimated conditional probability that subject \(j\) within stratum \(k\) is a case). Additional diagnostics:

  • Leverage — distance from the weighted match set to the mean
  • Lack of fit — change in the Pearson chi-square (\(\Delta \chi^2\))
  • Influence — delta-beta (Cook's distance analogue)

The final decision to exclude or include cases (entire strata) or controls should be based on the clinical plausibility of the data.

FeatureUnconditional LogisticConditional Logistic
Study designUnmatched / independentMatched case-control
Strata interceptsEstimated (one per study if unmatched)Conditioned out of likelihood
Incidental parameters problemDoes not applySolved by conditioning
R functionglm(..., family=binomial)clogit(..., strata())
Stata commandlogisticclogit ... , group()
Concordant pairsContribute to estimationUninformative — excluded automatically
Bias with many strataBiased (incidental parameters)Unbiased
Effect measureOdds RatioMatched Odds Ratio
When Would You Use Unconditional LR Instead?

If matching is very loose (e.g., broad age groups) and the strata are large, unconditional logistic regression with the matching variables included as covariates may be acceptable. However, if matching was done to control important confounders and strata are small (especially 1:1 matching), always use conditional logistic regression to avoid bias.

Key Points — Conditional Logistic Regression
  1. Use conditional logistic regression when data arise from a matched case-control study
  2. Standard logistic regression on matched data is biased due to the incidental parameters problem
  3. The conditional likelihood conditions out the stratum-specific intercepts, leaving only the covariate effects
  4. Concordant pairs contribute no information; only discordant pairs matter
  5. In R, use clogit(outcome ~ covariates + strata(ID), data=...)
  6. Output is interpreted as matched odds ratios
  7. Model building follows the same purposeful selection strategy as unconditional logistic regression
  8. For continuous covariates, check linearity using fractional polynomials
  9. Diagnostic tools: leverage, delta chi-square (lack-of-fit), delta-beta (influence)
References
  • Kleinbaum, David. (2010). Logistic Regression: A Self-Learning Text. New York: Springer.
  • Lemeshow, Stanley & Hosmer, David W. (2013). Applied Logistic Regression, 3rd Edition. Wiley.
  • Kamarul Imran Musa, Wan Nor Arifin, Tengku Muhammad Hanis (2022). Data Analysis in Medicine and Health using R. USM.
Topic 04 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Count and Rate Data

Poisson Regression, Overdispersion, Negative Binomial & Zero-Inflated Models · USM 2022

Learning Objectives

  1. Understand the concept of count data and rate data, and the Poisson distribution
  2. Apply simple and multiple Poisson regression models
  3. Use the offset term to model rates with varying denominators
  4. Interpret incidence rate ratios (IRR)
  5. Detect and handle overdispersion using quasi-Poisson and negative binomial regression
  6. Understand zero-inflated Poisson (ZIP) and zero-inflated negative binomial (ZINB) models
  7. Implement all models in R using glm(), glm.nb(), and zeroinfl()

What is Count Data?

The number of times an event occurs is a common form of data — these are known as count data. The Poisson distribution is often used to model count data. Examples include:

  • Number of asthma attacks per month
  • Number of hospital admissions per ward per week
  • Number of new HIV cases per year
  • Number of awards earned by students
  • Number of road accident deaths per month
Count vs. Rate Data — Key Distinction
Count DataRate Data
DefinitionRaw count of events \(Y\)Count divided by an exposure denominator \(Y/t\)
DenominatorNot needed (all units have equal exposure)Required (person-time, population size, etc.)
Model termStandard Poisson GLMPoisson GLM with offset = \(\log(t)\)
InterpretationIRR per unit change in XIRR per unit change in X, controlled for exposure

Why Poisson Distribution?

When events occur over time, space, or some other index of size, models can focus on the rate at which events occur. For example, in analyzing numbers of murders in 2006 for a sample of cities, we could form a rate by dividing the number of murders by the city's population size. A model might describe how the rate depends on unemployment rate, median income, and educational level.

Rate Parameters Require Careful Definition

The parameter in count data requires careful definition. Examples:

  • Motor vehicle crashes per 1,000 population
  • Crashes per 1,000 licensed drivers
  • Crashes per 100,000 kilometres travelled
  • Occupational injuries per person-year at work

The time scale and units of exposure should always be stated explicitly.

The Poisson Distribution

A Poisson distribution (Fleiss et al., 2003) is defined as:

\[P(Y = y \mid \mu) = \frac{e^{-\mu} \mu^y}{y!} \quad ext{for non-negative integers } y = 0, 1, 2, \ldots, \mu > 0\]
  • \(Y\) is a Poisson random variable: \(Y \sim ext{Poisson}(\mu)\)
  • The parameter \(\mu\) is the mean of \(Y\)
  • Key property: \( ext{Mean}(Y) = ext{Var}(Y) = \mu\) — the mean equals the variance
Assumptions of the Poisson Process (Daniel, 1995)
  1. The occurrences of events are independent — the occurrence in one interval does not affect probability in another
  2. Infinite number of occurrences is theoretically possible in the interval
  3. The probability of a single occurrence in an interval is proportionate to the interval length
  4. In a very small portion of the interval, the probability of more than one occurrence is negligible
Example 2.1 — Probability Calculations (from Wan Nor Arifin, 2019)

Suppose the number of deaths due to motor vehicle accidents per day in Malaysia follows a Poisson distribution with mean \(\mu = 17.2\).

R · Poisson probability calculations
dpois(10, 17.2)        # P(Y = 10) = 0.02116686
ppois(10, 17.2)        # P(Y ≤ 10) = 0.04470966
1 - ppois(10, 17.2)    # P(Y > 10) = 0.9552903

# Plot the Poisson PMF
X <- 0:50
p.X <- dpois(X, 17.2)
plot(p.X, type = "l",
     xlab = "Number of deaths", ylab = "Probability",
     main = "Poisson(μ = 17.2)")

Simple Poisson Regression

Let \(Y\) be the Poisson count of some events, associated with factor \(X\). The mean \(E(Y|X) = \mu\) is linked with \(X\) by the log link:

\[\ln E(Y|X) = \ln \mu = \beta_0 + \beta_1 X\]

Equivalently:

\[E(Y|X) = e^{\beta_0 + \beta_1 X} = e^{\beta_0} e^{\beta_1 X}\]

Interpretation for Binary Exposure

For exposure \(X = 0, 1\):

Reference group (\(X=0\)): \(E(Y|X=0) = e^{\beta_0}\)

Exposed group (\(X=1\)): \(E(Y|X=1) = e^{\beta_0 + \beta_1}\)

Rate Ratio (RR):

\[ ext{RR} = \frac{E(Y|X=1)}{E(Y|X=0)} = \frac{e^{\beta_0} e^{\beta_1}}{e^{\beta_0}} = e^{\beta_1}\]

Multiple Poisson Regression

\[\ln \mu = \beta_0 + \beta_1 X_1 + \beta_2 X_2 + \cdots + \beta_{p-1} X_{p-1}\]

Rate Ratio for \(X_j\): \( ext{RR} = e^{\beta_j}\)

Interpretation: For 1 unit increase in \(X_j\), holding all other variables constant, the expected count (or rate) multiplies by a factor of \(e^{\beta_j}\).

Key Properties of Poisson GLM
  • Link function: natural log (\(\ln\))
  • Distribution: Poisson
  • Estimation: Maximum likelihood
  • Dispersion assumption: \( ext{Var}(Y) = \mu\) (mean = variance)
  • Categorical predictors: Automatically create dummy variables in R when specified as factor()

Why Use an Offset?

In medicine, it is more common to describe the count in terms of prevalence, incidence, or person-years — i.e., a rate. When the response count \(Y\) has index (denominator) equal to \(t\), the sample rate is \(Y/t\) and the expected rate is \(\mu/t\). A log-linear model for the expected rate has the form:

\[\log\frac{\mu}{t} = lpha + \beta X\]

Re-arranging:

\[\log \mu - \log t = lpha + \beta X\] \[\log \mu = lpha + \beta X + \log t\]

The term \(\log t\) is called the offset. It is a known constant added to the linear predictor with coefficient fixed at 1.

The Expected Count Given the Offset
\[\mu = t imes \exp(lpha + \beta X)\]

For the loglinear model, the expected number of outcomes equals the exposure (person-time) multiplied by the rate.

Example — Train Accident Rate (Agresti 2007)

Outcome: Annual number of collisions between trains and road vehicles, for \(t\) million km of train travel. Predictor: years since 1975 (\(x\)).

\[\log \hat{\mu} - \log t = -4.21 - 0.0329x \quad ext{SE}(x) = 0.011\]

Estimated rate: \(\exp(-4.21 - 0.0329x) = 0.0148 imes 0.968^x\)

  • In 1975 (\(x=0\)): rate = 0.0148 per million km
  • In 2003 (\(x=28\)): rate = 0.0059 per million km (≈ 6 per billion km)
Example — Depression Rate in Schools

Dataset: \(n=100\) classrooms. DEPRESSED = count of depressed students, STRESS = average stress score, POP = class size (offset).

\[\log\frac{ ext{DEPRESSED}}{ ext{POP}} = -2.39 + 0.0208 imes ext{STRESS}\]

Interpretation: For 1 unit increase in stress score, the incidence rate of depression increases by a factor of \(\exp(0.0208) = 1.021\) — i.e., the rate increases by 2.1%.

For STRESS = 100: log rate = \(-2.39 + 0.0208(100) = -0.31\), so rate = \(\exp(-0.31) = 0.733\) — 73.3% of students with stress = 100 are expected to be depressed.

Interpreting \(e^{\hat\beta}\) as IRR
  • Binary predictor: \( ext{IRR} = e^{\beta_1}\) = ratio of expected counts (exposed vs. unexposed), holding other variables constant
  • Continuous predictor (1 unit change): \( ext{IRR} = e^{\beta_1}\)
  • Continuous predictor (\(c\) unit change): \( ext{IRR} = e^{c\beta_1}\)
  • IRR > 1: The predictor increases the rate
  • IRR < 1: The predictor decreases the rate (protective)
  • IRR = 1: No association
Example — Credit Card Use Dataset (CountRate.html)

Model: crcards ~ income + offset(lcases) — predicting number of credit cards from income, offset by log(number of credit card cases).

\[\log\frac{\mu}{t} = -2.387 + 0.021 imes ext{Income}\]

IRR for income: \(e^{0.021} = 1.021\) — With 1 unit increase in income, the incidence rate of credit card use increases by 2.1% (IRR = 1.021, 95% CI: 1.01–1.03, p < 0.0001).

Example — UK Road Accident Dataset (UKaccident.csv)

Model: driverskilled ~ law — predicting deaths from whether seatbelt law was in effect.

  • Before law (law=0): \(\exp(4.705) = 110.5\) deaths per period
  • After law (law=1): \(\exp(4.705 - 0.248) = 86.3\) deaths per period
  • IRR for law: \(e^{-0.248} = 0.78\) — the seatbelt law reduced driver deaths by 22% (IRR = 0.78, 95% CI: 0.74–0.83, p < 0.001)

What is Overdispersion?

The Poisson distribution assumes \( ext{Var}(Y) = \mu\) (mean = variance). Overdispersion occurs when the observed variance exceeds the mean: \( ext{Var}(Y) > \mu\). This is common in real data due to extra heterogeneity, clustering, or zero-inflation.

Consequences of Ignoring Overdispersion

If overdispersion is present but ignored:

  • Standard errors are underestimated
  • Test statistics are inflated
  • p-values are too small (spurious significance)
  • Confidence intervals are too narrow

Detection of Overdispersion

Three Approaches
  1. Chi-square goodness-of-fit test: In R, use poisgof(model) from epiDisplay. Based on residual deviance with df = \(n - p\). p-value > 0.05 indicates good fit (no overdispersion).
  2. Scaled Pearson chi-square statistic: \[\chi^2_P = \sum \frac{(Y_i - \hat\mu_i)^2}{\hat\mu_i}, \quad ext{scaled} = \chi^2_P / (n-p)\] A value close to 1 indicates good fit; a value substantially > 1 indicates overdispersion.
  3. Dispersion parameter from quasi-Poisson: summary(glm(..., family=quasipoisson))$dispersion. Value close to 1 = adequate; > 1 = overdispersion.
R · Overdispersion Detection
library(epiDisplay)

# Method 1: Goodness-of-fit test
poisgof(model.aw.m1)
# $p.value = 0.624 → good fit, no overdispersion issue

# Method 2: Scaled Pearson chi-square (manual)
pearson_chi2 <- sum(residuals(model.aw.m1, type = "pearson")^2)
df <- model.aw.m1$df.residual
scaled_pearson <- pearson_chi2 / df
scaled_pearson  # close to 1 = good

# Method 3: Quasi-Poisson dispersion parameter
quasi <- summary(glm(num_awards ~ math + prog2, data = aw,
                      family = quasipoisson))
quasi$dispersion  # = 1.090 → slightly over 1 but acceptable

Negative Binomial as Solution to Overdispersion

The negative binomial (NB) distribution extends the Poisson by adding a dispersion parameter \( heta\) (also called \(k\) or \(\phi\)) that allows the variance to exceed the mean:

\[ ext{Var}(Y) = \mu + \frac{\mu^2}{ heta}\]

As \( heta o \infty\), the NB distribution converges to Poisson. When \( heta\) is small, overdispersion is severe.

R Implementation

R · Negative Binomial Regression
library(MASS)       # contains glm.nb()
library(broom)

# Negative binomial regression
model.nb <- glm.nb(count ~ predictor1 + predictor2, data = mydata)
summary(model.nb)

# Theta (dispersion parameter)
model.nb$theta         # large theta → closer to Poisson
model.nb$SE.theta      # standard error of theta

# Tidy output with IRR
tidy(model.nb, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)

# Compare NB vs Poisson using LRT
library(lmtest)
lrtest(model.poisson, model.nb)
# Significant p → NB preferred over Poisson

Excess Zeros Problem

If the count outcome has more zeros than expected under a Poisson or NB distribution, a zero-inflated model is needed. These arise when there are two separate processes generating the data:

  1. A binary process determining whether the outcome is structurally zero (e.g., a non-smoker can never get lung cancer from smoking — always zero)
  2. A count process (Poisson or NB) generating the non-zero counts
ModelCount ProcessZero ProcessR Function
ZIPPoissonLogistic (binary)zeroinfl(y ~ x | z, dist="poisson")
ZINBNegative BinomialLogistic (binary)zeroinfl(y ~ x | z, dist="negbin")
Hurdle (Poisson)Poisson (truncated)Logistichurdle(y ~ x, dist="poisson")
R · Zero-Inflated Models
library(pscl)   # contains zeroinfl() and hurdle()

# Zero-Inflated Poisson (ZIP)
# Formula: count_model | zero_inflation_model
model.zip <- zeroinfl(count ~ x1 + x2 | x3,
                       data = mydata, dist = "poisson")
summary(model.zip)

# Zero-Inflated Negative Binomial (ZINB)
model.zinb <- zeroinfl(count ~ x1 + x2 | x3,
                        data = mydata, dist = "negbin")
summary(model.zinb)

# Hurdle model
model.hurdle <- hurdle(count ~ x1 + x2,
                        data = mydata, dist = "poisson")

# Compare models with AIC/BIC
AIC(model.zip, model.zinb, model.hurdle)
BIC(model.zip, model.zinb, model.hurdle)

# Vuong test: ZIP vs standard Poisson
vuong(model.zip, model.poisson)
Purposeful Variable Selection (Wan Nor Arifin, 2019)
  1. Univariable analysis
    • Fit each variable separately
    • Determine significance using Wald test or LR test
    • Select variables with p < 0.25 for multivariable, plus clinically important variables
  2. Multivariable analysis
    • Fit with all selected variables
    • Remove non-significant variables sequentially (backward elimination)
    • Check for confounders: 20% change in remaining coefficients
  3. Check interactions
    • Explore clinically plausible two-way interactions
    • Compare with and without interaction using LRT or AIC

The Deviance

Let \(L_M\) = maximized log-likelihood for model \(M\), and \(L_S\) = maximized log-likelihood for the saturated model (one parameter per observation). The deviance is:

\[ ext{Deviance} = -2(L_M - L_S)\]

It is the likelihood-ratio statistic for comparing model \(M\) to the saturated model.

Pearson Residuals

For Poisson GLMs, the Pearson residual for count \(i\) is:

\[e_i = \frac{y_i - \hat\mu_i}{\sqrt{\hat\mu_i}}\]

The standardized Pearson residual accounts for the estimated standard error. Values with \(| ext{standardized residual}| > 1.96\) (or 3.89 for \(lpha = 0.0001\)) are flagged as potential outliers.

R · Model Fit Assessment
library(epiDisplay)

# Chi-square goodness-of-fit test
poisgof(model.aw.m1)
# $chisq = 190.16, $df = 197, $p.value = 0.624
# p > 0.05 → model fits well

# Standardized residuals
sr <- rstandard(model.acc)
sr[abs(sr) > 1.96]        # observations with poor fit

# AIC comparison (lower is better)
AIC(model.aw.u1, model.aw.u2a, model.aw.m1)
#             df    AIC
# model.aw.u1  2  384.1
# model.aw.u2a 2  414.7
# model.aw.m1  3  372.2  ← best model

# Scaled Pearson chi-square statistic
quasi <- summary(glm(y ~ x1 + x2, family = quasipoisson, data = d))
quasi$dispersion  # value close to 1 = good fit
R · Libraries
library(tidyverse)
library(broom)       # tidy(), augment()
library(janitor)

library(car)         # recode()
library(MASS)        # glm.nb()

# ---- Read datasets ----
# Credit card data (rate data)
credit <- read_table("creditcard.txt",
                      col_names = c('income', 'cases', 'crcards'))
credit <- credit %>% mutate(lcases = log(cases))

# UK accident data (count data, categorical X)
acc <- read.csv("UKaccident.csv")
# driverskilled: number of deaths
# law: 0 = before seatbelt law, 1 = after law

# Poisson simulation data (count, numerical X)
aw <- read.csv("poisson_sim.csv")
# num_awards: awards at a high school
# prog: 1=General, 2=Academic, 3=Vocational
# math: math exam score

# Cigarette data (rate data, from Fleiss et al. 2003)
cigar.day   <- c(0, 5.2, 11.2, 15.9, 20.4, 27.4, 40.8)
person.yrs  <- c(1421, 927, 988, 849, 1567, 1409, 556)
cases       <- c(0, 0, 2, 2, 9, 10, 7)
cig <- data.frame(cigar.day, person.yrs, cases)
cig$rate <- cig$cases / cig$person.yrs
R · Simple Poisson — Count Data (UKaccident)
# Descriptives
tapply(acc$driverskilled, acc$law, sum)
# 0: 11826 total deaths before law
# 1: 1294 total deaths after law
table(acc$law)
# 0: 107 observations, 1: 15 observations
11826/107  # 110.5 mean deaths before law
1294/15    # 86.3 mean deaths after law

# Fit Poisson model
model.acc <- glm(driverskilled ~ law, data = acc, family = poisson)
summary(model.acc)
# (Intercept): 4.705, law: -0.248 (both p < 0.001)

# 95% CI
cbind(coef(model.acc), confint(model.acc))
# law: [-0.306, -0.191]

# Exponentiate: expected counts
exp(4.705)         # 111.1 → before law
exp(4.705 - 0.248) # 86.5 → after law

# Model fit
poisgof(model.acc)
# chisq=142.64, df=120, p=0.078 → fits well

# Standardized residuals → identify outliers
sr <- rstandard(model.acc)
sr[abs(sr) > 1.96]
# observations 4, 54, 55, 91, 113 flagged

# IRR with LR test
idr.display(model.acc)
# law: IDR = 0.78 (95%CI: 0.74, 0.83), p < 0.001
R · Poisson Rate Model — Credit Card & Cigarette Data
# === Credit card rate data ===
# Offset = log(cases), outcome = number of credit cards
credit.m <- glm(crcards ~ income + offset(lcases),
                 family = poisson, data = credit)
summary(credit.m)
# income: coef = 0.021, z = 4.02, p < 0.001
# AIC = 67.6, Residual deviance = 28.47 on 29 df

# CI for log-rate
tidy(credit.m, conf.int = TRUE)

# IRR (incidence rate ratio)
tidy(credit.m, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)
# income IRR = 1.021 (95%CI: 1.010–1.031)
# Interpretation: 1 unit increase in income →
#   rate of credit card use increases by 2.1%

# === Cigarette-lung cancer data (Fleiss et al. 2003) ===
model.cig <- glm(cases ~ cigar.day,
                  offset = log(person.yrs),
                  data = cig, family = "poisson")
summary(model.cig)
# cigar.day: coef = 0.0749, p < 0.001

idr.display(model.cig)
# cigar.day: IDR = 1.08 (95%CI: 1.05–1.11), p < 0.001
# Per 5 cigarettes/day: IRR = exp(0.0749 * 5) = 1.45
# Per 10 cigarettes/day: IRR = exp(0.0749 * 10) = 2.11

# Model fit
poisgof(model.cig)
# p = 0.229 → good fit
R · Multiple Poisson Regression (poisson_sim.csv)
# Create factor variable for programme
aw$prog1 <- factor(aw$prog, levels = 1:3,
                   labels = c("General", "Academic", "Vocational"))

# ---- Univariable ----
# Math score
model.aw.u1 <- glm(num_awards ~ math, data = aw, family = poisson)
# math: coef=0.086, IRR=1.09 (1.07–1.11), p<0.001

# Programme type
model.aw.u2 <- glm(num_awards ~ prog1, data = aw, family = poisson)
# Academic vs General: IRR = 5.00, p<0.001
# Vocational vs General: IRR = 1.20, p=0.679 (not sig.)

# Combine General & Vocational
aw$prog2 <- recode(aw$prog1,
                   "c('General','Vocational') = 'General & Vocational'")

# ---- Multivariable ----
model.aw.m1 <- glm(num_awards ~ math + prog2, data = aw, family = poisson)
summary(model.aw.m1)
# math: coef=0.070, IRR=1.07 (1.05–1.10), p<0.001
# prog2 Gen&Voc vs Academic: coef=-0.891, IRR=0.41 (0.25–0.68), p<0.001

poisgof(model.aw.m1)  # p=0.624 → good fit

idr.display(model.aw.m1)
# math: crude IRR=1.09, adj IRR=1.07, p<0.001
# prog2 Gen&Voc: crude IRR=0.22, adj IRR=0.41, p<0.001

# ---- Interaction check ----
model.aw.i1 <- glm(num_awards ~ math + prog2 + math:prog2,
                    data = aw, family = poisson)
AIC(model.aw.m1, model.aw.i1)
# model.aw.m1: AIC = 372.2 ← better (lower AIC)
# model.aw.i1: AIC = 374.0 → interaction not needed
R · Prediction from Poisson Models
# Predict log rate
predict(credit.m, type = 'link')          # log(μ/t)
predict(credit.m, type = 'link', se.fit = TRUE)

# Predict expected count (response scale)
predict(credit.m, type = 'response')      # μ = expected count

# Fitted values
fitted.values <- fitted(credit.m)

# Residuals
residuals(credit.m)                       # Pearson residuals (default)
residuals(credit.m, type = 'response')    # raw residuals = y - μ̂

# Manual calculation example (Income=65, log(t)=log(6)=1.79)
log.count <- -2.3866 + 0.0208*65 + 1.79
exp(log.count)  # = 2.13 expected count

# New data prediction
new.data <- data.frame(
  income = c(25, 35, 45, 55, 65, 75, 85),
  lcases = log(6)
)
predict(credit.m, newdata = new.data, type = 'response')
# 0.93, 1.14, 1.40, 1.73, 2.13, 2.62, 3.22

Stata · Negative Binomial & Zero-Inflated
* Negative binomial regression
nbreg count x1 x2, irr

* Zero-inflated Poisson (ZIP)
zip count x1 x2, inflate(x3) irr

* Zero-inflated Negative Binomial (ZINB)
zinb count x1 x2, inflate(x3) irr

* Vuong test (ZIP vs Poisson)
zip count x1, inflate(x1) vuong

* Model comparison
estimates store poisson_m
nbreg count x1 x2
estimates store nb_m
lrtest poisson_m nb_m   // NB better if significant

Key Results — Final Model

Best model: num_awards ~ math + prog2 (AIC = 372.2)

  • math: IRR = 1.07 (95%CI: 1.05–1.10), p < 0.001 — for each 1-point increase in math score, expected awards increase by 7%
  • General & Vocational vs Academic: The estimated IRR for General & Vocational students compared to Academic students is 0.41 (95% CI: 0.25–0.68), p < 0.001 — a 59% lower estimated incidence rate of awards — controlling for math score in the model.
  • Interaction (math × program) not significant (AIC increases)
  • Model fits well: chi-square GOF p = 0.624; scaled Pearson = 1.09

Interpretation of the Above Output
  • crude IDR: unadjusted incidence rate ratio (univariable analysis)
  • adj. IDR: adjusted incidence rate ratio (from the multivariable model)
  • For math: Adjusted IRR = 1.07 — for each additional mark in math, the expected number of awards increases by 7%, controlling for programme type
  • For programme type: The estimated IRR for General & Vocational students compared to Academic students is 0.41 (95% CI: 0.25–0.68), indicating a 59% lower estimated incidence rate of awards, controlling for math score in the model.
  • Both crude and adjusted IRRs are significant (p < 0.001 for both Wald and LR tests)
  • The crude IRR for programme (0.22) differs notably from adjusted (0.41) — math score is a confounder of the programme-awards relationship

Statistical Inference

A Wald 95% confidence interval for \(\beta\) is \(\hat\beta \pm 1.96( ext{SE})\). The Wald test statistic:

\[z = \frac{\hat\beta}{ ext{SE}}\]

has an approximate standard normal distribution under \(H_0: \beta = 0\). Equivalently, \(z^2\) follows chi-squared with \(df = 1\). The likelihood ratio test is preferred for inference in generalized linear models.

Dataset: couart2.dta — Biochemists’ Academic Publications

This dataset is from Long & Freese (Regression Models for Categorical Dependent Variables Using Stata). It contains data on 915 PhD biochemists, tracking how many articles they published in the last 3 years of their PhD programme.

VariableLabelTypeNotes

Research question: What factors are associated with the number of academic publications produced by biochemistry PhD students?

Interpretation of Poisson Results — couart2

Log-count (raw coefficient) interpretation:

  • femWomen = -0.225: The expected log of article count for women was on average 0.225 lower than for men, holding other variables constant.
  • marMarried = +0.155: Married scholars had a 0.155 higher expected log-count than single scholars.
  • kid5 = -0.185: Each additional child under 6 reduces the expected log-count by 0.185.
  • ment = +0.026: Each additional mentor publication increases the expected log-count by 0.026.
  • phd = +0.013: Department prestige is not statistically significant (p = 0.627).

IRR (rate ratio) interpretation: For a unit change in \(x_k\), the expected count changes by a factor of \(\exp(\hat{\beta}_k)\).

  • Being female: IRR = 0.799 — women publish at 0.80 times the rate of men (20% fewer articles).
  • Being married: IRR = 1.17 — married scholars publish at 1.17 times the rate of single scholars.
  • Each additional child <6: IRR = 0.831 — reduces publication rate by ~17%.
  • Each mentor publication: IRR = 1.026 — increases student’s rate by ~2.6%.

Dataset: melanoma — Tumour Type by Anatomical Site (Dobson Table 9.4)

Source: Dobson & Barnett, An Introduction to Generalized Linear Models (CRC Press).

Count data often comes in aggregated (tabular) form. This example examines whether there is an association between tumour type and anatomical site. The expected value of \(Y_{jk}\) is:

\[E(Y_{jk}) = \mu_{jk} = n heta_{jk}\]

The log link for the Poisson model gives:

\[\log \mu_{jk} = \log n + \log heta_{jk}\]

The term \(\log n\) is the same for all \(Y_{jk}\) and functions as an offset. The reference categories are Melanotic freckle (HMF) at head or neck (HNK).

Three Competing Models: Saturated, Additive, Minimal

# ── Saturated model (site x tumor interaction) ──────────────────────
ressat.melanoma <- glm(frequency ~ site * tumor,
                       family = poisson(),
                       data = melanoma)

# ── Additive model (main effects only) ──────────────────────────────
resadd.melanoma <- glm(frequency ~ site + tumor,
                       family = poisson(),
                       data = melanoma)

# ── Minimal model (intercept only) ───────────────────────────────────
resmin.melanoma <- glm(frequency ~ 1,
                       family = poisson(),
                       data = melanoma)

# ── Compare models ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
AIC(resmin.melanoma, resadd.melanoma, ressat.melanoma)
anova(resadd.melanoma, ressat.melanoma, test = "Chisq")
* Saturated model
poisson frequency i.site##i.tumor

* Additive model
poisson frequency i.site i.tumor

* Minimal (intercept only)
poisson frequency

* Compare
estimates stats sat add min
* Not covered in lecture notes.

Expected Frequencies from Each Model (Dobson Table 9-10)

Interpreting Expected Frequencies
CellModelCalculationExpected Frequency
HMF on HNK (reference) Minimal\(\exp(3.507)\)33.35
Additive\(\exp(1.754)\)5.78
Saturated\(\exp(3.091)\)22
IND in EXT Minimal\(\exp(3.507)\)33.35
Additive\(\exp(1.754 + 0.499 + 1.201)\)31.64
Saturated\(\exp(3.091 - 0.693 - 0.788 + 1.723)\)28

HMF = Melanotic freckle; HNK = Head or Neck; IND = Indeterminate tumour; EXT = Extremities.

Model Assessment: Residuals & Goodness of Fit

# ── Types of residuals ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Pearson residuals: (observed - expected) / sqrt(expected)
residuals(resadd.melanoma, type = "pearson")

# Deviance residuals (default in R)
residuals(resadd.melanoma, type = "deviance")

# ── Goodness-of-fit tests ───────────────────────────────────────────────
# Deviance GOF: under H0 (model fits), residual deviance ~ chi-sq(df)
with(resadd.melanoma, pchisq(deviance, df.residual, lower.tail = FALSE))

# Pearson chi-square GOF
pearson_x2 <- sum(residuals(resadd.melanoma, type = "pearson")^2)
pchisq(pearson_x2, df.residual(resadd.melanoma), lower.tail = FALSE)
* After fitting model:
poisson frequency i.site i.tumor
estat gof
* Output: Pearson chi-square and deviance GOF statistics

Modern R Workflow for Count Data (Kamarul Imran Musa, USM 2020)

The tidyverse/dplyr approach integrates data manipulation, model fitting, and results extraction into a coherent pipeline. The broom package provides tidy(), glance(), and augment() to extract model results in tidy data frames.

Full tidyverse Pipeline

# ── Libraries ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(dplyr)
library(ggplot2)
library(tidyverse)
library(haven)
library(broom)
library(janitor)

# ── Load & convert labelled variables ───────────────────────────────────
publish <- read_stata('couart2.dta') %>%
  mutate_if(is.labelled, ~as_factor(.))

# ── Exploratory pair plots ───────────────────────────────────────────────
library(GGally)
ggpairs(publish)

# ── Quick count of outcome values ────────────────────────────────────────
publish %>%
  count(art) %>%
  mutate(prop = n / sum(n))

# ── Group-level summaries ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
publish %>%
  group_by(fem, mar) %>%
  summarise(
    n        = n(),
    mean_art = mean(art),
    sd_art   = sd(art),
    .groups  = "drop"
  )

# ── Fit Poisson via pipe ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
mod_1 <- publish %>%
  glm(art ~ fem + mar + kid5 + phd + ment,
      family = poisson(link = 'log'),
      data = .)

# ── broom: tidy, glance, augment ──────────────────────────────────────────
tidy(mod_1, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)  # IRR table

glance(mod_1)
## # A tibble: 1 x 8
##   null.deviance df.null logLik   AIC   BIC deviance df.residual  nobs
##           <dbl>   <int>  <dbl> <dbl> <dbl>    <dbl>       <int> <int>
##        1817.      914  -1651. 3314.  3344.   1634.         909   915

augment(mod_1) %>%
  select(art, .fitted, .resid, .std.resid) %>%
  head(10)

Full Model Comparison: Poisson vs NB vs ZIP vs ZINB

library(MASS)   # for glm.nb()
library(pscl)   # for zeroinfl() and vuong()

# ── Fit all four model types on couart2 ────────────────────────────────
mod_pois <- glm(art ~ fem + mar + kid5 + phd + ment,
                family = poisson(), data = publish)

mod_nb   <- glm.nb(art ~ fem + mar + kid5 + phd + ment,
                   data = publish)

mod_zip  <- zeroinfl(art ~ fem + mar + kid5 + phd + ment | 1,
                     dist = "poisson", data = publish)

mod_zinb <- zeroinfl(art ~ fem + mar + kid5 + phd + ment | 1,
                     dist = "negbin", data = publish)

# ── AIC/BIC comparison table ───────────────────────────────────────────────
model_compare <- data.frame(
  Model  = c("Poisson", "Negative Binomial", "ZIP", "ZINB"),
  AIC    = c(AIC(mod_pois), AIC(mod_nb), AIC(mod_zip),  AIC(mod_zinb)),
  BIC    = c(BIC(mod_pois), BIC(mod_nb), BIC(mod_zip),  BIC(mod_zinb)),
  LogLik = c(logLik(mod_pois), logLik(mod_nb),
             logLik(mod_zip),  logLik(mod_zinb))
)
model_compare[order(model_compare$AIC), ]

# ── Vuong test: ZIP vs Poisson ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# Compares two non-nested models.
# Positive, significant z-statistic => model 1 (ZIP) is better
vuong(mod_zip, mod_pois)

# ── LRT: NB vs Poisson (nested via dispersion parameter) ───────────────
# Under H0: theta -> infinity (no overdispersion), Poisson is adequate
# 2 * (logLik(NB) - logLik(Poisson)) ~ chi-sq(1), one-sided
lrt_stat <- 2 * (as.numeric(logLik(mod_nb)) - as.numeric(logLik(mod_pois)))
pchisq(lrt_stat, df = 1, lower.tail = FALSE) / 2  # one-sided p-value
* Fit all models
poisson art i.fem i.mar kid5 phd ment, irr
estimates store m_pois

nbreg art i.fem i.mar kid5 phd ment, irr
estimates store m_nb

zip art i.fem i.mar kid5 phd ment, inflate(_cons) vuong
estimates store m_zip

zinb art i.fem i.mar kid5 phd ment, inflate(_cons) vuong
estimates store m_zinb

* Comparison table (AIC/BIC/log-likelihood)
estimates stats m_pois m_nb m_zip m_zinb

* Vuong test is run automatically with the vuong option in zip/zinb
* Not covered in lecture notes.
Model Selection Decision Guide — Count Data
SituationRecommended ModelR FunctionStata Command
Mean ≈ Variance, no excess zerosPoissonglm(..., family=poisson)poisson
Overdispersion (Var > Mean), no excess zerosNegative BinomialMASS::glm.nb()nbreg
Excess zeros, Poisson counts otherwiseZero-Inflated Poisson (ZIP)pscl::zeroinfl(..., dist="poisson")zip
Excess zeros + overdispersionZero-Inflated NB (ZINB)pscl::zeroinfl(..., dist="negbin")zinb
Rate with varying exposurePoisson + offsetglm(..., offset=log(t))poisson, exposure(t)

Decision workflow:

  1. Fit Poisson → check GOF (chi-square, deviance) and dispersion ratio (Pearson chi-sq / df)
  2. If dispersion > 1 → fit Negative Binomial; compare using LRT
  3. Check zero proportion: if observed zeros significantly exceed Poisson-expected zeros → consider ZIP or ZINB
  4. Use Vuong test to compare ZIP vs Poisson (or ZINB vs NB) — these are non-nested models
  5. Final model selection: lowest AIC, lowest BIC, best clinical/theoretical interpretability
Key Points — Count and Rate Data Analysis
  1. Poisson regression is used for count outcomes where events follow a Poisson distribution
  2. For rate data with varying exposure, add offset(log(t)) to the model
  3. Coefficients are interpreted as Incidence Rate Ratios (IRR) after exponentiation
  4. Poisson assumes mean = variance; violations lead to overdispersion
  5. Detect overdispersion using: chi-square GOF test, scaled Pearson statistic, or quasi-Poisson dispersion
  6. Solutions to overdispersion: quasi-Poisson (adjusts SE only) or negative binomial regression (models the excess variance explicitly)
  7. For excess zeros: use Zero-Inflated Poisson (ZIP) or Zero-Inflated Negative Binomial (ZINB) models
  8. Model selection: compare AIC, use LRT for nested models
  9. In R: glm(..., family=poisson), MASS::glm.nb(), pscl::zeroinfl()
  10. In Stata: poisson, nbreg, zip, zinb
References
  • Agresti, A. (2007). An Introduction to Categorical Data Analysis, 2nd Ed. Wiley.
  • Dobson, A.J. An Introduction to Generalized Linear Models. Chapman & Hall/CRC.
  • Fleiss, J.L., Levin, B., & Paik, M.C. (2003). Statistical Methods for Rates and Proportions, 3rd Ed. Wiley.
  • Wan Nor Arifin (2019). Poisson Regression. USM Biostatistics Notes.
  • Kamarul Imran Musa (2020). Analysis for Rate Data (CountRate.html). USM.
  • Regression Models for Count Data in R: pscl vignette

4.W1 Dr Wan — Poisson for Count Data (Asthma Dataset)

Source: Kamarul Imran Musa, Chapter 10 🔗 Dr Wan’s DrPH Lecture Notes

Why Poisson, not linear regression?
  • Count data are discrete — linear regression can predict impossible negative counts
  • Poisson models ln(count), guaranteeing predicted values ≥ 0
  • Coefficient exponentiated = IRR (Incidence Rate Ratio), interpreted like OR
\[ \text{IRR} = e^{b_p} \quad \text{for the coefficient } b_p \text{ of predictor } p \]

Libraries

library(tidyverse)   # data transformation
library(gtsummary)   # publication tables: tbl_regression()
library(broom)       # tidy() extracts model output as tibble
# epiDisplay used as: epiDisplay::function_name()  (avoids tidyverse conflict)

Dataset — asthma.csv (n=120)

gender: male/female  |  res_inf: recurrent respiratory infection (no/yes)  |  ghq12: GHQ-12 score 0–36 (continuous)  |  attack: asthmatic attacks/year (count outcome) 
asthma = read.csv("data/asthma.csv")
str(asthma)          # 120 obs, 4 variables
epiDisplay::codebook(asthma)
# attack: mean=2.46, median=2, sd=2.01, range 0-9

Univariable Analysis

pois_attack1 = glm(attack ~ gender,  data=asthma, family="poisson")
pois_attack2 = glm(attack ~ res_inf, data=asthma, family="poisson")
pois_attack3 = glm(attack ~ ghq12,   data=asthma, family="poisson")

summary(pois_attack1)  # gendermale:  p=0.013 *
summary(pois_attack2)  # res_infyes:  p<0.001 ***  (strongest predictor)
summary(pois_attack3)  # ghq12:       p<0.001 ***
Screen at P < 0.25 for multivariable inclusion. All three qualify.

Multivariable Analysis

# Full model
pois_attack_all = glm(attack ~ ., data=asthma, family="poisson")
# gendermale: p=0.732 --> NOT significant at multivariable level

# Drop gender
pois_attack_all1 = glm(attack ~ res_inf + ghq12, data=asthma, family="poisson")
summary(pois_attack_all1)
# res_infyes: Estimate=0.428, p=0.005 **
# ghq12:      Estimate=0.050, p<0.001 ***  --> PRELIMINARY FINAL MODEL

Interaction

pois_attack_allx = glm(attack ~ res_inf * ghq12, data=asthma, family="poisson")
# res_infyes:ghq12  p=0.041 * (borderline)
# AIC without interaction: 415.86  |  AIC with: 413.74 (marginal gain)
Decision: p=0.04 is borderline. For prediction include it; for clinical interpretation choose simpler model without interaction (IRR for GHQ-12 changes minimally: 1.04 vs 1.07). Parsimony preferred.

Model Fit Assessment

# 1. Chi-square goodness-of-fit (residual deviance method)
epiDisplay::poisgof(pois_attack_all1)
# p=0.102 --> p>0.05 = GOOD FIT

# 2. AIC comparison
AIC(pois_attack1, pois_attack2, pois_attack3, pois_attack_all1, pois_attack_allx)
# pois_attack_all1: 415.86  (best parsimonious)

# 3. Scaled Pearson chi-square (overdispersion check)
x2  = sum((asthma$attack - pois_attack_all1$fitted)^2 / pois_attack_all1$fitted)
df  = nrow(asthma) - length(pois_attack_all1$coefficients)
x2/df   # = 1.052  --> close to 1 = good fit

# Shortcut via quasipoisson:
summary(glm(attack ~ res_inf + ghq12, data=asthma,
            family="quasipoisson"))$dispersion  # [1] 1.052

# 4. Standardized residuals
std_res = rstandard(pois_attack_all1)
std_res[abs(std_res) > 1.96]   # obs 28, 38, 95, 103 flagged
CheckCriterionResultVerdict
Chi-sq GOFp > 0.05p = 0.102✓ Good
Scaled χ²/df≈ 11.052✓ Good
AICLowest415.86✓ Best parsimonious
Std residuals|z| < 1.964 outliers⚠ Review obs 28,38,95,103

Final Model — Presentation & Interpretation

pois_attack_final = pois_attack_all1
tbl_regression(pois_attack_final, exponentiate=TRUE)
# res_infyes: IRR=1.53 (95%CI: 1.14, 2.08), p=0.005
# ghq12:      IRR=1.05 (95%CI: 1.04, 1.07), p<0.001

tib = tidy(pois_attack_final, exponentiate=TRUE, conf.int=TRUE)
write.csv(tib, "tib_pois_attack.csv")
Model Equation
\[ \ln(\widehat{\text{attack}}) = -0.34 + 0.43 \times \text{res\_inf} + 0.05 \times \text{ghq12} \]
  • Those with recurrent respiratory infection are at higher risk of having an asthmatic attack with an IRR of 1.53 (95% CI: 1.14, 2.08), while controlling for the effect of GHQ-12 score.
  • An increase in GHQ-12 score by one mark increases the risk of having an asthmatic attack by 1.05 (95% CI: 1.04, 1.07), while controlling for the effect of recurrent respiratory infection.

Rescaling for clinically meaningful units

# IRR for 6-mark increase (1/6 of max GHQ-12 score):
# Manual: exp(0.05 x 6) = 1.35  -- no CI available this way

# Better: refit with rescaled variable
asthma_new = asthma %>% mutate(ghq12_by6 = ghq12 / 6)
pois_by6 = glm(attack ~ res_inf + ghq12_by6, data=asthma_new, family="poisson")
tidy(pois_by6, exponentiate=TRUE, conf.int=TRUE)
# ghq12_by6: IRR=1.35 (95%CI: 1.23, 1.48), p<0.001
# --> 6-mark increase raises attack rate by 35%

Prediction

# Single prediction
predict(pois_attack_final,
        list(res_inf="yes", ghq12=8), type="response")  # 1.6 attacks/year

# Range of predictions
new_data = data.frame(
  res_inf = rep(c("yes","no"), each=4),
  ghq12   = rep(c(0, 12, 24, 36), 2))
new_data$attack_pred = round(predict(pois_attack_final, new_data, type="response"), 1)
#   res_inf=yes: 1.1, 2.0, 3.6, 6.6 (GHQ 0, 12, 24, 36)
#   res_inf=no:  0.7, 1.3, 2.4, 4.3

4.W2 Dr Wan — Poisson for Rate Data (Smoking / Lung Cancer) 🔗 Dr Wan’s DrPH Lecture Notes

Source: Kamarul Imran Musa, Chapter 10 §10.5 | Original data: Doll (1971)

Why add an offset for rate data?

Same number of deaths in a small population = higher rate than in a large population. Adding offset = ln(denominator) adjusts for unequal exposure:

\[ \ln(\hat{y}) = \ln(t) + b_0 + b_1x_1 + \cdots + b_px_p \]

The offset term is not estimated — it is fixed with coefficient = 1. In R: offset = log(person_yrs) inside glm().

Dataset — smoke.csv (n=63)

63 rows = 7 cigarettes/day groups × 9 smoking-year strata. Original: Doll (1971).
smoke_yrs: years of smoking (categorical, 9 levels: 15–19 to 55–59)  |  cigar_day: cigarettes per day (continuous)  |  person_yrs: person-years at risk (offset)  |  case: lung cancer cases (count outcome) 
smoke = read.csv("data/smoke.csv")
epiDisplay::codebook(smoke)
# cigar_day: mean=17.3, sd=12.9, range 0-40.8
# person_yrs: mean=2426, range 104-10366
# case: mean=2.7, sd=3.3, range 0-12

smoke %>% mutate(rate = round(case/person_yrs, 4))
# Rate increases with cigar_day AND smoke_yrs

Univariable Analysis

# offset = log(person_yrs) added to ALL models
pois_case1 = glm(case ~ cigar_day,
                 data=smoke, family="poisson", offset=log(person_yrs))
summary(pois_case1)
# cigar_day: Estimate=0.070, z=10.88, p<0.001 ***

pois_case2 = glm(case ~ smoke_yrs,
                 data=smoke, family="poisson", offset=log(person_yrs))
summary(pois_case2)
# smoke_yrs 30-34 to 55-59: all p<0.001
# smoke_yrs 20-24: p=0.333 (NS at univariable)
# Residual deviance: 445 --> 170 (large reduction)

Multivariable Analysis

pois_case = glm(case ~ cigar_day + smoke_yrs,
                data=smoke, family="poisson", offset=log(person_yrs))
summary(pois_case)
# cigar_day:      Estimate=0.064, z=10.05, p<0.001 ***
# smoke_yrs30-34 to 55-59: all p<0.001
# smoke_yrs20-24 (p=0.40), smoke_yrs25-29 (p=0.11): NS but KEEP
#   (they are dummy variables of one categorical variable -- must keep together)
# Residual deviance: 68.0 on 53 df  (excellent reduction from null 445)

Interaction (numerical instability warning)

pois_casex = glm(case ~ cigar_day * smoke_yrs,
                 data=smoke, family="poisson", offset=log(person_yrs))
# WARNING: glm.fit: fitted rates numerically 0 occurred
# cigar_day SE = 319.6  (should be ~0.006) -- EXTREME inflation
# All interaction p-values ~0.99  --> drop interaction
⚠ Numerical instability: SE >> 1 for continuous predictor in interaction signals convergence failure (likely perfect separation in cells). Drop interaction — Hosmer, Lemeshow & Sturdivant (2013).

Model Fit Assessment

# 1. Chi-sq GOF
epiDisplay::poisgof(pois_case)
# p=0.080 --> GOOD FIT

# 2. AIC
AIC(pois_case1, pois_case2, pois_case)
# pois_case: AIC=207.9 (lowest) --> BEST

# 3. Scaled Pearson chi-square
summary(glm(case ~ cigar_day + smoke_yrs, data=smoke,
            family="quasipoisson",
            offset=log(person_yrs)))$dispersion
# [1] 1.073 --> close to 1 = good fit

# 4. Std residuals
std_res = rstandard(pois_case)
std_res[abs(std_res) > 1.96]  # obs 6, 8, 18
# All are non-smokers (cigar_day=0) with 0 cases
# but model predicts ~1.8-2.2 due to long smoking years

Final Model — Presentation & Interpretation

pois_case_final = pois_case
tbl_regression(pois_case_final, exponentiate=TRUE)

tib_pois_case = tidy(pois_case_final, exponentiate=TRUE, conf.int=TRUE)
write.csv(tib_pois_case, "tib_pois_case.csv")
Model Equation (with offset)
\[ \ln(\text{case}) = \ln(\text{person\_yrs}) - 11.32 + 0.06 \times \text{cigar\_day} + 0.96 \times \text{smoke\_yrs}_{20\text{-}24} + \cdots + 5.37 \times \text{smoke\_yrs}_{55\text{-}59} \]
smoke_yrsIRR95% CIp-valueInterpretation
15–19 (ref)1.00——Reference
20–242.620.34, 52.90.40NS; CI includes 1
25–295.530.94, 1050.11NS; CI includes 1
30–3424.75.23, 4420.00224.7× higher rate
40–4467.214.6, 1195<0.00167× higher rate
55–5921545.1, 3862<0.001215× higher; wide CI (few obs)
Interpretations (Dr Wan):
• Taking an additional cigarette per day increases the risk of having lung cancer by 1.07 (95% CI: 1.05, 1.08), while controlling for the other variables.
• Those who had been smoking for between 30 to 34 years are at higher risk of having lung cancer with an IRR of 24.7 (95% CI: 5.23, 442), while controlling for the other variables.
• Note: IRRs for smoke_yrs 20–24 and 25–29 have 95% CIs that include 1 (p > 0.05) — no significant difference from the 15–19 year reference group. Wide CIs for higher strata reflect sparse data in those cells.

4.W3 Dr Wan — Quasi-Poisson & REAAAPP Workflow Summary 🔗 Dr Wan’s DrPH Lecture Notes

Source: Kamarul Imran Musa, Chapter 10 §10.6

What is overdispersion & why it matters

Poisson assumes mean = variance. Overdispersion = variance > mean (scaled χ²/df > 1).

Consequence: standard Poisson underestimates SE → narrower CIs → inflated z-statistics → false positives.

Quasi-Poisson uses quasi-likelihood: inflates SE by √(dispersion), correcting for overdispersion. Coefficients are identical to standard Poisson.

Poisson vs Quasi-Poisson — Side by Side

pois_model  = glm(attack ~ ., data=asthma, family="poisson")
qpois_model = glm(attack ~ ., data=asthma, family="quasipoisson")

tidy(pois_model,  conf.int=TRUE)   # compare std.error and p.value
tidy(qpois_model, conf.int=TRUE)   # SE slightly larger, CI wider
TermEstimateSE (Poisson)SE (Quasi-P)p (Poisson)p (Quasi-P)
(Intercept)-0.3150.1830.1890.0860.098
gendermale-0.0420.1220.1260.7320.740
res_infyes0.4260.1530.1570.0050.008
ghq120.0500.007880.00812<0.001<0.001
Coefficients are identical. Only SE, CI and p-values differ. Interpretation of IRR is the same.
Model selection guide
SituationUse
Scaled χ²/df ≈ 1Standard Poisson
Scaled χ²/df > 1 (overdispersion)Quasi-Poisson or Negative Binomial (MASS::glm.nb)
Need AIC comparisonNegative Binomial (quasi-Poisson has no likelihood → no AIC)
Excess zeros in count outcomeZIP or ZINB via pscl::zeroinfl()
Rate data (varying exposure)Any model above + offset=log(denominator)

Dr Wan’s Full REAAAPP Workflow

StepActionR FunctionDecision Rule
Research QDefine count/rate outcome + predictors—Outcome a count/rate? → Poisson
ExploreDescriptive stats; check distributionepiDisplay::codebook()Mean ≈ variance? (Poisson assumption)
AssumptionsCheck overdispersionsummary(quasipoisson)$dispersionDispersion ≈ 1 → OK; >1 → quasi/NB
Analysis — UnivariableFit each predictor aloneglm(..., family="poisson")Screen P < 0.25
Analysis — MultivariableFull model; remove non-sigglm(y ~ ., ...)P < 0.05; LRT for nested models
Assess interactionFit interaction termsglm(y ~ a * b, ...)p < 0.05 AND clinically meaningful
PresentIRR table + model equationtbl_regression(..., exponentiate=TRUE)Report IRR, 95% CI, p; include offset in equation
PredictPredict for new valuespredict(model, newdata, type="response")Returns predicted count (or rate × t)

3-Element IRR Interpretation Template

  1. Direction + IRR: “Those with [exposure] have a [higher/lower] rate of [outcome] with IRR = [value]…”
  2. 95% CI: “…(95% CI: [lower], [upper])…”
  3. p-value + controlling for: “…(p = [value]), while controlling for [covariates].”
Example: “Those with recurrent respiratory infection have a higher rate of asthmatic attacks with IRR = 1.53 (95% CI: 1.14, 2.08; p=0.005), while controlling for GHQ-12 score.”

Common Exam Mistakes

MistakeCorrect approach
Forgetting offset for rate dataAlways add offset=log(denominator)
Removing non-sig dummy variables individuallyKeep all dummies for a categorical variable together
IRR for 1-unit change when clinically irrelevantRescale variable; refit model to get CI for meaningful unit
Ignoring overdispersionAlways check scaled χ²; switch to quasi-Poisson if >1
Forgetting to exponentiateexponentiate=TRUE in tbl_regression() or tidy()
Interaction: interpreting main effect onlySubstitute modifier value into equation; obtain separate slopes
References: Doll R (1971); Fleiss, Levin & Paik (2003); Frome (1983); Woodward (2013); Hosmer, Lemeshow & Sturdivant (2013)
Topic 05 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Correlated Categorical Data

GEE · GLMM · ICC · Repeated Measures · Wide & Long Format · Kamarul Imran Musa & Dr Hazlienor Mohd Hatta

Course Objectives — Binary Logistic Regression for Correlated Data

Kamarul Imran Musa, Associate Professor (Epidemiology and Statistics), USM — 2020

  1. Understand why standard logistic regression fails for correlated/clustered binary data
  2. Distinguish between marginal (GEE) and conditional (GLMM) models
  3. Estimate and interpret parameters from both model families
  4. Apply model checking and diagnostics for correlated binary outcomes
  5. Implement analyses in R and Stata using GLOW_RAND and toenail datasets

Introduction for Correlated Binary Data

A particular issue in the analysis of proportions is the presence of extrabinomial variation. This is caused by a violation of the assumption that the binary responses on which a proportion is based are independent.

One way to allow for clustering (non-independence) due to omitted group-level predictors is to fit a multilevel model with group-level random effects.

Repeated measure data with categorical outcome has the same problems:

  1. Data with non-normal distribution
  2. Violation of the independence assumption — assuming independence in repeated measure produces small standard errors (falsely narrow CIs, inflated Type I error)

To cater for the second problem, two simple methods have been normally used to correct the standard errors in repeated measures:

  1. Bootstrapping
  2. Robust variance estimator
IMPORTANT

Both methods above do not consider fully the data structure, including dependencies over time. More advanced methods — marginal models and conditional models — can handle this problem better. They consider dependencies but the correlation assumptions differ between them.

Goals for Analysis

The goals are identical to non-clustered binary logistic regression: estimate the effect of covariates on the dichotomous outcome via odds ratio. The biggest challenge is properly handling correlations among observations during the analysis.

Data Comes in 2 Forms

FormatDescriptionExample
MultilevelSubjects nested within clustersPatients within physicians within hospitals
Repeated measuresMultiple observations per subject over timeToenail fungal trial — 7 visits per patient
GLOW_RAND 3-Level Structure

Level 1: sub_id (500 subjects) → Level 2: phy_id (124 physicians, cluster size 1–13) → Level 3: site_id (6 sites, cluster size 24–113)

Models to Analyse Correlated/Repeated Categorical Data

Model TypeAlso Known AsInterpretationR functionStata

Conditional (GLMM)Random effects, mixed models, multilevelSubject-specific ORglmer()melogit
TransitionalMarkov modelTransition probabilitiesmsm()msset

Multilevel Models

Multilevel models (also known as hierarchical linear models, nested data models, mixed models, random coefficient, random-effects models, random parameter models, or split-plot designs) are statistical models of parameters that vary at more than one level.

An example: a model of student performance that contains measures for individual students as well as measures for classrooms within which the students are grouped.

Reference

Ref: YouTube — Multilevel models explained

Conditional Models — Three Subtypes

  1. Random intercept model — each cluster has its own baseline; slope is common
  2. Random slope model — the effect of a predictor varies across clusters
  3. Random intercepts and slopes model — both vary across clusters

Models to Analyze Repeated Measures

Three main families (from lecture notes):

  1. Marginal models
  2. Conditional models (random effect models)
  3. Transitional models
Conditional models are also known as
  1. Hierarchical models
  2. Multilevel models
  3. Mixed models
  4. Random intercept and coefficient models

Wide vs Long Data Format for Correlated Data Analysis

Why This Matters

All GEE and GLMM packages in R (gee, geepack, lme4) require data in LONG format. If your data is in wide format, you must reshape it first. Understanding this distinction prevents the most common data preparation errors.

FormatStructureRowsColumnsRequired For
WideOne row per subjectN subjectsOne column per time point/variableSome ANOVA procedures; easy to read
LongOne row per observationN × T observationsTime/visit as a variableGEE, GLMM, melogit, xtgee

Suitable Packages by Task

PackageFunctionFormatModelNotes
geegee()LongGEEOriginal GEE; id= specifies cluster
geepackgeeglm()LongGEERecommended; supports QIC; better summary
lme4glmer()LongGLMMStandard for mixed models; (1|id) syntax
MASSglmmPQL()LongGLMM (PQL)Fast but biased; avoid for binary with small N
nlmelme()LongLMM (continuous)For continuous outcomes only
tidyrpivot_longer()Wide→LongReshapingModern tidyverse approach; recommended
tidyrpivot_wider()Long→WideReshapingReverse of pivot_longer
reshape2melt()Wide→LongReshapingOlder approach; still widely used
reshape2dcast()Long→WideReshapingReverse of melt
performanceicc()LongICCConvenience wrapper for ICC from glmer
sjPlottab_model()LongOutput tablesPublication-ready tables for GLMM
DHARMasimulateResiduals()LongDiagnosticsSimulation-based residuals for GLMMs
broom.mixedtidy()LongTidy outputTidy coefficients from lme4 models

R Code: Reshaping and Checking Format

library(tidyverse)
library(reshape2)
library(tidyr)

# ── Example: Toenail data ─────────────────────────────────────────
library(haven)
toenail <- read_dta("toenail.dta")

# Long format (one row per observation — required for GEE/GLMM)
head(toenail)
# ID | Y | treatment | month | visit
#  1 | 0 |         1 |     0 |     1
#  1 | 0 |         1 |     1 |     2

# Converting WIDE → LONG with tidyr::pivot_longer()
# Suppose data is in wide format (one row per subject, columns = time)
df_wide <- data.frame(
  ID        = 1:3,
  Y_visit1  = c(0,1,0),
  Y_visit2  = c(0,0,1),
  Y_visit3  = c(1,0,1),
  treatment = c(1,2,1)
)
df_wide

df_long <- df_wide %>%
  pivot_longer(cols      = starts_with("Y_"),
               names_to  = "visit",
               values_to = "Y") %>%
  mutate(visit = as.integer(gsub("Y_visit", "", visit)))
df_long

# Converting LONG → WIDE with tidyr::pivot_wider()
df_back_wide <- df_long %>%
  pivot_wider(names_from  = visit,
              values_from = Y,
              names_prefix = "Y_visit")
df_back_wide

# reshape2 alternative
df_long2 <- melt(df_wide,
                 id.vars       = c("ID","treatment"),
                 variable.name = "visit",
                 value.name    = "Y")
df_long2

# ── Check format requirements for each package ─────────────────────
# gee::gee()       → LONG format, id = cluster ID variable
# geepack::geeglm() → LONG format, id = cluster ID variable
# lme4::glmer()    → LONG format, random effect specified via (1|id)
# MASS::glmmPQL()  → LONG format
# nlme::lme()      → LONG format (for continuous outcomes)

# ── GLOW_RAND is already in long/multilevel format ─────────────────
data_glow <- glow_rand
# Each row = one subject; clustering by site_id and phy_id
# No reshaping needed — just specify id = site_id in gee()

# ── For repeated measures: ensure proper ordering ──────────────────
df_long <- df_long %>% arrange(ID, visit)  # MUST sort by ID then time

# ── Useful checks before fitting GEE/GLMM ─────────────────────────
# 1. Number of observations per cluster
toenail %>% count(ID) %>% summary()

# 2. Check for complete/balanced data
toenail %>%
  group_by(ID) %>%
  summarise(n_visits = n()) %>%
  count(n_visits)

# 3. Missing data pattern
toenail %>%
  group_by(visit) %>%
  summarise(n = n(), missing = sum(is.na(Y)))
* Stata: reshape between LONG and WIDE
* Assume data in LONG format (standard for xtgee/melogit)

* LONG → WIDE
reshape wide Y, i(ID treatment) j(visit)
list in 1/5

* WIDE → LONG (required for xtgee and melogit)
reshape long Y, i(ID treatment) j(visit)
sort ID visit

* Check cluster sizes
bysort ID: gen n_obs = _N
tab n_obs

* xtset declares panel structure (required before xt commands)
xtset ID visit
xtdescribe         /* describe panel structure */
xtsum Y            /* panel-aware summary */

* For GEE (xtgee) — data must be in LONG format, xtset not required
* but sorting by cluster variable recommended
sort site_id
xtgee frac2 weight5 i.raterisk i.armassist, ///
      i(site_id) family(binomial) link(logit) corr(exchangeable)

* For melogit — LONG format, no xtset needed
melogit fracture weight5 i.raterisk i.armassist || site_id:
* SPSS: Restructure between WIDE and LONG

* WIDE → LONG (Restructure wizard or VARSTOCASES)
VARSTOCASES
  /MAKE Y FROM Y_visit1 Y_visit2 Y_visit3
  /INDEX = visit (3)
  /KEEP = ID treatment
  /NULL = KEEP.

* LONG → WIDE (CASESTOVARS)
CASESTOVARS
  /ID = ID treatment
  /INDEX = visit
  /GROUPBY = VARIABLE.

* GEE in SPSS (Generalized Estimating Equations)
* Data must be in LONG format
GENLIN Y (REFERENCE=FIRST) BY treatment visit
  /MODEL treatment visit INTERCEPT=YES
         DISTRIBUTION=BINOMIAL LINK=LOGIT
  /REPEATED SUBJECT=ID WITHINSUBJECT=visit
            SORT=YES
         CORRTYPE=EXCHANGEABLE
  /PRINT SOLUTION.
Golden Rules for Correlated Data Preparation
  1. Always reshape to LONG format before fitting GEE or GLMM
  2. Sort data by cluster ID then time (arrange(ID, visit))
  3. Verify cluster sizes with count(ID) before fitting
  4. Check for missing observations — GEE handles MAR; GLMM also MAR under certain conditions
  5. In Stata, use xtset ID time to declare panel structure before xtgee
  6. In SPSS, use GENLIN with REPEATED subcommand — data must be LONG

Package Usability Guide: Wide & Long Format

Choosing the right package for reshaping or analysis depends on your data format, software ecosystem, and performance needs. The notes below cover each package's usability profile, including strengths, limitations, and common pitfalls.

🔄 Reshaping Packages (R)

PackageFunctionsEase of UseSpeedBest ForLimitations
tidyr (tidyverse) pivot_longer() / pivot_wider() ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ Very intuitive; pipe-friendly Fast for moderate data Recommended for most use cases. Multiple column sets, regex column selection, handles names/values_to cleanly. Slightly verbose syntax for complex pivots; slower than data.table on very large datasets (>1M rows).
reshape2 melt() / dcast() ⭐⭐⭐⭐ Mature; well-documented Moderate Legacy code; simpler syntax for basic reshaping. melt() = Wide→Long; dcast() = Long→Wide with optional aggregation function. No longer actively developed. dcast() aggregation can be confusing. pivot_longer() preferred in new scripts.
data.table melt() / dcast() ⭐⭐⭐ Requires data.table syntax ⚡ Fastest option for large datasets Large epidemiological datasets (>500K rows). Parallelised. melt.data.table() handles multiple simultaneous reshape columns. Steeper learning curve; different syntax paradigm from tidyverse. Not beginner-friendly.
stats::reshape() (base R) reshape() ⭐⭐ Complex arguments Moderate No package dependencies; useful in package development. Confusing argument names (idvar, timevar, direction). Avoid unless no other packages available.
tidyr vs reshape2 — Practical Comparison
Tasktidyr (recommended)reshape2 (legacy)
Wide → Longpivot_longer(cols=starts_with("Y_"), names_to="visit", values_to="Y")melt(df, id.vars=c("ID","treat"), variable.name="visit", value.name="Y")
Long → Widepivot_wider(names_from=visit, values_from=Y, names_prefix="Y_")dcast(df, ID+treat ~ visit, value.var="Y")
Multiple value columns✅ Yes — values_from=c(Y,X)⚠️ Requires multiple calls
Name cleaning✅ names_prefix, names_sep, names_pattern❌ Manual post-processing
Pipe-compatible✅ Full tidyverse pipe support⚠️ Partial

📊 Analysis Packages: Format Requirements & Usability

PackageRequired FormatUsabilityKey Syntax NoteSuitable When
geepack::geeglm() LONG ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ Best GEE option in R geeglm(Y ~ X, id=cluster_id, data=df, corstr="exchangeable") Clustered/repeated binary data; population-average OR needed; large sample.
gee::gee() LONG ⭐⭐⭐ Original GEE; less output gee(Y ~ X, id=cluster_id, data=df, family=binomial, corstr="exchangeable") Quick GEE fit; no QIC needed. Prefer geepack for full output.
lme4::glmer() LONG ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ Gold standard for GLMM glmer(Y ~ X + (1|cluster_id), data=df, family=binomial) Subject-specific OR; random intercept per cluster; ICC estimation.
MASS::glmmPQL() LONG ⭐⭐⭐ Simple syntax; biased estimates glmmPQL(Y ~ X, random=~1|cluster_id, data=df, family=binomial) Quick approximation only. Not recommended for binary outcome with small cluster size — PQL estimates are biased.
nlme::lme() LONG ⭐⭐⭐⭐ Good for continuous outcomes lme(Y ~ X, random=~1|cluster_id, data=df) Continuous repeated measures only. Not suitable for binary/count outcomes.
nnet::multinom() LONG (standard data frame) ⭐⭐⭐⭐ Simple; no Wald p-values by default multinom(Y ~ X, data=df) + summary() + manual z/p calculation Multinomial outcome (Topic 1). Does not need wide format unlike mlogit.
VGAM::vglm() WIDE (for multinomial via cbind) or LONG ⭐⭐⭐ Flexible but complex vglm(cbind(Y1,Y2,Y3) ~ X, family=multinomial, data=df) — wide; or vglm(Y ~ X, family=multinomial, data=df) — long Multinomial or ordinal outcomes; supports many GLM families. Steeper learning curve.
mlogit::mlogit() WIDE (then uses mlogit.data()) ⭐⭐⭐ Requires special data format df_ml <- mlogit.data(df, shape="wide", choice="Y", id.var="ID") then mlogit(Y ~ 1 | X, data=df_ml) Discrete choice models with alternative-varying covariates. Unnecessary complexity for standard multinomial logistic.
survival::clogit() LONG (matched sets as strata) ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ Clean syntax; widely used clogit(Y ~ X + strata(match_id), data=df) Matched case-control; each matched set is a stratum. Requires 1 row per subject per matched set.

⚠️ Common Wide/Long Format Pitfalls

PitfallSymptomSolution
Fitting GEE/GLMM on wide dataError: "id variable not found" or incorrect cluster assignmentReshape to long first using pivot_longer(); verify with head(df_long)
Unsorted cluster dataGEE convergence issues; wrong correlation estimatesAlways arrange(cluster_id, time) before fitting
Factor levels lost after pivotVariables become character; model drops reference level incorrectlyRe-apply mutate(across(c(X1,X2), as.factor)) after reshaping
mlogit package confusionError: "data must be in wide format"Use mlogit.data() wrapper; or switch to nnet::multinom() which uses standard long format
Duplicate rows after pivot_widerCells contain lists instead of valuesAggregate first: group_by() %>% summarise() before widening, or use values_fn=mean
SPSS VARSTOCASES drops covariatesTime-invariant covariates missing in long dataList all time-invariant variables in /KEEP subcommand
Stata reshape direction confusionWrong j variable usedreshape long Y, i(ID) j(visit) — j is the new time index variable; i is the cluster ID

🔑 Decision Guide: Which Package to Use?

Choose Your Reshaping Package
  • Standard epidemiology work (most cases): Use tidyr::pivot_longer() / pivot_wider() — best documentation, active development, pipe-friendly.
  • Legacy scripts / inherited code: reshape2::melt() / dcast() — still works; consider migrating to tidyr.
  • Large datasets (>500K rows) or performance-critical work: data.table::melt() / dcast() — fastest option.
  • No package dependencies allowed: stats::reshape() — base R fallback only.
Choose Your Analysis Package
  • Clustered binary data, population-level inference → GEE: Use geepack::geeglm(). Data must be LONG. Specify corstr, id.
  • Repeated measures binary data, subject-specific inference → GLMM: Use lme4::glmer(). Data must be LONG. Use (1|cluster_id) syntax.
  • Multinomial outcome (3+ unordered categories): Use nnet::multinom() (LONG, simpler) or VGAM::vglm() (more flexible). Avoid mlogit unless you have alternative-specific covariates.
  • Matched case-control: Use survival::clogit(). Data must be LONG with one row per subject; matched set specified via strata().
  • Avoid MASS::glmmPQL() for binary outcomes — PQL approximation is biased, especially with small cluster sizes or rare outcomes.

Marginal Models — Generalised Estimating Equations (GEE)

In marginal models, a common method is GEE. It is also known as the population average approach. A marginal regression model does not address questions concerning heterogeneity between individuals.

In the population average model, we average probabilities of the outcome over the statistical distribution of the random effect.

The Marginal Model

\[ \text{logit}[E(Y_{ij}|X_{ij})] = \beta_0 + \beta_1 X_{ij1} + \cdots + \beta_p X_{ijp} \]

Where \(Y_{ij}\) is the binary outcome for subject j in cluster i, and \(\beta\) parameters have population-average interpretation. The correlation structure of \(Y_{ij}\) is modelled separately via a working correlation matrix.

Working Correlation Structures

StructureDescriptionWhen to Usegeepack option
IndependenceAll correlations = 0Baseline; estimates same as standard logisticcorstr="independence"
ExchangeableAll pairs: same correlation ρClustered data, no time orderingcorstr="exchangeable"
AR-1Correlation decays: ρ^|t1−t2|Equally-spaced repeated measurescorstr="ar1"
UnstructuredEach pair has own correlationSmall clusters, sufficient datacorstr="unstructured"
GEE Robustness

GEE estimates are consistent even if the working correlation structure is misspecified, provided the mean model is correct. This is due to the sandwich (robust) variance estimator. However, efficiency is gained when the correct structure is chosen. Minimum ~40–50 clusters recommended for reliable sandwich SE.

Model Selection — QIC

For GEE, AIC is not available. Use QIC (Quasi-likelihood under Independence model Criterion):

\[ \text{QIC} = -2\hat{Q}(\hat{\beta};\,I) + 2\,\text{tr}(\hat{\Omega}_I^{-1}\hat{V}_R) \]

Lower QIC = better model. Compare across correlation structures.

R Code — GEE

library(gee)
library(geepack)

# Data wrangling first
data_glow <- data_glow %>%
  mutate(weight5 = weight / 5,
         frac2   = as.integer(fracture) - 1)

# ── gee package ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
glow_gee_ind <- gee(frac2 ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
                    id = site_id, data = data_glow,
                    family = binomial(link = "logit"),
                    corstr = "independence",
                    scale.fix = TRUE, scale.value = 1,
                    maxiter = 100)

# Results table
data.frame(log_odds   = coef(glow_gee_ind),
           odds_ratio = exp(coef(glow_gee_ind)))

# ── geepack (recommended) — exchangeable ─────────────────────────
glow_gee_exch <- geeglm(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
                        id = site_id, data = data_glow,
                        family = binomial(link = "logit"),
                        corstr = "exchangeable")
summary(glow_gee_exch)
exp(coef(glow_gee_exch))    # OR
exp(confint(glow_gee_exch)) # 95% CI

# ── Model selection: QIC ──────────────────────────────────────────
glow_gee_ind2  <- geeglm(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
                         id = site_id, data = data_glow,
                         family = binomial, corstr = "independence")
glow_gee_ar1   <- geeglm(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
                         id = site_id, data = data_glow,
                         family = binomial, corstr = "ar1")
glow_gee_unstr <- geeglm(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
                         id = site_id, data = data_glow,
                         family = binomial, corstr = "unstructured")

QIC(glow_gee_ind2)
QIC(glow_gee_exch)
QIC(glow_gee_ar1)
QIC(glow_gee_unstr)
# Choose model with lowest QIC

Conditional Models — GLMM (Generalised Linear Mixed Models)

Random Intercept Model

Each cluster i has its own intercept \(b_i\) — a random deviation from the overall intercept \(\beta_0\):

\[ \text{logit}[P(Y_{ij}=1|X_{ij},b_i)] = (\beta_0 + b_i) + \beta_1 X_{ij1} + \cdots + \beta_p X_{ijp} \]

Where \(b_i \sim N(0, \sigma^2_b)\).

Subject-Specific Interpretation

The coefficient \(\beta_1\) represents the change in log-odds for a one-unit increase in \(X_1\) within the same cluster, holding the random effect \(b_i\) constant. This is a cluster-specific (subject-specific) interpretation.

Random Slope Model

The effect of predictor also varies across clusters:

\[ \text{logit}[P(Y_{ij}=1|X_{ij},b_i)] = (\beta_0 + b_{0i}) + (\beta_1 + b_{1i})X_{ij1} + \cdots \]

Where \((b_{0i}, b_{1i}) \sim MVN(\mathbf{0}, \mathbf{D})\).

Estimation Methods

MethodDescriptionR optionAccuracy
LaplaceSecond-order Taylor expansion; defaultnAGQ=1Fast; may be biased for binary
Adaptive Gauss-Hermite (AGQ)Numerical integration; recommended for binarynAGQ=20More accurate; slower
PQLPenalised quasi-likelihood; very fastMASS::glmmPQL()Biased; avoid for binary small N
nAGQ Recommendation

For binary outcomes with small cluster sizes, use nAGQ=20 or higher. The Laplace approximation (nAGQ=1) can produce biased estimates when clusters are small or outcome is rare.

R Code — GLMM

library(lme4)
library(performance)
library(sjPlot)

# ── Random intercept model ────────────────────────────────────────
glow_lme <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                    (1 | site_id),
                  family = binomial,
                  nAGQ   = 20,        # 20 quadrature points (accurate)
                  data   = data_glow)
summary(glow_lme)

# Fixed effects (OR)
fixef(glow_lme)
exp(fixef(glow_lme))
exp(confint(glow_lme, method = "Wald"))

# Random effects (BLUPs per site)
ranef(glow_lme)
VarCorr(glow_lme)   # variance components

# ICC
icc(glow_lme)       # performance package
# Manual:
vc  <- as.numeric(VarCorr(glow_lme)$site_id)
icc_val <- vc / (vc + pi^2/3)
cat("ICC =", round(icc_val, 4))

# ── Three-level model ─────────────────────────────────────────────
glow_3lv <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                    (1 | site_id/phy_id),
                  family = binomial,
                  nAGQ   = 1,
                  data   = data_glow)
summary(glow_3lv)
icc(glow_3lv)

# ── Random slope model ────────────────────────────────────────────
glow_slope <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                      (1 + weight5 | site_id),
                    family = binomial,
                    data   = data_glow)
summary(glow_slope)

# ── Model comparison ──────────────────────────────────────────────
AIC(glow_lme, glow_3lv, glow_slope)
anova(glow_lme, glow_3lv)

# Publication table
tab_model(glow_lme, glow_3lv,
          show.re.var = TRUE,
          show.icc    = TRUE,
          dv.labels   = c("2-level", "3-level"))

estat icc
predict prob_marg, mu fixedonly    // marginal (fixed effects only)
predict re, reffects               // BLUPs

* Three-level model (site / physician)

estat icc

* Random slope

* Old xtlogit, re approach (single-level random effects)

Marginal vs Conditional: The Critical Distinction

CRITICAL

In non-linear models (like logistic regression), the marginal OR ≠ conditional OR. The conditional OR is always larger in absolute terms because it is conditioned on the random effect. This is different from linear models where marginal and conditional coefficients are identical.

FeatureGEE (Marginal)GLMM (Conditional)
InterpretationPopulation-averagedCluster/Subject-specific
OR meaningOR for average subject in populationOR for a given subject at fixed random effect
Random effectsNuisance — integrated outExplicitly modelled and estimated
EstimationQuasi-likelihood (no full distribution needed)Full ML (requires distributional assumption)
Model selectionQICAIC, BIC, LRT
Missing dataRequires MARMore robust under MAR in some settings
PredictionPopulation-levelCluster-specific possible via BLUPs
Min clusters40–50 recommended for robust SEWorks with fewer clusters (with caution)
Software Rgee, geeglmlme4::glmer()
Software Stataxtgeemelogit, xtlogit, re
Software SPSSGENLIN + REPEATEDMIXED / GENLINMIXED

Population Average Interpretation (Lecture Notes)

In the population average model, the estimated odds ratio represents the ratio of odds across the entire population. For example, if \(\hat{\beta}_1 = 0.77\), then \(\exp(0.77) = 2.16\) means that subjects with the exposure have 2.16 times the odds of the outcome compared to those without, on average across all clusters/sites.

Conditional Interpretation (Lecture Notes)

In the conditional model, the estimated OR represents the change in odds for a given individual or cluster, holding the random effect constant. Interpretation: "Among subjects with the same random effect (same cluster), those with the exposure have X times the odds of the outcome."

When to Use Which?
  • Use GEE when the research question concerns population-level effects (public health policy, group comparisons, average treatment effect)
  • Use GLMM when you want to make predictions for specific individuals/clusters or understand between-cluster variability and ICC

Intraclass Correlation Coefficient (ICC)

The ICC quantifies the proportion of total variability attributable to between-cluster differences:

\[ \text{ICC} = \frac{\sigma^2_b}{\sigma^2_b + \pi^2/3} \]

Where \(\sigma^2_b\) = variance of the random intercept and \(\pi^2/3 \approx 3.29\) = variance of the standard logistic distribution.

ICC ValueInterpretationRecommendation
< 0.05Negligible clusteringStandard logistic may be adequate
0.05–0.10Moderate clusteringMultilevel model recommended
> 0.10Strong clusteringMultilevel model strongly needed
> 0.25Very strong clusteringThree-level model may be needed
Always Compute ICC First

Fit the null model (intercept only with random effect) first to determine if multilevel modelling is warranted. If ICC < 0.05, standard logistic regression may be sufficient.

ICC Formula Derivation

The total variance of the latent response is partitioned into between-cluster variance (\(\sigma^2_b\)) and within-cluster variance (assumed to be \(\pi^2/3\) under the logistic distribution):

\[ \sigma^2_{total} = \sigma^2_b + \frac{\pi^2}{3} \]

R Code — ICC

library(lme4)
    library(performance)

    # ── Null model first (no predictors) ─────────────────────────────
    glow_null <- glmer(fracture ~ 1 + (1 | site_id),
                       family = binomial,
                       nAGQ   = 20,
                       data   = data_glow)
    summary(glow_null)

    # Manual ICC
    vc_null <- as.numeric(VarCorr(glow_null)$site_id)
    icc_null <- vc_null / (vc_null + pi^2/3)
    cat("ICC (null model) =", round(icc_null, 4), "
")
    cat("Clustering explains", round(icc_null*100, 1), "% of total variance
")

    # performance package
    icc(glow_null)

    # ── After fitting full model ───────────────────────────────────────
    glow_lme <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                        (1 | site_id),
                      family = binomial, nAGQ = 20,
                      data   = data_glow)
    icc(glow_lme)

    # ── Three-level ICC ───────────────────────────────────────────────
    glow_3lv <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                        (1 | site_id/phy_id),
                      family = binomial, nAGQ = 1,
                      data   = data_glow)
    icc(glow_3lv)   # reports ICC for each level
    performance::variance_decomposition(glow_3lv)

    # sjPlot table with ICC
    tab_model(glow_lme, show.re.var = TRUE, show.icc = TRUE)

estat icc    /* reports rho = ICC */
