Estimation

GEE Estimating Equations

\[ \sum_{i=1}^{n} D_i^T V_i^{-1}(Y_i - \mu_i) = 0 \]

Where \(V_i = A_i^{1/2} R_i(\alpha) A_i^{1/2}\) is the working covariance matrix and \(R_i(\alpha)\) is the working correlation matrix.

GLMM Likelihood

\[ L(\beta, D) = \prod_{i=1}^{n} \int \prod_{j=1}^{n_i} f(y_{ij}|b_i) \cdot f(b_i) \, db_i \]

Since this integral is analytically intractable for binary outcomes, approximation methods (AGQ, Laplace) are used.

Inference

For GEE: sandwich (robust) variance estimator gives valid SEs even under misspecified correlation. For GLMM: Wald tests and LRT for fixed effects; boundary-corrected LRT for random effects.

Boundary Issue for Random Effects

When testing \(H_0: \sigma^2_b = 0\), the null hypothesis is on the boundary of the parameter space. The standard LRT p-value must be halved (mixture of chi-square distributions). This is automatically handled by lrtest in Stata, but must be done manually in R.

R Code — Inference

library(lme4)

# ── LRT: is random effect significant? ───────────────────────────
m_fixed  <- glm(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
                family = binomial, data = data_glow)
m_random <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                    (1 | site_id),
                  family = binomial, nAGQ = 20,
                  data   = data_glow)

# LRT statistic (boundary issue: divide p by 2)
lrt_stat <- as.numeric(-2 * (logLik(m_fixed) - logLik(m_random)))
p_boundary <- pchisq(lrt_stat, df = 1, lower.tail = FALSE) / 2
cat("LRT =", round(lrt_stat, 3),
    " p (boundary-corrected) =", round(p_boundary, 4))

# Confidence intervals
confint(m_random, method = "Wald")     # fast Wald CI
confint(m_random, method = "profile")  # profile CI (accurate)

# ── Inference for fixed effects (Wald) ───────────────────────────
summary(m_random)  # z-values and p-values for fixed effects

# ── Bootstrap CI (most accurate) ─────────────────────────────────
library(boot)
# For random effects: use bootMer()
boot_ci <- bootMer(m_random,
                   FUN   = fixef,
                   nsim  = 1000,
                   type  = "parametric",
                   use.u = FALSE)
boot.ci(boot_ci, index = 2, type = "perc")  # CI for weight5
* LRT in Stata
quietly logit fracture weight5 i.raterisk i.armassist
estimates store m_fixed

melogit fracture weight5 i.raterisk i.armassist || site_id:
estimates store m_random

lrtest m_fixed m_random
* Note: boundary issue — p-value should be halved for variance test

* Wald CI
melogit fracture weight5 i.raterisk i.armassist || site_id:
lincom weight5     /* linear combination with CI */

* Profile CI (Stata does this automatically)
estat summarize
* Not covered in lecture notes for this step

Prediction

Three Types of GLMM Predictions

TypeFormulaR codeIncludes random effect
Random effects only (BLUPs)\(\hat{b}_i\)ranef(model)Random effects only
Cluster-specific\(\hat{\mu}_{ij} = g^{-1}(X_{ij}\hat{\beta} + \hat{b}_i)\)fitted(model)Yes
Marginal (population)\(\hat{\mu}_{ij} = g^{-1}(X_{ij}\hat{\beta})\)predict(model, re.form=NA)No

Model Checking

For GLMMs, standard model checking includes:

  • Residual plots — Pearson and deviance residuals vs fitted values
  • Q-Q plot of random effects — check normality assumption of random effects
  • DHARMa simulation residuals — most reliable for binary GLMMs
  • Influence diagnostics — detect influential clusters
DHARMa Package

DHARMa uses simulation to create uniformly distributed residuals for any GLMM. It is the recommended approach for checking binary GLMMs because standard Pearson residuals are not interpretable for binary outcomes. Look for: uniform distribution in QQ plot, no pattern in residual vs fitted plot.

R Code — Prediction & Model Checking

library(lme4)
library(DHARMa)
library(sjPlot)

# ── GEE predictions (population-level) ───────────────────────────
data_glow$pred_gee <- fitted(glow_gee_exch)
hist(data_glow$pred_gee)

# Manual GEE prediction for new obs
b  <- coef(glow_gee_ind)
lp <- b["(Intercept)"] + b["weight5"]*14 + b["rateriskSame"]*1
cat("Pred prob (GEE):", round(plogis(lp), 4))

# ── GLMM predictions ──────────────────────────────────────────────
# 1. Random effects BLUPs per site
ranef(glow_lme)$site_id

# 2. Cluster-specific (fixed + random)
data_glow$pred_cond <- fitted(glow_lme)

# 3. Marginal (fixed effects only; set random = 0)
data_glow$pred_marg <- predict(glow_lme,
                               re.form = NA,
                               type    = "response")

head(data_glow[, c("fracture","pred_cond","pred_marg")])

# ── Model checking ────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Standard residual plot
plot(glow_lme)

# Q-Q of random effects
qqnorm(unlist(ranef(glow_lme)), main = "Q-Q: Random Effects")
qqline(unlist(ranef(glow_lme)))

# DHARMa (recommended for GLMMs)
sim_res <- simulateResiduals(glow_lme, n = 500)
plot(sim_res)
testDispersion(sim_res)
testZeroInflation(sim_res)

# sjPlot visualisations
plot_model(glow_lme, type = "re")    # random effects caterpillar
plot_model(glow_lme, type = "pred")  # predicted probabilities
plot_model(glow_lme, type = "est",   # coefficient plot
           show.values = TRUE)

Dataset: GLOW_RAND (Global Longitudinal Study of Osteoporosis in Women)

Source: Hosmer, Lemeshow & Sturdivant, Applied Logistic Regression, 3rd ed. (2013), p.323

Structure: 500 observations; 3-level hierarchy

  • Level 1: sub_id — 500 subjects
  • Level 2: phy_id — 124 physicians (cluster sizes 1–13)
  • Level 3: site_id — 6 sites (sizes 24–113)

Outcome: fracture — bone fracture in first year of follow-up (No/Yes)

Predictors: weight5, raterisk, armassist

Full Analysis Workflow

library(tidyverse); library(aplore3)
library(gee); library(geepack); library(lme4)
library(performance); library(sjPlot); library(DHARMa)

# ── Data prep ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
data_glow <- glow_rand %>%
  mutate(weight5 = weight / 5,
         frac2   = as.integer(fracture) - 1)

# Cluster sizes
data_glow %>% count(site_id)

# ── Step 1: ICC from null model ───────────────────────────────────
m_null <- glmer(fracture ~ 1 + (1 | site_id),
                family = binomial, nAGQ = 20,
                data   = data_glow)
icc(m_null)

# ── Step 2: Standard logistic (ignores clustering) ────────────────
m_std <- glm(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
             family = binomial, data = data_glow)
summary(m_std)

# ── Step 3: GEE (independence) ────────────────────────────────────
m_gee <- gee(frac2 ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
             id = site_id, data = data_glow,
             family = binomial(link = "logit"),
             corstr = "independence",
             scale.fix = TRUE, scale.value = 1)

# ── Step 4: GEE (exchangeable, with QIC) ─────────────────────────
m_gee2 <- geeglm(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist,
                 id = site_id, data = data_glow,
                 family = binomial, corstr = "exchangeable")
QIC(m_gee2)

# ── Step 5: GLMM random intercept (site) ─────────────────────────
m_glmm <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                  (1 | site_id),
                family = binomial, nAGQ = 20,
                data   = data_glow)
summary(m_glmm)
exp(fixef(m_glmm))
icc(m_glmm)

# ── Step 6: GLMM three-level (site / physician) ───────────────────
m_glmm3 <- glmer(fracture ~ weight5 + raterisk + armassist +
                   (1 | site_id/phy_id),
                 family = binomial, nAGQ = 1,
                 data   = data_glow)
icc(m_glmm3)

# ── Step 7: Compare ───────────────────────────────────────────────
tab_model(m_glmm, m_glmm3,
          show.re.var = TRUE, show.icc = TRUE,
          dv.labels   = c("2-level", "3-level"))
AIC(m_glmm, m_glmm3)

# ── Step 8: Model checking ────────────────────────────────────────
sim_res <- simulateResiduals(m_glmm, n = 500)
plot(sim_res)
plot_model(m_glmm, type = "re")

Research Question — Lung Cancer Remission

Source: Dr Hazlienor Mohd Hatta, DrPH Sem 2

Researchers want to know what patient and physician factors are most related to whether a patient's lung cancer goes into remission after treatment, as part of a larger study of treatment outcomes and quality of life in patients with lung cancer.

Data: 8,525 patients nested within doctors (DID), nested within hospitals (HID)

Outcome: remission (0/1)

Key predictors: tumorsize, IL6, CRP, lungcapacity, CancerStage

Full R Workflow

# Source: Dr Hazlienor Mohd Hatta, DrPH Sem 2

library(tidyverse); library(aplore3)
library(gee); library(lme4); library(sjPlot)
library(summarytools); library(broom.mixed)
library(gtsummary); library(reshape2)
library(performance); library(DHARMa)

# ── Load cancer dataset ───────────────────────────────────────────
cancer <- read.csv("https://stats.idre.ucla.edu/stat/data/hdp.csv")

# Data structure: 8525 patients, nested within doctors (DID),
# nested within hospitals (HID)
str(cancer)

# Outcome: remission (0/1)
table(cancer$remission)

# ── Data wrangling ────────────────────────────────────────────────
cancer <- cancer %>%
  mutate(remission    = factor(remission),
         famhistory   = factor(famhistory),
         CancerStage  = factor(CancerStage))

# ── Descriptive analysis ──────────────────────────────────────────
dfSummary(cancer)
cancer %>% tbl_summary(include = c(tumorsize, IL6, CRP, remission))

# ── Marginal model (GEE) ──────────────────────────────────────────
cancer_gee <- gee(as.numeric(remission) - 1 ~ tumorsize + IL6 + CRP,
                  id = DID, data = cancer,
                  family = binomial(link = "logit"),
                  corstr = "exchangeable")
summary(cancer_gee)
exp(coef(cancer_gee))

# ── Mixed Effect Logistic Regression (GLMM) ───────────────────────
# Justification: patients nested within doctors; expect clustering

# Null model ICC first
m_null <- glmer(remission ~ 1 + (1 | DID),
                family = binomial, data = cancer)
icc(m_null)

# Random intercept model
cancer_glmm <- glmer(remission ~ tumorsize + IL6 + CRP +
                       (1 | DID),
                     family = binomial, nAGQ = 1,
                     data = cancer)
summary(cancer_glmm)
icc(cancer_glmm)

# Inference: publication table
tbl_regression(cancer_glmm, exponentiate = TRUE) %>%
  bold_labels() %>% bold_p()

# Prediction
cancer$pred_prob <- predict(cancer_glmm, type = "response")

# Model fitness
sim_res <- simulateResiduals(cancer_glmm, n = 500)
plot(sim_res)

# Forest plot
plot_model(cancer_glmm, type = "est", show.values = TRUE,
           title = "Mixed Effect Logistic: Lung Cancer Remission")

Dataset: Toenail

Study: Randomised clinical trial — two antifungal treatments for toenail dermatophyte onychomycosis

Structure: 1,908 observations; 294 patients; up to 7 repeated measurements over 18 months

Variables:

  • ID — patient ID
  • Y — onycholysis presence (1=yes, 0=no)
  • treatment — treatment group (1 or 2)
  • month — time of measurement (0–18 months)
  • visit — visit number (1–7)

Research question: Is there a difference between the two antifungal treatments in the probability of onycholysis over time?

R Code — Toenail Analysis

library(haven); library(geepack); library(lme4)
library(performance); library(sjPlot)

# ── Load toenail data ─────────────────────────────────────────────
toenail <- read_dta("toenail.dta")
# Variables: ID, Y (onycholysis 0/1), treatment (1/2),
#            month (0–18), visit (1–7)

# Data structure
head(toenail)
toenail %>% count(ID) %>% summary()     # obs per subject
toenail %>% count(visit)                # obs per visit

# ── ICC from null model ───────────────────────────────────────────
m_null <- glmer(Y ~ 1 + (1 | ID),
                family = binomial, nAGQ = 10,
                data   = toenail)
icc(m_null)

# ── GEE approach ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
toe_gee <- geeglm(Y ~ treatment + month + treatment:month,
                  id     = ID, data = toenail,
                  family = binomial(link = "logit"),
                  corstr = "exchangeable")
summary(toe_gee)
exp(coef(toe_gee))
QIC(toe_gee)

# ── GLMM approach ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
toe_lme <- glmer(Y ~ treatment + month + treatment:month +
                   (1 | ID),
                 family = binomial, nAGQ = 10,
                 data   = toenail)
summary(toe_lme)
exp(fixef(toe_lme))

# ICC
vc_toe <- as.numeric(VarCorr(toe_lme)$ID)
icc_toe <- vc_toe / (vc_toe + pi^2/3)
cat("ICC (toenail) =", round(icc_toe, 3))

# Publication table
tab_model(toe_lme, show.re.var = TRUE, show.icc = TRUE)

      i(ID) family(binomial) link(logit) ///
      corr(exchangeable) eform

* GLMM
melogit Y i.treatment month i.treatment#c.month || ID:
estat icc

GET502 Assignment 1 — Key Questions

Assignment Context (GET502)

The assignment uses the GLOW_RAND dataset and asks students to:

  1. Examine data structure and determine if multilevel modelling is needed (ICC check)
  2. Fit an appropriate GEE model and interpret the results
  3. Fit an appropriate GLMM and interpret the results
  4. Compare GEE vs GLMM estimates and explain the differences
  5. Draw conclusions about factors associated with bone fracture
Assignment Guidance
  • Always start by examining the data structure and cluster sizes
  • Report ICC from the null model first to justify multilevel approach
  • Use nAGQ=20 for binary outcomes with small clusters
  • For GEE: try both independence and exchangeable; compare with QIC
  • The conditional OR (GLMM) will be larger than the marginal OR (GEE) — explain why
  • Interpret ORs in plain clinical language

Summary & Key Points

ConceptKey Point
When to useBinary outcome with clustered/repeated data — independence assumption violated
GEEPopulation-average; quasi-likelihood; robust SE; QIC for selection
GLMMSubject-specific; ML estimation; models between-cluster variance; AIC/LRT
ICC formula\(\sigma^2_b / (\sigma^2_b + \pi^2/3)\)
ICC ruleICC < 0.05 → standard logistic may suffice; > 0.05 → multilevel needed
OR comparisonConditional OR (GLMM) always larger in magnitude than Marginal OR (GEE)
Working correlationGEE robust to misspecification; exchangeable most common for clusters
nAGQUse 20 for binary GLMM; Laplace (nAGQ=1) may be biased
Data formatLONG format required for all GEE/GLMM packages
Reshapingpivot_longer() (tidyr) or melt() (reshape2) for wide→long
R packagesgee/geepack for GEE; lme4 for GLMM; DHARMa for diagnostics; performance for ICC
Stataxtgee for GEE; melogit / xtlogit,re for GLMM
SPSSGENLIN + REPEATED subcommand for GEE; GENLINMIXED for GLMM
DatasetsGLOW_RAND (3-level); toenail (repeated measures RCT)
Decision Flow
  1. Is data clustered or repeated? Yes → continue
  2. Compute ICC from null model (intercept only + random effect)
  3. ICC < 0.05 → standard logistic may be adequate
  4. ICC ≥ 0.05 → multilevel model recommended
  5. Research question = population effect? → GEE
  6. Research question = individual/cluster effect? → GLMM
  7. Data in wide format? → reshape to long first
  8. Model check: DHARMa residuals + Q-Q of random effects
Topic 06 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Survival Analysis — Stratified Cox

Cox PH Model · PH Assumption Testing · Stratified Cox · Time-Varying Covariates · Schoenfeld Residuals

Learning Objectives — Stratified Cox Survival Analysis

  1. Review the Cox Proportional Hazards (PH) model and the PH assumption
  2. Test the PH assumption using Schoenfeld residuals (cox.zph()) and graphical methods
  3. Apply stratified Cox regression when the PH assumption is violated for a covariate
  4. Understand the role of time-varying covariates and how to restructure data in counting process style
  5. Interpret hazard ratios from stratified Cox and time-varying Cox models
  6. Implement analyses in R (survival, survminer) and Stata (stcox, stphtest)

Why Stratified Cox?

The standard Cox Proportional Hazards model assumes a single baseline hazard shared by all subjects, with covariates multiplying that hazard by a constant factor over time (proportional hazards). When this assumption fails for a covariate, two main remedies exist:

RemedyWhen to UseTrade-off
Stratified CoxCovariate violates PH; you do not need to estimate its HRCannot estimate effect of stratifying variable
Time-Varying Covariate/CoefficientCovariate violates PH; you want its effect over timeMore complex data structure (counting process)
Functional form changeNon-linearity suspected (e.g., add X²)Trial and error; may still violate PH
Reference

Cox DR. "Regression Models and Life-Tables." J R Statist Soc B 1972;34:187–220. — The foundational paper for the Cox PH model.
Grambsch PM, Therneau TM. "Proportional hazards tests and diagnostics based on weighted residuals." Biometrika 1994;81:515–526. — The statistical basis for cox.zph().
Zhang Z, et al. "Time-varying covariates and coefficients in Cox regression models." Ann Transl Med 2018;6(7):121. PMCID: PMC6015946.

Dataset Used in This Topic

lung dataset (R survival package)

Source: NCCTG Lung Cancer Trial; North Central Cancer Treatment Group

Structure: 228 patients with advanced lung cancer

Variables: time (survival days), status (2=dead, 1=censored), age, sex (1=Male, 2=Female), ph.ecog (ECOG score), ph.karno (Karnofsky score rated by physician), pat.karno (patient-rated), meal.cal, wt.loss

Cox Proportional Hazards Model — Recap

The Hazard Function

The hazard function \(h(t)\) is the instantaneous risk of experiencing the event at time \(t\), given survival up to \(t\):

\[ h(t) = \lim_{\Delta t o 0} \frac{P(t \leq T < t+\Delta t \mid T \geq t)}{\Delta t} \]

Cox PH Model Formula

\[ h(t \mid X) = h_0(t) \cdot \exp(\beta_1 X_1 + \beta_2 X_2 + \cdots + \beta_p X_p) \]

Where:

  • \(h_0(t)\) = unspecified baseline hazard (non-parametric; cancels out in partial likelihood)
  • \(\exp(\beta_k)\) = Hazard Ratio (HR) for a one-unit increase in \(X_k\)
  • The model is semi-parametric: no distributional assumption for \(h_0(t)\)

Hazard Ratio Interpretation

HR ValueInterpretation
HR = 1.0No difference in hazard between groups
HR > 1.0Higher hazard (shorter survival) in exposed group
HR < 1.0Lower hazard (longer survival; protective) in exposed group

Key Assumptions of the Standard Cox Model

Three Assumptions to Always Verify
  1. Proportional Hazards (PH): The HR between any two subjects is constant over time — the hazard curves are proportional, never crossing
  2. Time-fixed covariates: Covariate values do not change over the follow-up period
  3. Log-linearity: Continuous predictors have a linear relationship with \(\log h(t)\)

Partial Likelihood Estimation

Cox proposed estimating \(\beta\) via partial likelihood, eliminating \(h_0(t)\):

\[ L(\beta) = \prod_{i: \delta_i=1} \frac{\exp(X_i \beta)}{\sum_{j \in R(t_i)} \exp(X_j \beta)} \]

Where \(R(t_i)\) is the risk set at time \(t_i\) (all subjects still event-free just before \(t_i\)) and \(\delta_i = 1\) if subject \(i\) experienced the event.

Basic Cox in R, Stata, SPSS

library(survival)
library(survminer)

# Load data
data(lung)
lung$status2 <- ifelse(lung$status == 2, 1, 0)  # recode: 1=dead, 0=censored

# Basic Cox model
res.cox <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno + wt.loss,
                 data = lung)
summary(res.cox)

# Extract HR with 95% CI
exp(cbind(HR = coef(res.cox), confint(res.cox)))

# Model fit statistics
extractAIC(res.cox)   # AIC
logLik(res.cox)       # log-likelihood
* Stata: Basic Cox regression
stset time, failure(status==2)

stcox age sex ph_karno wt_loss
stcox age sex ph_karno wt_loss, hr       /* display HRs */

* Forest plot of HRs
coefplot, eform base                     /* requires coefplot */
* SPSS: Cox Regression
COXREG time
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /METHOD=ENTER age sex ph_karno wt_loss
  /PRINT=CI(95)
  /CRITERIA=PIN(.05) POUT(.10) ITERATE(20).

The Proportional Hazards Assumption

What Does PH Mean?

For two subjects \(i\) and \(j\) with covariate vectors \(X_i\) and \(X_j\), the PH assumption requires:

\[ \frac{h(t \mid X_i)}{h(t \mid X_j)} = \frac{h_0(t)\exp(X_i\beta)}{h_0(t)\exp(X_j\beta)} = \exp\{(X_i - X_j)\beta\} = ext{constant for all } t \]

The hazard ratio is constant over time — independent of \(t\). This means the baseline hazard \(h_0(t)\) cancels out and the hazard ratio depends only on the covariate values, not when during follow-up they are observed.

When PH Fails

PH is violated when a covariate's effect on the hazard changes over time. For example: a treatment that is effective early but wanes over time; sex having different mortality effects in younger vs older age; surgery having high short-term risk but long-term benefit.

Graphical Intuition

PH HoldsPH Violated
Survival curves for groups do not crossSurvival curves cross at some point
log(−log(S(t))) vs log(t) curves are parallellog(−log(S(t))) curves converge, cross, or diverge
Schoenfeld residuals show no trend over timeSchoenfeld residuals show a systematic slope with time
HR plot is flat across timeHR changes over time (increases or decreases)

Why Does It Matter?

If PH is violated and you ignore it, the estimated HR is a weighted average of the true time-varying HRs, which may be meaningless or misleading. Confidence intervals will be incorrect. The model will be misspecified.

Analogy

Think of PH like assuming the speed ratio of two cars remains constant over the entire race. If Car A is faster at the start but slows down, using a single constant ratio misleads you about who will win.

Testing the Proportional Hazards Assumption

Method 1: Schoenfeld Residuals — cox.zph()

Schoenfeld residuals are defined for each covariate and each event time. For the \(k\)-th covariate at event time \(t_i\):

\[ r_{ik} = X_{ik} - \frac{\sum_{j \in R(t_i)} X_{jk} \exp(X_j \hat\beta)}{\sum_{j \in R(t_i)} \exp(X_j \hat\beta)} \]

This is the observed covariate value minus its expected value under the model. Under PH, Schoenfeld residuals are independent of time. The cox.zph() function tests this by correlating scaled Schoenfeld residuals with a function of time:

  • H\(_0\): No correlation between residuals and time (PH holds) → p > 0.05 → accept PH
  • H\(_1\): Significant correlation (PH violated) → p < 0.05 → reject PH
Interpreting cox.zph() Output

The output gives: rho (correlation between residuals and time), chisq (test statistic), p (p-value). A significant p-value per covariate means that variable violates PH. The GLOBAL row tests the overall model.

Method 2: Log-Minus-Log (LML) Plot

For a categorical predictor, plot \(\log(-\log(\hat{S}(t)))\) vs \(\log(t)\) for each group. Under PH, the curves will be roughly parallel. Crossing or converging curves indicate PH violation.

LML vs Schoenfeld

The LML plot is informal/graphical — useful for detection but subjective. Schoenfeld residual test (cox.zph()) is the formal statistical test. Use both together.

Method 3: Time Interaction Term

Add an interaction of the covariate with time (or log time) to the model. A significant interaction term indicates PH violation:

\[ h(t \mid X) = h_0(t)\exp(\beta X + \gamma X \cdot g(t)) \]

If \(\hat\gamma\) is significant, the effect of \(X\) changes with time.

R / Stata / SPSS Code — Testing PH

library(survival); library(survminer)
data(lung)
lung$status2 <- ifelse(lung$status == 2, 1, 0)

res.cox <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno + wt.loss, data = lung)

# ── METHOD 1: Schoenfeld residual test ────────────────────────────
test.ph <- cox.zph(res.cox)
print(test.ph)
# Output: rho, chisq, p for each covariate + GLOBAL row
# p < 0.05 → PH violated for that covariate

# Plot Schoenfeld residuals (flat line = PH holds)
ggcoxzph(test.ph)
# Solid line = smoothing spline; dashed = ±2 SE band
# Systematic non-horizontal trend → PH violated

# ── METHOD 2: Log-Minus-Log plot ─────────────────────────────────
# For categorical variable (sex)
km_sex <- survfit(Surv(time, status2) ~ sex, data = lung)
plot(km_sex, fun = "cloglog", col = c("blue","red"),
     xlab = "log(Time)", ylab = "log(-log(S(t)))",
     main = "Log-Minus-Log Plot by Sex")
legend("bottomright", c("Male","Female"), col = c("blue","red"), lty = 1)
# Parallel lines → PH holds; crossing/converging → PH violated

# survminer version
ggsurvplot(km_sex, fun = "cloglog", palette = c("#E7B800","#2E9FDF"),
           ggtheme = theme_bw(), legend.labs = c("Male","Female"))

# ── METHOD 3: Time interaction test ──────────────────────────────
# Add X * log(time) interaction to test PH for ph.karno
lung$log_time <- log(lung$time)
cox_int <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno + wt.loss +
                   ph.karno:log_time, data = lung)
summary(cox_int)
# Significant ph.karno:log_time → ph.karno violates PH
* Stata: Testing PH assumption
stset time, failure(status==2)
stcox age sex ph_karno wt_loss

* METHOD 1: Schoenfeld residual test
estat phtest, detail        /* global + per-variable test */
* OR
stphtest, detail            /* older command */
* p < 0.05 → PH violated

* METHOD 2: Log-minus-log plot (for categorical: sex)
stphplot, by(sex) noshow
* Parallel lines → PH holds

* METHOD 3: Time interaction (scaled Schoenfeld residual plot)
stcoxkm, by(sex)            /* KM vs predicted Cox curves */

* Alternative: add time interaction manually
gen ph_karno_t = ph_karno * _t
stcox age sex ph_karno wt_loss ph_karno_t
* Test ph_karno_t coefficient
* SPSS: Testing PH assumption
* Option 1: Time-dependent covariate test (via T_COV_)
COXREG time
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /METHOD=ENTER age sex ph_karno wt_loss
  /STRATA=
  /PLOT SURVIVAL HAZARD OMS LOGLOGLML
  /PRINT=CI(95)
  /CRITERIA=PIN(.05) POUT(.10) ITERATE(20).

* Log-Minus-Log: In Cox Regression dialog:
* Plots → Log minus log → separate line for each value of the factor

* Time-dependent covariate (manual)
COMPUTE Tph_karno = T_ * ph_karno.
