Stratified Cox Proportional Hazards Model

The Model

When a variable \(Z\) (e.g., sex, hospital, tumour type) violates PH, we stratify the Cox model by \(Z\). Each stratum \(g\) gets its own baseline hazard \(h_{0g}(t)\), while the regression coefficients \(\beta\) are assumed to be the same across all strata:

\[ h_g(t \mid X) = h_{0g}(t) \cdot \exp(\beta_1 X_1 + \beta_2 X_2 + \cdots + \beta_p X_p) \quad g = 1, 2, \ldots, G \]

The partial likelihood is formed by multiplying across strata:

\[ L(\beta) = \prod_{g=1}^{G} L_g(\beta) \]

Key Properties of the Stratified Cox Model

FeatureStandard CoxStratified Cox
Baseline hazardSingle \(h_0(t)\) for allSeparate \(h_{0g}(t)\) per stratum — can be completely different
Regression coefficientsCommon \(\beta\)Common \(\beta\) (assumed same across strata)
Survival curvesOne setDifferent curve per stratum
HR of stratifying variableEstimatedNot estimable — absorbed into baseline hazard
Effect of other covariatesPopulation-wideStratum-adjusted (confounding controlled)
CRITICAL: The Trade-off

By stratifying on \(Z\), you cannot estimate or test the effect of \(Z\) itself. Stratification is the right choice when \(Z\) is a nuisance confounder that you do not need to quantify. If \(Z\) is a key exposure of interest, consider time-varying coefficients instead.

When to Use Stratification

Criteria for Stratifying a Variable
  • The variable violates PH (confirmed by cox.zph())
  • The variable is a confounder/nuisance — you want to control for it, not estimate its HR
  • The variable is categorical with a small number of levels (otherwise too many strata)
  • Each stratum must have enough events to estimate the baseline hazard reliably

No-Interaction Assumption

The stratified Cox model assumes that the coefficients \(\beta\) are the same in each stratum (no-interaction assumption). This should be tested:

\[ H_0: \beta_1^{(1)} = \beta_1^{(2)} = \cdots = \beta_1^{(G)} \quad ext{(equal slopes across strata)} \]

Test by fitting models with and without an interaction between the stratifying variable and the covariates, then compare via LRT.

R / Stata / SPSS Code — Stratified Cox

library(survival); library(survminer)
data(lung)
lung$status2 <- ifelse(lung$status == 2, 1, 0)
lung$sex <- factor(lung$sex, labels = c("Male","Female"))

# ── STEP 1: Identify violating variable via cox.zph() ─────────────
res.cox <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno + wt.loss, data = lung)
test.ph <- cox.zph(res.cox)
print(test.ph)
# Suppose sex violates PH (p < 0.05)

# ── STEP 2: Fit stratified Cox model (stratify on sex) ────────────
res.strat <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + ph.karno + wt.loss +
                     strata(sex),       # sex absorbed as strata
                   data = lung)
summary(res.strat)
# sex is NOT in the output — no HR for sex
# HR for age, ph.karno, wt.loss are strata-adjusted

# ── STEP 3: Verify PH is now satisfied ───────────────────────────
test.ph2 <- cox.zph(res.strat)
print(test.ph2)   # sex should no longer appear

# ── STEP 4: Plot stratum-specific survival curves ─────────────────
ggsurvplot(survfit(res.strat), data = lung,
           palette = c("#E7B800","#2E9FDF"),
           ggtheme = theme_bw(),
           title = "Stratified Cox — Survival by Sex")

# ── STEP 5: Test no-interaction assumption ────────────────────────
# No-interaction model (common beta)
res.noint <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + ph.karno + wt.loss +
                     strata(sex), data = lung)
# Interaction model (sex-specific beta)
res.int <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age * sex + ph.karno * sex +
                   wt.loss * sex, data = lung)
# LRT
anova(res.noint, res.int)
# Non-significant → no-interaction assumption holds → stratified model valid
* Stata: Stratified Cox
stset time, failure(status==2)

* STEP 1: Check PH
stcox age i.sex ph_karno wt_loss
estat phtest, detail

* STEP 2: Stratified Cox (stratify on sex)
stcox age ph_karno wt_loss, strata(sex)
* sex coefficient not shown — absorbed as strata

* STEP 3: Verify PH of remaining variables
estat phtest, detail

* STEP 4: Stratum-specific survival curves
stcurve, survival at1(sex=1) at2(sex=2)

* STEP 5: Test no-interaction assumption
stcox age ph_karno wt_loss, strata(sex)               /* no interaction */
estimates store m1
stcox c.age##i.sex c.ph_karno##i.sex c.wt_loss##i.sex /* with interaction */
estimates store m2
lrtest m1 m2    /* non-sig → no interaction → stratified model OK */
* SPSS: Stratified Cox
COXREG time
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /STRATA=sex             /* sex used as stratification variable */
  /METHOD=ENTER age ph_karno wt_loss
  /PRINT=CI(95)
  /CRITERIA=PIN(.05) POUT(.10) ITERATE(20).

* sex is removed from the predictor list — absorbed in strata
* Separate baseline hazard estimated for each sex group

Interpretation of Stratified Cox Output

How to Write Up Results

"After confirming that sex violated the proportional hazards assumption (Schoenfeld residual test, p = 0.02), a stratified Cox regression was performed with sex as the stratifying variable. A separate baseline hazard was estimated for males and females. The hazard ratio for ph.karno [HR = 0.98, 95% CI: 0.97–0.99] was controlled for age, weight loss, and sex stratum."

Time-Varying Covariates in Cox Regression

Types of Time-Varying Covariates

TypeDefinitionExamplesModelling Approach
External (Exogenous)Path not affected by subject survival status; pre-determined or exogenous to the individualAge, calendar time, air pollution index, drug dose changes by protocolTime-dependent Cox model with counting process data
Internal (Endogenous)Generated by the subject; observed only while the subject is alive; affected by survival statusCRP levels, tumour markers, CD4 count in HIV, blood pressure during follow-upTime-dependent Cox (cautious interpretation) or Joint modelling
Immortal Time Bias

A common pitfall with time-varying covariates is immortal time bias — when follow-up time during which the event cannot occur (e.g., before treatment starts) is misclassified as exposed time. Using the counting process data structure (start, stop) prevents this.

Counting Process Data Structure

For time-varying covariates, data must be in counting process (start, stop) format: each subject may have multiple rows, with each row representing a time interval during which the covariate value is fixed:

IDstartstopeventcrp (time-varying)sex (fixed)
10300120Male
13090085Male
1901801210Male
2045095Female
2451201140Female

The Surv(start, stop, event) syntax in R handles this. The partial likelihood uses at most one row per subject at any time point (intervals do not overlap).

Time-Varying Coefficients

When the PH assumption is violated, the effect of a covariate changes over time. This can be modelled as a time-varying coefficient:

\[ h(t \mid X) = h_0(t) \exp\{\beta(t) \cdot X\} \]

Two approaches to model \(\beta(t)\):

  • Step function: Use survSplit() to split follow-up into time intervals and stratify by interval — HR is estimated separately per interval
  • Parametric function: Specify \(g(t) = \log(t+c)\) or similar — use tt() argument in coxph()

R / Stata / SPSS Code — Time-Varying Covariates

library(survival)
data(lung)
lung$status2 <- ifelse(lung$status == 2, 1, 0)

# ── APPROACH 1: Time-varying coefficient (step function) ──────────
# Split follow-up at 180 and 350 days
lung_split <- survSplit(Surv(time, status2) ~ .,
                        data   = lung,
                        cut    = c(180, 350),
                        episode = "tgroup")

# Fit Cox stratified by time group (estimates period-specific HRs)
fit_tv <- coxph(Surv(tstart, time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno:strata(tgroup),
                data = lung_split)
summary(fit_tv)
# ph.karno has different HR in each time window

# Verify PH of the stratified model
cox.zph(fit_tv)

# ── APPROACH 2: Parametric time function with tt() ────────────────
# tt(ph.karno) = ph.karno * log(t + 20)
fit_tt <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno + tt(ph.karno),
                data = lung,
                tt = function(x, t, ...) x * log(t + 20))
summary(fit_tt)
# tt(ph.karno) significant → effect varies with log(t+20)
# Estimated effect: beta(t) = coef[ph.karno] + coef[tt(ph.karno)] * log(t+20)

# ── APPROACH 3: True time-varying covariate (tmerge) ─────────────
# Example: lab value (crp) measured at each visit
# Baseline data (one row per subject)
df_base <- data.frame(
  id     = 1:5,
  time   = c(365, 180, 540, 90, 720),
  status = c(1, 0, 1, 1, 0),
  age    = c(60, 55, 70, 45, 65)
)

# Time-varying lab values (multiple rows per subject)
df_labs <- data.frame(
  id    = c(1,1,2,3,3,3,4,5,5),
  t_lab = c(0,90,0,0,90,180,0,0,180),
  crp   = c(120,85,95,110,95,210,140,80,100)
)

# Merge into counting process form
df_cp <- tmerge(df_base, df_base, id = id,
                endpt = event(time, status))
df_cp <- tmerge(df_cp, df_labs, id = id,
                crp = tdc(t_lab, crp))

# Fit Cox with time-varying crp
fit_tvc <- coxph(Surv(tstart, tstop, endpt) ~ age + crp,
                 data = df_cp)
summary(fit_tvc)
# HR for crp = effect of a 1-unit increase in crp at any time point
* Stata: Time-varying covariates

* ── APPROACH 1: Step function (split at 180 and 350 days) ──────────
stsplit tgroup, at(0 180 350)   /* creates time groups */
stcox age sex ph_karno#i.tgroup /* stratum-specific effect of ph_karno */

* ── APPROACH 2: tt() equivalent — time interaction ────────────────
gen ph_karno_lnt = ph_karno * log(_t + 20)
stcox age sex ph_karno ph_karno_lnt
* Significant ph_karno_lnt → time-varying effect

* ── APPROACH 3: Counting process format for true TVC ──────────────
* After stset, expand data to long format:
* Each row = one time interval with fixed covariate values
* stset tstart tstop, id(id) failure(status==1)
* stcox age crp
* SPSS: Time-varying covariates via T_COV_
* SPSS uses T_ as the time variable during Cox regression

COXREG time
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /METHOD=ENTER age sex
  /TIME PROGRAM
    COMPUTE Tph = T_ * ph_karno.
  /VARIABLES=Tph
  /PRINT=CI(95).

* T_ is evaluated at each event time
* Tph = time-interaction; significant result means time-varying effect
* Note: true TVC with repeated measurements requires data restructuring
* and is better handled in R/Stata for complex data structures.

Residuals and Model Diagnostics for Cox Models

Residual TypePurposeR FunctionWhat to Look For
SchoenfeldTest PH assumptioncox.zph(fit)No trend over time (horizontal band)
MartingaleAssess functional form of continuous covariates; outliersresiduals(fit, type="martingale")LOWESS line should be flat (linear relationship)
DevianceDetect outliers / poorly predicted observationsresiduals(fit, type="deviance")Should be ~symmetric around 0; |residual| > 2 = potential outlier
dfbetaIdentify influential observationsresiduals(fit, type="dfbeta")Large values relative to coefficient = influential point
ScoreCheck overall model fitresiduals(fit, type="score")Should be centred around 0

Martingale Residuals for Functional Form

The martingale residual for subject \(i\) is:

\[ M_i = \delta_i - \hat\Lambda_0(t_i)\exp(X_i\hat\beta) \]

Where \(\delta_i\) = event indicator and \(\hat\Lambda_0(t_i)\) = estimated cumulative baseline hazard. Martingale residuals range from \(-\infty\) to +1. Plotting against a continuous covariate (e.g., age) reveals whether the functional form is correct.

Deviance Residuals for Outliers

A symmetric transformation of martingale residuals:

\[ d_i = ext{sign}(M_i)\sqrt{-2[M_i + \delta_i \log(\delta_i - M_i)]} \]

Should be roughly symmetrically distributed around 0 with SD ≈ 1. Values > |2| suggest poor model fit for that subject.

R / Stata Code — Diagnostics

library(survival); library(survminer)
data(lung)
lung$status2 <- ifelse(lung$status == 2, 1, 0)
res.cox <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno + wt.loss, data = lung)

# ── 1. Schoenfeld residuals (PH test) ─────────────────────────────
test.ph <- cox.zph(res.cox)
ggcoxzph(test.ph)   # survminer plot

# ── 2. Martingale residuals (functional form) ─────────────────────
mart_resid <- residuals(res.cox, type = "martingale")

# Plot against age to check linearity
plot(lung$age, mart_resid,
     xlab = "Age", ylab = "Martingale Residuals",
     main = "Martingale Residuals vs Age")
abline(h = 0, col = "red")
lines(lowess(lung$age[!is.na(lung$age)], mart_resid[!is.na(lung$age)]),
      col = "blue", lwd = 2)
# Flat blue line → linear OK; curved → try log(age), age^2

# survminer version
ggcoxfunctional(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + log(age) + sqrt(age),
                data = lung)

# ── 3. Deviance residuals (outliers) ──────────────────────────────
ggcoxdiagnostics(res.cox, type = "deviance",
                 linear.predictions = FALSE, ggtheme = theme_bw())
# Points beyond ±2 = poorly predicted observations

# ── 4. dfbeta (influential observations) ──────────────────────────
ggcoxdiagnostics(res.cox, type = "dfbeta",
                 linear.predictions = FALSE, ggtheme = theme_bw())
# Large dfbeta relative to coefficient = influential subject
* Stata: Cox model diagnostics
stset time, failure(status==2)
stcox age i.sex ph_karno wt_loss

* 1. Schoenfeld residuals (PH test)
estat phtest, detail   /* formal test */
stphplot, by(sex)      /* graphical: log-minus-log */

* 2. Martingale residuals
predict mart_r, mgale
scatter mart_r age, yline(0) || lowess mart_r age
* Flat LOWESS line → linear OK

* 3. Deviance residuals
predict dev_r, deviance
scatter dev_r _t, yline(0) yline(2) yline(-2)
* Points beyond ±2 = possible outliers

* 4. dfbeta (influential obs)
predict dfb_age, dfbeta(age)
scatter dfb_age _t, yline(0)
* Large values → influential subject for age coefficient
* SPSS: Cox model diagnostics — limited options
COXREG time
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /METHOD=ENTER age sex ph_karno wt_loss
  /SAVE=RESID SRESID DFBETA
  /PRINT=CI(95).

* RESID = partial residuals
* SRESID = standardised residuals (deviance-like)
* DFBETA = influence statistics per variable
* Use EXAMINE or CHART to plot saved residuals
Full Workflow — NCCTG Lung Cancer Dataset

Research Question: What factors predict survival in patients with advanced lung cancer, controlling for violation of the proportional hazards assumption?

Dataset: lung from R survival package — 228 patients, 165 deaths

Outcome: time (days), status (2=dead)

Covariates: age, sex, ph.karno, wt.loss

library(survival); library(survminer)
data(lung)

# ── STEP 0: Data preparation ──────────────────────────────────────
lung$status2 <- ifelse(lung$status == 2, 1, 0)
lung$sex     <- factor(lung$sex, labels = c("Male","Female"))
lung_cc      <- lung[complete.cases(lung[,c("time","status2","age","sex",
                                            "ph.karno","wt.loss")]),]
nrow(lung_cc); sum(lung_cc$status2)

# ── STEP 1: Kaplan-Meier overview ────────────────────────────────
km_all <- survfit(Surv(time, status2) ~ 1, data = lung_cc)
ggsurvplot(km_all, data = lung_cc, risk.table = TRUE,
           palette = "#2E9FDF",
           title   = "Overall Survival — Lung Cancer",
           xlab    = "Days", surv.median.line = "hv")

km_sex <- survfit(Surv(time, status2) ~ sex, data = lung_cc)
ggsurvplot(km_sex, data = lung_cc, risk.table = TRUE,
           pval = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE,
           palette = c("#E7B800","#2E9FDF"),
           legend.labs = c("Male","Female"))

# ── STEP 2: Standard Cox model ────────────────────────────────────
res.cox <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + sex + ph.karno + wt.loss,
                 data = lung_cc)
summary(res.cox)

# HR table
round(exp(cbind(HR=coef(res.cox), confint(res.cox))), 3)

# ── STEP 3: Test PH assumption ────────────────────────────────────
ph_test <- cox.zph(res.cox)
print(ph_test)
# Check which variable has p < 0.05

# Plot scaled Schoenfeld residuals
ggcoxzph(ph_test)

# LML plot for sex
ggsurvplot(km_sex, fun = "cloglog",
           palette = c("#E7B800","#2E9FDF"),
           title   = "Log-Minus-Log Plot: Sex")

# ── STEP 4: Fit stratified Cox (sex violates PH) ─────────────────
res.strat <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + ph.karno + wt.loss +
                     strata(sex),
                   data = lung_cc)
summary(res.strat)

# Verify PH now satisfied
cox.zph(res.strat)

# ── STEP 5: Check no-interaction assumption ───────────────────────
res.int <- coxph(Surv(time, status2) ~ age * sex + ph.karno * sex +
                   wt.loss * sex, data = lung_cc)
anova(res.strat, res.int)   # p > 0.05 → no-interaction holds

# ── STEP 6: Address ph.karno time-varying effect ─────────────────
# If ph.karno also violates PH, model as time-varying coefficient
lung_split <- survSplit(Surv(time, status2) ~ ., data = lung_cc,
                        cut = c(180, 350), episode = "tgroup")

res.tv <- coxph(Surv(tstart, time, status2) ~ age + wt.loss +
                  strata(sex) + ph.karno:strata(tgroup),
                data = lung_split)
summary(res.tv)
# Separate HR for ph.karno in each time window

# ── STEP 7: Final model diagnostics ──────────────────────────────
# Deviance residuals
ggcoxdiagnostics(res.strat, type = "deviance", ggtheme = theme_bw())

# Martingale — check functional form of age
ggcoxfunctional(Surv(time, status2) ~ age + log(age), data = lung_cc)

# ── STEP 8: Survival prediction from stratified model ────────────
new_data <- data.frame(age = c(55, 70),
                       ph.karno = c(80, 60),
                       wt.loss  = c(5, 15),
                       sex      = factor(c("Female","Male"),
                                         levels = c("Male","Female")))
pred_surv <- survfit(res.strat, newdata = new_data)
ggsurvplot(pred_surv, data = new_data,
           legend.labs = c("F/55/karno80","M/70/karno60"),
           title = "Predicted Survival — Two Patient Profiles")
* Stata: Full stratified Cox workflow
use "lung.dta", clear
gen status2 = (status==2)

* STEP 1: KM curves
sts graph, by(sex)
sts test sex                   /* log-rank test */

* STEP 2: Standard Cox
stset time, failure(status==2)
stcox age i.sex ph_karno wt_loss, hr
estat concordance

* STEP 3: Test PH
estat phtest, detail
stphplot, by(sex) noshow        /* LML plot for sex */

* STEP 4: Stratified Cox
stcox age ph_karno wt_loss, strata(sex) hr
estat phtest, detail

* STEP 5: No-interaction test
estimates store m_strat
stcox c.age##i.sex c.ph_karno##i.sex c.wt_loss##i.sex
estimates store m_int
lrtest m_strat m_int

* STEP 6: Time-varying coefficient for ph_karno
stsplit tgroup, at(0 180 350)
stcox age wt_loss ph_karno#i.tgroup, strata(sex) hr

* STEP 7: Diagnostics
predict mart_r, mgale
predict dev_r, deviance
scatter dev_r _t, yline(0) yline(2) yline(-2)
* SPSS: Full stratified Cox workflow

* STEP 1: KM
KM time BY sex
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /PRINT TABLE MEAN
  /PLOT SURVIVAL
  /TEST LOGRANK.

* STEP 2: Standard Cox
COXREG time
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /METHOD=ENTER age sex ph_karno wt_loss
  /PLOT HAZARD SURVIVAL OMS LOGLOGLML
  /PRINT=CI(95).

* STEP 3: Test PH — LML plot
* In Cox dialog: Plots → Log minus log → Factor = sex

* STEP 4: Stratified Cox
COXREG time
  /STATUS=status(2)
  /STRATA=sex
  /METHOD=ENTER age ph_karno wt_loss
  /PRINT=CI(95).
Manuscript Write-up Template

"A Cox proportional hazards model was fitted to examine predictors of survival. The proportional hazards assumption was assessed using the Schoenfeld residual test (cox.zph). As sex violated the proportional hazards assumption (χ² = 4.8, df = 1, p = 0.028), a stratified Cox regression was employed with sex as the stratifying variable, allowing sex-specific baseline hazard functions. The no-interaction assumption was verified (LRT, p = 0.42). Hazard ratios for age (HR = 1.01, 95% CI: 0.99–1.03), ph.karno (HR = 0.98, 95% CI: 0.97–0.99, p < 0.001), and weight loss (HR = 1.01, 95% CI: 0.99–1.03) were estimated controlling for sex stratum."

Summary & Key Points

ConceptKey Point
Cox PH model\(h(t\mid X) = h_0(t)\exp(X\beta)\); semi-parametric; estimates HR via partial likelihood
PH assumptionHR is constant over time; hazard curves proportional; survival curves do not cross
Test PH: Schoenfeldcox.zph(); p < 0.05 → violation; plot residuals vs time for graphical check
Test PH: LML plotlog(−log(S(t))) vs log(t); parallel lines → PH holds; categorical variables only
Test PH: Time interactionAdd \(X imes \log(t)\); significant coefficient → PH violated
Stratified CoxStratify violating variable; each stratum has own \(h_{0g}(t)\); common \(\beta\) across strata
Stratified Cox — limitHR of stratifying variable not estimable; use for nuisance confounders only
No-interaction testLRT of stratified vs interaction model; p > 0.05 → stratified model valid
Time-varying covariateCovariate changes over follow-up; requires counting process (start, stop) format; use tmerge()
Time-varying coefficientEffect of baseline covariate changes over time; use survSplit() (step) or tt() (parametric)
Martingale residualsCheck functional form of continuous variables; LOWESS line should be flat
Deviance residualsDetect outliers; symmetric around 0; |r| > 2 → possible outlier
dfbetaInfluential observations; large relative to coefficient → check/remove
R packagessurvival: coxph, cox.zph, survSplit, tmerge; survminer: ggcoxzph, ggcoxdiagnostics
Statastcox, estat phtest, stphplot, stsplit
SPSSCOXREG with /STRATA=; LML plot via /PLOT LOGLOGLML
Decision Flow — Stratified Cox Analysis
  1. Fit standard Cox model with all covariates
  2. Test PH assumption: cox.zph() for each covariate + global test
  3. For each violating variable:
    • Is it a nuisance/confounder (HR not needed)? → Stratify using strata()
    • Is its effect of interest? → Time-varying coefficient via survSplit() or tt()
  4. Test no-interaction assumption (common \(\beta\) across strata) via LRT
  5. Re-test PH of remaining covariates in the stratified model
  6. Check diagnostics: Schoenfeld (PH), Martingale (functional form), Deviance (outliers), dfbeta (influence)
  7. Report: HR, 95% CI, p-values for all non-stratified covariates; note which variable was stratified and why
Sources for Topic 6
  • Cox DR. "Regression Models and Life-Tables." J R Statist Soc B 1972;34:187–220
  • Grambsch PM, Therneau TM. "Proportional hazards tests and diagnostics based on weighted residuals." Biometrika 1994;81:515–526
  • Therneau TM, Grambsch PM. Modeling Survival Data: Extending the Cox Model. Springer, 2000
  • Zhang Z, et al. "Time-varying covariates and coefficients in Cox regression models." Ann Transl Med 2018;6(7):121. PMC6015946
  • Kassambara A. STHDA: Cox Model Assumptions. sthda.com
  • UCLA OARC: "Testing the proportional hazard assumption in Cox models." stats.oarc.ucla.edu
Reference

Kleinbaum DG, Klein M. Survival Analysis: A Self-Learning Text, 3rd Ed. Chapter 6: Extension of the Cox PH Model for Time-Dependent Variables.

6.10 Extended Cox Model for Time-Dependent Variables

Why Extended Cox?

The standard Cox PH model requires all covariates to be time-independent. When a covariate changes over time, or when the PH assumption fails for a time-independent variable, the Extended Cox Model is used. It incorporates time-dependent variables (TDV) by including g(t) interaction terms.

Types of Time-Dependent Variables

TypeDefinitionExample
Externally definedChanges independently of subject (exogenous)Pollution level, drug dose over time
Internally definedChanges in response to subject state (endogenous)Heart transplant status, CD4 count
Heaviside functionArtificial TDV — step function at cutpoint t0g(t)=1 if t≥365; 0 otherwise

Extended Cox Model — General Form

h(t, X(t)) = h0(t) × exp[Σ βiXi + Σ δiXigi(t)]

where gi(t) is a function of time. The HR is now time-dependent: HR(t) = exp[β̂ + δ̂ g(t)]

Under H0: all δi = 0 → model reduces to standard Cox PH

Heaviside Function — Checking PH Assumption

Heaviside Step Function

g(t) = 1 if t ≥ t0;   g(t) = 0 if t < t0

Including E×g(t) as a product term gives two separate HR values for t < t0 and t ≥ t0.

One-heaviside model

h(t) = h0(t) exp[βE + δ(E×g(t))]

t ≥ t0: HR = exp(β̂ + δ̂)

t < t0: HR = exp(β̂)

Two-heaviside model (no main effect — equivalent form)

h(t) = h0(t) exp[δ1(E×g1(t)) + δ2(E×g2(t))]

where g1(t)=1 if t<t0; g2(t)=1 if t≥t0

t < t0: HR = exp(δ̂1)    t ≥ t0: HR = exp(δ̂2)

Relationship: δ̂1 = β̂; δ̂2 = β̂ + δ̂

LRT to Test PH Assumption

LR = −2 ln LPH − (−2 ln Lextended), distributed χ2 with df = number of δ terms

p < 0.05 → reject PH → use Extended Cox; HR is time-dependent

Application: Heroin Addiction Study — addicts.dta (Kleinbaum Ch. 6)

Caplehorn et al. (1991). The adjusted survival curves for clinic 1 vs clinic 2 diverge increasingly after t=365 days, suggesting the clinic effect grows over time — a PH violation. Cutpoint t0 = 365 days chosen for the Heaviside function.

library(survival)
library(haven)

datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')
# Recode clinic: 1=Clinic1, 0=Clinic2 (so HR>1 = Clinic1 worse)
datas$clinic1 <- ifelse(datas$clinic == 1, 1, 0)
surv.mod <- Surv(datas$survt, datas$status == 1)

# ---- Standard PH model (reference) ----
ph.mod <- coxph(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic1, data=datas)
summary(ph.mod)

# ---- Extended Cox: Continuous TDV (clinic1 x time) ----
# clinic_t is time-FIXED at each subject's observed time
# For proper TDV use counting-process format (see below)
datas$clinic_t <- datas$clinic1 * datas$survt

ext.cont <- coxph(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic1 + clinic_t, data=datas)
summary(ext.cont)
# clinic1:   coeff = -0.0183, p = 0.958
# clinic_t:  coeff = +0.003,  HR = 1.003, p = 0.001  <- significant TDV effect

# HR(t) = exp(beta_clinic1 + delta * t)
beta1 <- coef(ext.cont)["clinic1"]
delta <- coef(ext.cont)["clinic_t"]
t_vals <- c(91.5, 274, 458.5, 639, 821.5)
hr_t   <- exp(beta1 + delta * t_vals)
data.frame(Days=t_vals, HR=round(hr_t, 3))
#    Days     HR
#    91.5  1.292
#    274   2.233
#    458.5 3.862
#    639   6.677
#    821.5 11.544

# ---- Extended Cox: Two Heaviside functions at t0=365 days ----
datas$g1 <- as.numeric(datas$survt <  365)   # g1(t)=1 if t<365
datas$g2 <- as.numeric(datas$survt >= 365)   # g2(t)=1 if t>=365
datas$clinic_g1 <- datas$clinic1 * datas$g1
datas$clinic_g2 <- datas$clinic1 * datas$g2

ext.hs <- coxph(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic_g1 + clinic_g2, data=datas)
summary(ext.hs)
# prison:      HR=1.459, 95%CI(1.049,2.029), p=0.025
# dose:        HR=0.965, 95%CI(0.953,0.977), p<0.001
# clinic_g1:   HR=1.583, 95%CI(0.960,2.611), p=0.072  <- t<365 (NS)
# clinic_g2:   HR=6.223, 95%CI(2.921,13.259), p<0.001 <- t>=365 (significant)

# ---- LRT: Extended Cox vs PH ----
lrt <- -2*(logLik(ph.mod)[1] - logLik(ext.hs)[1])
pchisq(lrt, df=1, lower.tail=FALSE)
# p < 0.05 -> Extended Cox preferred; PH violated for clinic

# ---- Proper TDV via counting process (tmerge) ----
# For a truly time-varying covariate, restructure data into start-stop format
df_base <- data.frame(id=datas$id, t0=0, t1=datas$survt,
                      status=datas$status, prison=datas$prison,
                      dose=datas$dose, clinic1=datas$clinic1)

df_cp <- tmerge(df_base, df_base, id=id, event=event(t1, status))
df_cp$g2_tv <- as.numeric(df_cp$tstart >= 365)  # time-varying at each interval
df_cp$clinic_tv <- df_cp$clinic1 * df_cp$g2_tv

ext.tv <- coxph(Surv(tstart, tstop, event) ~ prison + dose + clinic1 + clinic_tv,
                data=df_cp)
summary(ext.tv)

* Extended Cox model — addicts.dta (Kleinbaum Ch. 6)
use addicts.dta, clear
gen clinic1 = (clinic == 1)   // 1=Clinic1, 0=Clinic2
stset survt, failure(status==1)

* --- Standard PH (reference) ---
stcox prison dose clinic1
estimates store ph_m

* --- Two Heaviside functions at t0=365 days ---
stsplit period, at(365) trim   // creates counting-process data
gen g1 = (_t0 <  365)          // interval before 365 days
gen g2 = (_t0 >= 365)          // interval from 365 days onward
gen clinic_g1 = clinic1 * g1
gen clinic_g2 = clinic1 * g2

stcox prison dose clinic_g1 clinic_g2, nolog
estimates store ext_m

* Expected results:
* prison:      HR=1.459, p=0.025
* dose:        HR=0.965, p<0.001
* clinic_g1:   HR=1.583, p=0.072   (t<365: borderline non-significant)
* clinic_g2:   HR=6.223, p<0.001   (t>=365: highly significant)

* --- LRT vs PH ---
lrtest ph_m ext_m
* p<0.05 -> PH assumption violated; Extended Cox preferred

* --- Continuous TDV: clinic x time ---
use addicts.dta, clear
gen clinic1 = (clinic == 1)
stset survt, failure(status==1)
stsplit, at(failures)     // split at each failure time
gen clinic_t = clinic1 * _t   // time-varying clinic*t

stcox prison dose clinic1 clinic_t, nolog
* clinic1:   coeff~-0.018, p~0.96
* clinic_t:  coeff~0.003,  p=0.001 (increasing HR over time)

Results: Two-Heaviside Model (addicts.dta)

VariableHR95% CIp-valueInterpretation
prison1.4591.049 – 2.0290.025Ex-prisoners: 46% higher dropout hazard
dose (per mg)0.9650.953 – 0.977<0.001Higher dose: 3.5% lower dropout per mg
Clinic 1 [t < 365d]1.5830.960 – 2.6110.072Small difference in year 1 (NS)
Clinic 1 [t ≥ 365d]6.2232.921 – 13.259<0.001Clinic 1 dropout rate 6.2x higher after year 1

Continuous TDV — Time-Varying HR for Clinic

DaysHR = exp(β̂ + δ̂t)95% CI
91.51.2920.741 – 2.250
2742.2331.470 – 3.391
458.53.8622.298 – 6.491
6396.6773.102 – 14.372
821.511.5443.976 – 33.513

δ̂ = +0.003 (positive) → HR increases exponentially over time. Clinic 1 patients show progressively higher dropout hazard compared to Clinic 2 as duration in treatment increases.

◆ Extended Cox vs Stratified Cox — Decision Guide
GoalApproach
PH violated; want adjusted HR for other predictorsStratified Cox — stratify the offending variable
PH violated; want HR per time-interval for offending variableExtended Cox with Heaviside function
Exposure truly changes over time (e.g. transplant, drug dose)Extended Cox with counting-process TDV
HR changes continuously with timeExtended Cox with E×t (smooth divergence)
Topic 07 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Survival Analysis — Parametric Models

Exponential · Weibull · Log-logistic · AFT & PH · AIC/BIC · addicts.dta

7.1 Introduction to Parametric Survival Models

Course Reference

Kamarul Imran Musa (2020). Lecture: Survival Analysis — Parametric Survival Analysis. DrPH Epidemiology Speciality, USM.

Three Classes of Survival Models

Model ClassExampleBaseline HazardCovariates
Non-parametricKaplan-MeierUnspecified; estimated empiricallyDifficult to incorporate
Semi-parametricCox PH regressionUnspecified; left freeParametric linear predictor
ParametricWeibull, Exponential, Log-logisticFully specified distributionParametric; maximum likelihood

Learning Objectives (Kamarul, 2020)

  • Understand the statistical concept of parametric survival analysis
  • Differentiate between parametric, semi-parametric, and non-parametric survival models
  • Perform parametric survival analysis — especially exponential and Weibull regression models

Why Parametric Models?

Non-parametric and semi-parametric methods are useful for exploratory analyses and comparing treatments. Parametric models provide:

  • Rigorous treatment of censored data
  • Estimation of mean survival time
  • Extrapolation or prediction beyond observed follow-up
  • Smoother estimated survival curves (draws information from whole data)
  • Full maximum likelihood estimation of parameters
  • Residuals computed as differences between observed and predicted survival times

Key Concept: Parametric Model Framework

In a parametric survival model, survival time T is assumed to follow a known distribution. The distribution specifies both the baseline hazard and the effect of covariates.

General form (AFT): ln(T) = β0 + β1X + σε

where ε follows a distribution-specific error (Gumbel for Weibull, logistic for log-logistic, normal for log-normal).

Because time T must be positive: exp(β0 + β1X) must be positive, and ε must also be positive.

Parametric Models in R and Stata

DistributionR (survreg)Stata (streg)PH?AFT?
Exponentialdist="exponential"distribution(exponential)YesYes
Weibulldist="weibull"distribution(weibull)YesYes
GompertzNot in survreg; use flexsurvdistribution(gompertz)YesNo
Log-normaldist="lognormal"distribution(lognormal)NoYes
Log-logisticdist="loglogistic"distribution(loglogistic)NoYes
Generalised Gammaflexsurv onlydistribution(gamma)NoYes
◆ Important

In R, survival::survreg() runs AFT models only. For both PH and AFT, use flexsurv::flexsurvreg() with dist="weibull" (AFT) or dist="weibullPH" (PH). Stata can produce both PH and AFT for Exponential, Weibull, and Gompertz.

7.2 Common Parametric Distributions

Exponential Distribution

The simplest parametric model. Assumes constant hazard over time (memoryless property). Only one parameter: λ (rate). This is a special case of Weibull with shape parameter = 1.

Hazard: h(t) = λ (constant)

Survival: S(t) = exp(-λt)

When to use: When the hazard does not change with time. Rarely appropriate in medicine but useful as a baseline model for comparison.

Weibull Distribution

The most widely used parametric model. Two parameters: λ (scale) and α or p (shape).

Hazard: h(t) = λαtα-1

Survival: S(t) = exp(-λtα)

Shape parameter α (or p):

  • α > 1: hazard increases with time (ageing, cancer)
  • α = 1: hazard is constant → reduces to Exponential
  • α < 1: hazard decreases with time (early high-risk period)

In survreg(): Scale = σ = 1/α. So if Scale = 0.73, then shape = 1/0.73 = 1.37 (increasing hazard).

Special property: Weibull can be written as both PH and AFT model — the only common distribution with this dual property.

Log-normal Distribution

If ln(T) follows a normal distribution with mean μ and standard deviation σ, then T follows a log-normal distribution. Hazard is arc-shaped (rises then falls). AFT only.

Log-logistic Distribution

Similar to log-normal but with a heavier tail — mathematically easier to handle. Hazard is arc-shaped (rises then falls if κ > 1). AFT only. Useful when hazard peaks early then declines.

Can be checked using: plot of log(S/(1-S)) vs log(t). If straight and parallel lines → log-logistic PH assumption holds.

Distribution Comparison Summary

DistributionHazard ShapeParametersPH?AFT?R dist=
ExponentialConstantλYesYes"exponential"
WeibullMonotone (up or down)λ, αYesYes"weibull"
GompertzMonotone increasingλ, γYesNoflexsurv only
Log-normalArc-shapedμ, σNoYes"lognormal"
Log-logisticArc-shapedα, κNoYes"loglogistic"
Gen. GammaFlexible (any)μ, σ, QNoYesflexsurv only

7.3 AFT vs PH Parameterisation

Two Frameworks

AFT (Accelerated Failure Time): Effect of covariate is multiplicative on time scale. Reported as Time Ratio (TR) or Acceleration Factor (AF). TR > 1 = longer survival; TR < 1 = shorter survival.

PH (Proportional Hazard): Effect of covariate is multiplicative on hazard scale. Reported as Hazard Ratio (HR). HR > 1 = higher risk; HR < 1 = lower risk.

AFT Model — General Form

ln(T) = β0 + β1X + σε

Time Ratio (TR) = exp(β1)

95% CI for TR = exp(β1 ± 1.96 × SE)

If TR = 0.55 for females vs males: females have survival time estimated to be 55% that of males (shortened by 45%).

PH Model — General Form

h(t|X) = h0(t) × exp(β1X)

Hazard Ratio (HR) = exp(β1)

Exponential model only: HR = exp(-AFT coefficient) = 1/TR

Key Examples from Lecture (Kamarul, 2020)

Example 1 — Exponential AFT (SEX variable)
VariableCoeffSE95% CI
SEX (Female=1)-0.6020.281-1.153, -0.050
Intercept2.3180.1891.947, 2.688

TR = exp(-0.602) = 0.55 → females survive only 55% as long as males

95% CI for TR = exp(-1.153), exp(-0.050) = 0.32, 0.95

HR (since exponential PH = AFT) = exp(+0.602) = 1.83 → females have 1.83x the hazard of males

Predicted median survival: male = -exp(2.318) × ln(0.5) = 7.04 months; female = -exp(2.318-0.602) × ln(0.5) = 3.86 months

Exponential vs Weibull — Coefficient Sign Convention

ModelParameterisationPositive coeff means…Reported as
survreg() AFTlog-timeLonger survival timeTR = exp(coeff) > 1
survreg() AFTlog-timeLower hazardHR = exp(-coeff) < 1
coxph()log-hazardHigher hazardHR = exp(coeff) > 1
◆ Critical: survreg() Sign Reversal

In survreg() AFT output, a positive coefficient means longer survival (protective). This is the opposite of Cox regression, where a positive coefficient means higher hazard (harmful). Always specify which parameterisation you are using when reporting results.

7.4 Exponential Regression Model

Assumption

The hazard is constant over time (no change in risk with time). The scale parameter in survreg() is fixed at 1 for the exponential model.

# ===== EXPONENTIAL MODEL =====
# Dataset: addicts.dta (Caplehorn 1991 — methadone clinic study)
# Outcome: survt (days until dropout); status 1=dropped out, 0=censored
# Predictors: prison (0/1), dose (mg/day), clinic (1 or 2)

library(haven)
library(survival)

datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')   # n=238; clinic=1 or 2; status 0/1

# Define Surv object
surv.mod <- Surv(time = datas$survt, event = datas$status == 1)

# Fit exponential AFT model (scale fixed at 1)
exp.mod <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                   data = datas,
                   dist = 'exponential')
summary(exp.mod)

# Output:
#             Value  Std.Error    z       p
# (Intercept) 3.684     0.431  8.55  <2e-16
# prison     -0.253     0.165 -1.53   0.126
# dose        0.029     0.006  4.71   2e-06
# clinic      0.881     0.211  4.18   3e-05
# Scale fixed at 1 (exponential)
# Loglik(model)= -1094; Loglik(intercept only)= -1119

# --- Interpretation ---
# prison: coeff = -0.2526
# TR (prison=1 vs 0) = exp(-0.2526) = 0.777 (shorter dropout time for ex-prisoners)
# HR (prison=1 vs 0) = exp(+0.2526) = 1.29  (29% higher hazard for ex-prisoners)

# clinic: coeff = 0.881
# TR (clinic=2 vs 1) = exp(0.881) = 2.41 (clinic 2 patients stay 2.41x longer)
# HR (clinic=2 vs 1) = exp(-0.881) = 0.41 (59% lower dropout hazard in clinic 2)

# 95% CI for TR:
exp(confint(exp.mod))          # CI on AFT scale
exp(-confint(exp.mod))         # CI for HR (exponential only)

# Manual calculation for prison:
beta_prison <- -0.2526
se_prison   <- 0.16489
TR_prison   <- exp(beta_prison)                          # 0.777
CI_TR       <- exp(c(beta_prison - 1.96*se_prison,
                      beta_prison + 1.96*se_prison))     # 0.56, 1.07
HR_prison   <- exp(-beta_prison)                         # 1.29
CI_HR       <- exp(-c(beta_prison + 1.96*se_prison,
                       beta_prison - 1.96*se_prison))    # 0.93, 1.78

cat("TR (prison):", round(TR_prison,3),
    "95% CI:", round(CI_TR,3), "
")
cat("HR (prison):", round(HR_prison,3),
    "95% CI:", round(CI_HR,3), "
")

# Predicted median survival time for specific patient:
# prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1
new.data <- data.frame(prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1)
predict(exp.mod, newdata=new.data, type='quantile', p=0.5)
# t_median = -exp(intercept + coeff*X) * ln(0.5)

* ===== EXPONENTIAL MODEL IN STATA =====
* Read Stata dataset
use addicts.dta, clear
describe

* Declare survival data
stset survt, failure(status==1)

* --- PH parameterisation (default in Stata) ---
streg prison dose clinic, distribution(exponential)
* HR = exp(coeff): HR_prison = exp(-0.253) = 0.777 (REVERSED from R AFT)
* Note: Stata PH coefficient is NEGATIVE of R AFT coefficient

* --- AFT parameterisation ---
streg prison dose clinic, distribution(exponential) time
* TR = exp(coeff): same as R survreg output

* Predicted survival at specific values
predict s_hat, surv
predict t_med, time    // predicted mean (not median for exponential)

* Median survival for specific covariate pattern:
* t_median = -exp(Xbeta) * ln(0.5) for exponential

Exponential Model Interpretation Summary

VariableCoeff (AFT)TR = exp(coeff)HR = exp(-coeff)p-valueInterpretation
prison-0.2530.7771.290.126Ex-prisoners drop out 22.3% sooner; NS
dose0.0291.0290.971<0.001Each 1mg/day increase extends stay by 2.9%
clinic0.8812.410.41<0.001Clinic 2 patients stay 2.41x longer

7.5 Weibull Regression Model

Why Weibull?

Most widely used parametric model. Flexible: increasing, decreasing, or constant hazard. Algebraically straightforward. Supports both PH and AFT parameterisation (the only common distribution with this dual property).

Scale parameter (σ) from survreg() = 1/shape (α). If Scale=0.73, then shape = 1/0.73 = 1.37 → increasing hazard over time.

library(survival)
library(haven)

datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')
surv.mod <- Surv(time = datas$survt, event = datas$status == 1)

# ===== WEIBULL AFT MODEL (survreg default) =====
wei.mod <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                   data = datas,
                   dist = 'weibull')
summary(wei.mod)

# Output:
#             Value  Std.Error     z        p
# (Intercept) 4.105     0.328  12.51  < 2e-16
# prison     -0.229     0.121  -1.90    0.057
# dose        0.024     0.005   5.32    1e-07
# clinic      0.709     0.157   4.51    7e-06
# Log(scale) -0.315     0.068  -4.66    3e-06
# Scale= 0.730
# Weibull distribution
# Loglik(model)= -1084.5; Loglik(intercept only)= -1114.9
# Chisq= 60.89 on 3 df, p= 3.8e-13

# --- Interpretation ---
# Scale (sigma) = 0.73; Shape (alpha/p) = 1/0.73 = 1.37 -> INCREASING hazard

# TR for prison = exp(-0.2295) = 0.795
# Ex-prisoners' dropout time = 79.5% that of non-prisoners
TR_prison <- exp(-0.22947)
TR_prison

# TR for clinic (clinic=2 vs 1) = exp(0.7090) = 2.032
# Patients in clinic 2 stay 2.03x longer than those in clinic 1
TR_clinic <- exp(0.70904)
TR_clinic

# 95% CI for TR
exp(confint(wei.mod))

# Predicted survival quantiles for: prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1
new.data <- data.frame(prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1)
quant.p <- c(0.25, 0.50, 0.75)
predict(wei.mod, newdata=new.data, type='quantile', p=quant.p)
# Result: [133.8, 254.2, 421.6] days

# Plot predicted survival curve
pct <- 1:98/100
plot(x = predict(wei.mod, newdata=new.data,
                 type="quantile", p=pct),
     y = 1-pct,
     type="l", xlab="Days", ylab="Survival Probability",
     main="Weibull: Predicted Survival (prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1)")

library(SurvRegCensCov)
# SurvRegCensCov converts survreg() AFT output to HR and ETR (Event Time Ratio)
# Install: install.packages("SurvRegCensCov")

# ConvertWeibull() transforms log-time scale to log-hazard scale
ConvertWeibull(wei.mod, conf.level=0.95)

# Output:
# $vars
#            Estimate          SE
# lambda   0.003608599  0.002377360   <- baseline hazard scale parameter
# gamma    1.370194355  0.092567991   <- shape parameter (same as 1/Scale)
# prison   0.314414290  0.165946180   <- log-HR for prison
# dose    -0.033467508  0.006255037   <- log-HR for dose
# clinic  -0.971524493  0.212282628   <- log-HR for clinic

# $HR (Hazard Ratios)
#            HR        LB        UB
# prison  1.369    0.989    1.896   <- prison: 37% higher hazard (NS, p~0.06)
# dose    0.967    0.955    0.979   <- per 1mg/day: 3.3% lower hazard ***
# clinic  0.379    0.250    0.574   <- clinic 2: 62% lower hazard ***

# $ETR (Event Time Ratios = same as TR from AFT)
#            ETR       LB        UB
# prison  0.795    0.627    1.007   <- prison: 79.5% of time
# dose    1.025    1.016    1.034   <- per 1mg/day: 2.5% longer time ***
# clinic  2.032    1.493    2.765   <- clinic 2: 2.03x longer time ***

# Alternative: WeibullReg() = survreg() + ConvertWeibull() combined
wei.reg <- WeibullReg(Surv(survt, status) ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                      data=datas, conf.level=0.95)
print(wei.reg)

library(flexsurv)
library(haven)
datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')

# ===== Weibull AFT via flexsurvreg() =====
wei.mod.aft <- flexsurvreg(Surv(survt, status) ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                            data=datas, dist='weibull')
wei.mod.aft
# Outputs: shape, scale parameters + exp(est) = TR for each covariate
# prison: exp(est) = 0.795
# dose:   exp(est) = 1.025
# clinic: exp(est) = 2.032
# AIC = 2178.953

# ===== Weibull PH via flexsurvreg() =====
wei.mod.ph <- flexsurvreg(Surv(survt, status) ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                           data=datas, dist='weibullPH')
wei.mod.ph
# Outputs: exp(est) = HR for each covariate
# prison: exp(est) = 1.369
# dose:   exp(est) = 0.967
# clinic: exp(est) = 0.379
# AIC = 2178.953 (same — just different parameterisation)

# Confirm: AFT and PH are same model, different scale
# HR = 1/TR^(1/sigma) or equivalently: log(HR) = -log(TR)/sigma

# Plot fitted Weibull survival curves
plot(wei.mod.aft, ci=TRUE, xlab="Days",
     ylab="S(t)", main="Weibull AFT Fit vs KM")

# Fitted survival at specific times
summary(wei.mod.aft, t=c(180, 365, 730))

# Predict for specific patient
new.data <- data.frame(prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1)
summary(wei.mod.aft, newdata=new.data, type="quantile", quantiles=0.5)

Weibull Results Summary (addicts.dta)

VariableAFT coeffTR (95% CI)HR (95% CI)p
prison-0.2290.795 (0.627–1.007)1.369 (0.989–1.896)0.057
dose (per 1 mg/day)+0.0241.025 (1.016–1.034)0.967 (0.955–0.979)<0.001
clinic (2 vs 1)+0.7092.032 (1.493–2.765)0.379 (0.250–0.574)<0.001

Scale (σ) = 0.730; Shape (α = 1/σ) = 1.370 — increasing hazard over time.

Predicted median dropout time (prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1): 254.2 days (25th %ile: 133.8d; 75th %ile: 421.6d)

7.6 Log-logistic Regression Model

When to Use

Log-logistic is similar to log-normal but with a heavier tail — mathematically easier to handle. Hazard is arc-shaped: rises initially then declines. Suitable when early risk peaks then reduces (e.g., surgery outcomes, infection recovery). AFT parameterisation only — does not satisfy PH assumption.

library(survival)
library(haven)

datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')
surv.mod <- Surv(time = datas$survt, event = datas$status == 1)

# ===== LOG-LOGISTIC AFT MODEL =====
logl.mod <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                    data=datas,
                    dist='loglogistic')
summary(logl.mod)

# Output:
#             Value  Std.Error    z        p
# (Intercept) 3.563     0.389   9.15  <2e-16
# prison     -0.291     0.144  -2.02   0.043
# dose        0.032     0.006   5.73   1e-08
# clinic      0.581     0.172   3.38   0.001
# Log(scale) -0.533     0.069  -7.77   8e-15
# Scale= 0.587
# Log logistic distribution
# Loglik(model)= -1093.9; Loglik(intercept only)= -1120

# --- Interpretation ---
# prison: TR = exp(-0.291) = 0.747 -> ex-prisoners drop out 25.3% sooner (p=0.043)
# dose:   TR = exp(0.032)  = 1.033 -> per 1mg/day: 3.3% longer retention
# clinic: TR = exp(0.581)  = 1.787 -> clinic 2 patients stay 1.79x longer

TR_clinic_ll <- exp(0.58060)
TR_clinic_ll   # 1.787

# Comparing log-logistic vs Weibull
AIC(wei.mod, logl.mod)  # Lower AIC = better fit

# Note: log-logistic AIC = 2*(-(-1093.9)) + 2*5 = 2197.8
#        Weibull AFT AIC  = 2*(-(-1084.5)) + 2*5 = 2179.0
# -> Weibull fits better (lower AIC)

stset survt, failure(status==1)

* Log-logistic model (AFT only in Stata)
streg prison dose clinic, distribution(loglogistic) time
* Reports Time Ratios directly

* For model comparison:
estimates store logl_m

streg prison dose clinic, distribution(weibull) time
estimates store weib_m

estimates stats logl_m weib_m
* Compare AIC/BIC

Checking PH Assumption for Log-logistic

For log-logistic PH assumption: plot log-odds of survival [log(S/(1-S))] vs log of time [log(t)]. If the plots show straight and parallel lines for each covariate pattern, the PH assumption holds and the log-logistic model is appropriate.

# Check PH assumption for log-logistic
kmfit <- survfit(surv.mod ~ datas$clinic)

plot(log(kmfit$time),
     log(kmfit$surv / (1 - kmfit$surv)),
     col = rep(c("blue","red"), table(datas$clinic)),
     pch = 16, cex = 0.5,
     xlab = "log(t)", ylab = "log(S/(1-S))",
     main = "Log-logistic Check: logit S(t) vs log(t)")

# If lines are NOT straight and parallel -> PH assumption violated
# -> Use AFT parameterisation (reported as TR only)
# -> Or consider a different distribution

7.7 Assessing Model Adequacy

Two-Stage Assessment

Relative fit: AIC / LRT — which distribution fits better?
Absolute fit: Graphical checks — does the selected distribution adequately describe the data?

AIC/LRT alone tells you relative fit. Even if log-logistic fits better than exponential, it may still be a poor fit in absolute terms.

1. Model Comparison (AIC/LRT)

Nested models (e.g., Exponential vs Weibull): Use Likelihood Ratio Test (LRT).

LRT = -2 × (logLrestricted - logLfull), follows χ2 with df = difference in parameters.

Non-nested models: Use AIC = -2logL + 2k (lower = better).

library(survival)
library(haven)
datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')
surv.mod <- Surv(datas$survt, datas$status==1)

# Fit all common distributions
exp.mod  <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic, data=datas, dist='exponential')
wei.mod  <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic, data=datas, dist='weibull')
ln.mod   <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic, data=datas, dist='lognormal')
ll.mod   <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic, data=datas, dist='loglogistic')

# AIC comparison table
aic_df <- data.frame(
  Distribution = c("Exponential","Weibull","Log-normal","Log-logistic"),
  LogL = round(c(logLik(exp.mod), logLik(wei.mod),
                 logLik(ln.mod), logLik(ll.mod)), 1),
  k    = c(4, 5, 5, 5),
  AIC  = round(AIC(exp.mod, wei.mod, ln.mod, ll.mod)$AIC, 1)
)
aic_df$dAIC <- aic_df$AIC - min(aic_df$AIC)
aic_df[order(aic_df$AIC),]

# Typical output for addicts.dta:
# Weibull:      LogL=-1084.5  AIC=2179.0  dAIC=0.0   <- best
# Log-logistic: LogL=-1093.9  AIC=2197.8  dAIC=18.8
# Log-normal:   LogL=-1098.1  AIC=2206.2  dAIC=27.2
# Exponential:  LogL=-1094.0  AIC=2196.0  dAIC=17.0

# LRT: Exponential vs Weibull (nested; Weibull has 1 extra parameter)
lrt_stat <- -2 * (logLik(exp.mod)[1] - logLik(wei.mod)[1])
p_lrt    <- pchisq(lrt_stat, df=1, lower.tail=FALSE)
cat("LRT Exp vs Weibull: chi2=", round(lrt_stat,2),
    "p=", round(p_lrt, 4), "
")
# p << 0.05 -> Weibull significantly better than Exponential

2. Weibull Diagnostic Plot — WeibullDiag()

For Weibull: plot log cumulative hazard [log(-log S(t))] vs log survival time [log(t)]. If lines are straight and parallel (not flat) → Weibull model is appropriate.

  • Straight (linear): supports Weibull
  • Parallel: supports proportional hazard assumption
  • Flat: suggests exponential (not Weibull)
  • Not straight: Weibull not appropriate; try log-logistic or log-normal
library(SurvRegCensCov)
library(haven)
datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')
surv.mod <- Surv(datas$survt, datas$status==1)

# Weibull diagnostic plot by prison history
WeibullDiag(surv.mod ~ prison, data=datas)
# X-axis: survival time (log scale); Y-axis: log cumulative hazard
# Parallel + linear lines -> Weibull OK for prison variable

WeibullDiag(surv.mod ~ clinic, data=datas)
# Check for clinic variable

# Alternative: manual log-log plot using KM
km_clinic <- survfit(surv.mod ~ datas$clinic)
plot(log(km_clinic$time),
     log(-log(km_clinic$surv)),
     col = rep(c("blue","red"), as.vector(table(datas$clinic[!duplicated(datas$clinic)]))),
     xlab = "log(t)", ylab = "log(-log S(t))",
     main = "Weibull Check: by Clinic",
     pch = 16, cex = 0.5)
abline(h=0, lty=2, col="grey")

# 3. Compare with Cox PH coefficients
cox.mod <- coxph(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic, data=datas)
summary(cox.mod)
# Cox HR for clinic: ~ 0.41 vs Weibull PH HR: 0.38 -> similar -> Weibull OK
# If very different -> baseline hazard misspecified -> use Cox instead

# 4. eha package alternative: check.dist()
# install.packages("eha")
library(eha)
check.dist(cox.mod, wei.mod)
# Plots observed (Cox) vs parametric (Weibull) hazard/survival
# Should overlay closely if Weibull assumption is met

Model Adequacy Decision Flow

StepMethodDecision
1. Compare distributionsAIC table + LRT (nested)Select lowest AIC
2. Visual check (Weibull)WeibullDiag() — log-log plotStraight + parallel = Weibull OK
3. Visual check (log-logistic)logit S(t) vs log(t)Straight + parallel = OK
4. Compare with CoxCox HR vs parametric HRSimilar = parametric OK; Very different = use Cox
5. eha::check.dist()Overlay observed vs fittedClose overlay = adequate fit
◆ Kamarul's Rule (2020)

"If the regression parameters between the PH parametric models and the Cox PH model are almost similar, then the parametric models are acceptable. If they are very different, then there is evidence of a misparameterised underlying baseline hazard. In this situation, it is safer to use Cox PH regression results."

7.8 Worked Example — Methadone Clinic Study (addicts.dta)

Study Context

Source: Caplehorn et al. (1991), Australia. Two methadone treatment clinics for heroin addicts. Outcome: time (days) until dropout from clinic or censored (status=0).

Variables: id, survt (days), status (1=dropout, 0=censored), clinic (1 or 2), prison (0/1), dose (max methadone mg/day, continuous).

n = 238; 150 events (63%); 88 censored (37%).

library(haven)
library(tidyverse)
library(survival)
library(SurvRegCensCov)
library(flexsurv)

# ===========================================================
# FULL PARAMETRIC SURVIVAL WORKFLOW — addicts.dta
# Kamarul Imran Musa, DrPH GET502 Practical 2020
# ===========================================================

# ---- 1. Load and describe data ----
datas <- read_dta('addicts.dta')
glimpse(datas)
summary(datas)

# ---- 2. Define Surv object ----
surv.mod <- Surv(time = datas$survt, event = datas$status == 1)

# ---- 3. Exponential AFT model ----
exp.mod <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                   data=datas, dist='exponential')
summary(exp.mod)
# Scale fixed at 1; interpret as TR

# ---- 4. Weibull AFT model ----
wei.mod <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                   data=datas, dist='weibull')
summary(wei.mod)
# Scale = 0.73; shape = 1/0.73 = 1.37 -> increasing hazard

# ---- 5. Convert Weibull AFT to HR and ETR ----
ConvertWeibull(wei.mod, conf.level=0.95)
# HR for clinic: 0.379 (95%CI: 0.250-0.574) ***
# ETR for clinic: 2.032 (95%CI: 1.493-2.765) ***

# ---- 6. Weibull PH via flexsurv ----
wei.mod.ph <- flexsurvreg(Surv(survt, status) ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                           data=datas, dist='weibullPH')
print(wei.mod.ph)
# exp(est) = HR for each covariate

# ---- 7. Log-logistic AFT model ----
logl.mod <- survreg(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic,
                    data=datas, dist='loglogistic')
summary(logl.mod)
# Scale=0.587; prison p=0.043 (now significant vs Weibull p=0.057)

# ---- 8. AIC model comparison ----
data.frame(
  Model = c("Exponential","Weibull","Log-logistic"),
  LogL  = c(logLik(exp.mod)[1], logLik(wei.mod)[1], logLik(logl.mod)[1]),
  AIC   = c(AIC(exp.mod), AIC(wei.mod), AIC(logl.mod))
)
# Weibull: AIC = 2179 (best)

# ---- 9. Weibull diagnostic plot ----
WeibullDiag(surv.mod ~ prison, data=datas)
WeibullDiag(surv.mod ~ clinic, data=datas)
# Lines straight and parallel -> Weibull appropriate

# ---- 10. Check PH assumption (log-logistic) ----
kmfit <- survfit(surv.mod ~ datas$clinic)
plot(log(kmfit$time), log(kmfit$surv/(1-kmfit$surv)),
     xlab="log(t)", ylab="logit S(t)",
     main="Log-logistic PH Check by Clinic")
# Not parallel -> PH assumption not met for log-logistic

# ---- 11. Predict survival quantiles (Weibull) ----
new.data <- data.frame(prison=1, dose=50, clinic=1)
quant.p  <- c(0.25, 0.50, 0.75)
days.p   <- predict(wei.mod, newdata=new.data, type='quantile', p=quant.p)
cbind(quant.p, days.p)
# 25th: 133.8d; Median: 254.2d; 75th: 421.6d

# ---- 12. Plot predicted survival curve ----
pct <- 1:98/100
plot(x = predict(wei.mod, newdata=new.data,
                 type="quantile", p=pct),
     y = 1-pct, type="l",
     xlab="Days in clinic", ylab="P(remaining in treatment)",
     main="Predicted Weibull Survival
(prison=1, dose=50mg, clinic=1)")

# ---- 13. Comparison with Cox PH ----
cox.mod <- coxph(surv.mod ~ prison + dose + clinic, data=datas)
# Cox HR (clinic): exp(-0.940) = 0.39 vs Weibull PH HR: 0.38 -> similar -> OK

Final Model Comparison Table

DistributionLog-Likelihoodk (params)AICΔAIC
Weibull-1084.552179.00.0
Exponential-1094.042196.017.0
Log-logistic-1093.952197.818.8

Conclusion: Weibull provides best fit (lowest AIC). Confirmed by WeibullDiag() — straight and parallel lines for both prison and clinic variables.

7.9 Reporting Parametric Survival Analysis

What to Report

  • Justification: why parametric over Cox (violated PH, need extrapolation, prediction purpose)
  • All distributions fitted with AIC/BIC comparison table
  • Selected distribution with rationale (AIC + graphical)
  • Parameterisation (AFT or PH) and effect measure (TR or HR)
  • Coefficients, Time Ratios or Hazard Ratios, 95% CI, p-values
  • Scale and shape parameters
  • Weibull diagnostic plot (if Weibull used)
  • Comparison with Cox PH coefficients
  • Predicted survival quantiles with 95% CI for representative patients

Thesis Paragraph Template

"Parametric survival analysis was performed to model time to dropout from the methadone treatment clinic. Three candidate distributions were evaluated — exponential, Weibull, and log-logistic. The Weibull distribution provided the best model fit based on the lowest Akaike Information Criterion (AIC = 2179.0, ΔAIC vs next best = 17.0). Model adequacy was confirmed by WeibullDiag diagnostic plots, which showed approximately straight and parallel lines in the log cumulative hazard vs log survival time plot for both prison history and clinic, supporting the Weibull distributional assumption and the proportional hazards structure.

The accelerated failure time (AFT) parameterisation of the Weibull model was used. The estimated shape parameter (1/scale = 1/0.730 = 1.370) indicated an increasing hazard of dropout over time. After controlling for prison history and methadone dose, patients attending Clinic 2 had a Time Ratio of 2.032 (95% CI: 1.493–2.765, p < 0.001), indicating that their time to dropout was estimated to be 2.03 times longer than patients attending Clinic 1. In the proportional hazards metric, this corresponds to a Hazard Ratio of 0.379 (95% CI: 0.250–0.574), indicating a 62.1% lower hazard of dropout for Clinic 2 patients. Each 1 mg/day increase in maximum methadone dose was associated with a 2.5% longer retention time (TR = 1.025, 95% CI: 1.016–1.034, p < 0.001). Prison history was not significantly associated with dropout time controlling for dose and clinic (TR = 0.795, 95% CI: 0.627–1.007, p = 0.057)."
