Topic 08 · GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis

Machine Learning — Classification

Decision Tree · k-Nearest Neighbors · Artificial Neural Network · Cross-Validation · Performance Evaluation

8.1 What is Machine Learning?

Definition 1 — Arthur Samuel (1959):
"Field of study that gives computers the ability to learn without being explicitly programmed."
Definition 2 — Tom Mitchell (1997):
"A computer program is said to learn from experience E with respect to some task T and some performance measure P, if its performance on T, as measured by P, improves with experience E."

Example — Spam Email Detection:
• E = user labels emails as spam or not spam
• T = classify emails as spam or not spam
• P = number of emails correctly classified as spam or not spam

Types of Machine Learning

TypeDescriptionExample
Supervised Learning Direct supervision — labelled training data provided; model learns input-output mapping Classification, Regression
Unsupervised Learning No direct supervision — model finds hidden structure in unlabelled data Clustering, Dimensionality Reduction
Reinforcement Learning Trial-and-error with feedback — agent learns from rewards and penalties Game playing, Robotics
Note: Classification (this topic) is a supervised learning task — the model is trained on labelled examples with a known target class variable.

ML in Health and Medicine

ML classification has broad public health and clinical applications:

  • Disease diagnosis (diabetes, cancer detection)
  • Hospital readmission risk prediction
  • Outbreak detection and epidemic modelling
  • Drug response classification
  • Medical imaging analysis

Reference: Linda Chua, PhD (slchua@mmu.edu.my), GET502 Lecture, 2024.

8.2 Classification

Classification is a supervised learning task where the goal is to predict a categorical target class (Y) for new, unseen records.

Target Variable Y: the variable being predicted (dependent variable) — it is a categorical outcome (e.g., Play Tennis: Yes/No; Diabetes: pos/neg; Disease: present/absent).

PlayTennis Dataset — Running Example

This 14-record dataset is used throughout the lecture to illustrate classification algorithms (Linda Chua 2024):

OutlookTemperatureHumidityWindPlay Tennis?
SunnyHotHighWeakNo
SunnyHotHighStrongNo
OvercastHotHighWeakYes
RainMildHighWeakYes
RainCoolNormalWeakYes
RainCoolNormalStrongNo
OvercastCoolNormalStrongYes
SunnyMildHighWeakNo
SunnyCoolNormalWeakYes
RainMildNormalWeakYes
SunnyMildNormalStrongYes
OvercastMildHighStrongYes
OvercastHotNormalWeakYes
RainMildHighStrongNo

n = 14; 9 Yes, 5 No. Four predictor variables: Outlook, Temperature, Humidity, Wind.

Classification Goal

Train: Build a model on records with known class labels
Apply: Use the model to predict the class of new (unseen) records

Methods Covered

  • Decision Tree — rule-based hierarchical splitting (entropy & information gain)
  • k-Nearest Neighbors (KNN) — classify by majority vote of k closest training points
  • Artificial Neural Network (ANN) — perceptron-based weight learning via backpropagation

8.3 Decision Tree

A decision tree is a flow-chart-like structure consisting of a sequence of decision nodes, each testing a variable, leading to leaf nodes with class labels. Easy to understand and explain.

Entropy

Entropy measures the amount of impurity (disorder) in a set of examples:

\[ \text{Entropy}(S) = -\sum_{i} p_i \log_2 p_i \]

where \(p_i\) is the proportion of set \(S\) belonging to class \(i\).

  • Entropy = 0 → perfectly pure (all one class)
  • Entropy = 1 → maximally impure (equal split for 2 classes)

PlayTennis: Entropy of full dataset S (9 Yes, 5 No out of 14)

\[ \text{Entropy}(S) = -\frac{9}{14}\log_2\frac{9}{14} - \frac{5}{14}\log_2\frac{5}{14} = \mathbf{0.940} \]

Information Gain

Information Gain is the expected reduction in entropy achieved by partitioning the examples according to a variable A:

\[ \text{Gain}(S, A) = \text{Entropy}(S) - \sum_{v \in \text{Values}(A)} \frac{|S_v|}{|S|} \cdot \text{Entropy}(S_v) \]

where \(\text{Values}(A)\) = set of all possible values for variable A, and \(S_v\) = subset of S where A = v.

At each node, select the variable with the highest Information Gain as the splitting variable.

Worked Example — PlayTennis Root Node

Gain(S, Wind)

Wind has two values: Weak (n=8: 6 Yes, 2 No) and Strong (n=6: 3 Yes, 3 No)

\[ \text{Entropy}(S_{\text{Weak}}) = -\frac{6}{8}\log_2\frac{6}{8} - \frac{2}{8}\log_2\frac{2}{8} = 0.811 \] \[ \text{Entropy}(S_{\text{Strong}}) = -\frac{3}{6}\log_2\frac{3}{6} - \frac{3}{6}\log_2\frac{3}{6} = 1.000 \] \[ \text{Gain}(S, \text{Wind}) = 0.940 - \frac{8}{14}(0.811) - \frac{6}{14}(1.000) = \mathbf{0.048} \]

Gain(S, Humidity)

Humidity: High (n=7: 3 Yes, 4 No) and Normal (n=7: 6 Yes, 1 No)

\[ \text{Entropy}(S_{\text{High}}) = -\frac{3}{7}\log_2\frac{3}{7} - \frac{4}{7}\log_2\frac{4}{7} = 0.985 \] \[ \text{Entropy}(S_{\text{Normal}}) = -\frac{6}{7}\log_2\frac{6}{7} - \frac{1}{7}\log_2\frac{1}{7} = 0.592 \] \[ \text{Gain}(S, \text{Humidity}) = 0.940 - \frac{7}{14}(0.985) - \frac{7}{14}(0.592) = \mathbf{0.151} \]

Summary of Information Gains

VariableInformation GainSelected?
Outlook0.246✓ Root node
Humidity0.151
Wind0.048
Temperature0.029

Outlook has the highest information gain (0.246) → selected as the root node.

Recursive Splitting (Sunny Branch)

For the Sunny branch (n=5: 2 Yes, 3 No), compute gain for remaining variables:

\[ \text{Entropy}(S_{\text{Sunny}}) = -\frac{2}{5}\log_2\frac{2}{5} - \frac{3}{5}\log_2\frac{3}{5} = 0.971 \] \[ \text{Gain}(S_{\text{Sunny}}, \text{Humidity}) = 0.971 - \frac{3}{5}(0) - \frac{2}{5}(0) = \mathbf{0.971} \] \[ \text{Gain}(S_{\text{Sunny}}, \text{Temperature}) = \mathbf{0.571} \] \[ \text{Gain}(S_{\text{Sunny}}, \text{Wind}) = \mathbf{0.019} \]

Humidity selected for Sunny branch: High → No (3/3), Normal → Yes (2/2). Both pure nodes (entropy = 0) → leaf nodes.

Note on Overcast branch: All 4 Overcast records are Yes (pure node) → leaf node immediately. No further splitting needed.

Decision Tree Algorithms

AlgorithmSplitting CriterionNotes
ID3Information Gain (Entropy)Used in Linda Chua's lectures
CARTGINI IndexUsed in R rpart() by default; also handles regression
C5.0 / C4.5Gain RatioReduces bias toward high-cardinality variables
CHAIDChi-square testSuitable for categorical predictors
Algorithm (ID3):
1. At each node, compute Information Gain for all candidate variables
2. Select the variable with the highest gain as the split variable
3. Create child nodes for each possible value of the split variable
4. Repeat recursively until all leaf nodes are pure OR no variables remain

8.4 k-Nearest Neighbors (k-NN)

k-NN classifies an object by a majority vote of its k nearest neighbors in the feature space. It is the simplest non-parametric classification method.

Algorithm

  1. Compute a distance between the new object and every training record
  2. Pick the k closest training records (smallest distance)
  3. Conduct a majority vote among those k neighbors → assign that class

Euclidean Distance

\[ \text{dist}(x, y) = \sqrt{\sum_{i=1}^{n}(x_i - y_i)^2} \]

Example: dist((5,6),(2,2)) = √((5−2)² + (6−2)²) = √(9+16) = √25 = 5

Worked Example — Gender Classification (Linda Chua 2024)

Classify a new person (Height=170, Weight=70) using k=3 from training data:

GenderHeightWeightDistance to (170,70)
M175725.39 ← 1st nearest
F1706010.00 ← 2nd nearest
F1715911.05 ← 3rd nearest
F1685812.17
M1798114.21
M1847816.12
F1635516.55
M1819830.08
k = 3 nearest neighbors: M (d=5.39), F (d=10.00), F (d=11.05)
Majority vote: F(2) vs M(1) → Classified as Female

Normalization

Before computing distances, normalize each feature to the range [0, 1] to prevent variables with large scales from dominating:

\[ x' = \frac{x - \min(A)}{\max(A) - \min(A)} \]

In R (tutorial practical):

normalize <- function(x) {
  return ((x - min(x)) / (max(x) - min(x)))
}

Characteristics of k-NN

PropertyDescription
Lazy learningNo explicit training phase — entire training dataset is memorised
Memory intensiveNeeds to keep the entire training set in memory at prediction time
Best suited forSmaller datasets with few features; continuous predictor variables
Sensitive to kSmall k → noisy; large k → smooth boundaries. Odd k recommended for binary classification
Choice of kk=7 used in Linda Chua practical (Pima diabetes dataset)
Practical tip: Normalize all continuous predictors before k-NN. Categorical variables require special treatment (binary encoding or Hamming distance).

8.5 Artificial Neural Network (ANN)

ANN is inspired by the biological neuron system. It is an adaptive system that adjusts its internal structure (weights) through training to minimise prediction error.

McCulloch-Pitts Neuron (Perceptron)

Weighted sum of inputs → activation function → output:

\[ \text{output} = f\!\left(\sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i w_i + b w_0\right) \]

where \(x_i\) = inputs, \(w_i\) = weights, \(b\) = bias, \(f\) = activation function.

Activation Functions

FunctionFormulaOutput RangeUse
Binary Step \(y = \begin{cases} 0 & x < 0 \\ 1 & x \geq 0 \end{cases}\) {0, 1} Simple perceptron (binary classification)
Sigmoid \(y = \dfrac{1}{1+e^{-x}}\) (0, 1) Probabilistic output; hidden/output layers

Backpropagation — Weight Update Rule

\[ \delta = y_{\text{target}} - y_{\text{output}} \] \[ w_{\text{new}} = w_{\text{old}} + \eta \cdot \delta \cdot x \]

where \(\eta\) = learning rate (controls step size), \(\delta\) = error signal.

ANN Training Algorithm

  1. Initialise all weights at random
  2. For each training example \((x, y_{\text{target}})\):
    • Compute \(y_{\text{output}}\) (forward pass)
    • If \(y_{\text{target}} \neq y_{\text{output}}\): compute \(\delta = y_{\text{target}} - y_{\text{output}}\); update all weights \(w_{\text{new}} = w_{\text{old}} + \eta \cdot \delta \cdot x\)
  3. Repeat Step 2 until convergence or maximum epochs reached

Worked Example — Perceptron Training (Linda Chua 2024)

Training data: logical OR-like gate; η = 0.2; initial weights: w&sub0;=0.5, w&sub1;=−0.3, w&sub2;=1; activation = binary step.

Training Cycle 1InputΣTargetOutputUpdate?Action
(0,0) 1×0.5 + 0×(−0.5) + 0×1 = 0.5 0 1 Yes w&sub0;=0.5+0.2×(0−1)×1=0.3; w&sub1;=−0.5; w&sub2;=1
(0,1) 1×0.3 + 0×(−0.5) + 1×1 = 1.3 1 1 No No change
(1,0) 1×0.3 + 1×(−0.5) + 0×1 = −0.2 1 0 Yes w&sub0;=0.5; w&sub1;=−0.3; w&sub2;=1
(1,1) 1×0.5 + 1×(−0.3) + 1×1 = 1.2 1 1 No No change

Training repeats until all 4 examples produce correct output (no weight updates needed) → convergence.

Network Architectures

ArchitectureDescriptionCapability
Single-Layer Perceptron Input layer + output layer; no hidden layer Linearly separable problems only
Multilayer Perceptron (MLP) Input + one or more hidden layers + output layer Non-linear decision boundaries; XOR, complex patterns
In R (neuralnet package): hidden=1 specifies one hidden layer with one node. Increase hidden for deeper networks. Use linear.output=FALSE for classification.

8.6 Methodology

General ML Workflow

  1. Explore and understand the data — descriptive statistics, class distribution, missing values
  2. Prepare the data — handle missing values, encode categorical variables, normalize (for KNN/ANN)
  3. Split data — into training and test sets (or use cross-validation)
  4. Build model — train on training data
  5. Evaluate model — predict on test data, compute confusion matrix and performance metrics

Train / Test Split

A common strategy is an 80/20 split: 80% for training, 20% for testing.

The test set is held out and never seen during training — it simulates unseen real-world data.

# 80/20 split (Linda Chua practical, n=532 after filtering)
sRow <- floor(nrow(df1) * 0.8)
trainData <- df1[1:sRow, ]
testData  <- df1[(sRow+1):nrow(df1), ]

Pima Indian Diabetes Dataset (Tutorial Practical)

Used in the GET502 practical to demonstrate all three algorithms:

#VariableDescription
1pregnantNumber of times pregnant
2glucosePlasma glucose concentration (2-hr oral glucose tolerance test)
3pressureDiastolic blood pressure (mmHg)
4tricepsTriceps skin fold thickness (mm)
5insulin2-hour serum insulin (μU/ml)
6massBody mass index (kg/m²)
7pedigreeDiabetes pedigree function
8ageAge (years)
9diabetesTarget: pos / neg

n=768 total; n=532 after preprocessing in tutorial. Source: mlbench package.

Required R Packages

PackagePurpose
mlbenchContains PimaIndiansDiabetes dataset
plyrSplit-apply-combine functions (used in k-fold loop)
rpartDecision tree (CART algorithm)
rpart.plotTree visualization
classk-NN classifier (knn())
neuralnetArtificial neural network
caretConfusion matrix and performance metrics

8.7 Cross-Validation

Cross-validation is a resampling technique that evaluates model performance more robustly than a single train/test split by performing multiple experiments with different splits, using each sample in a test set exactly once.

k-Fold Cross-Validation

  1. Partition the dataset into k equal-sized folds
  2. For each fold i (i = 1, 2, ..., k):
    • Use fold i as the test set
    • Use all remaining k−1 folds as the training set
    • Train model → evaluate on test fold → record accuracy
  3. Average the k accuracy values → CV accuracy estimate
Linda Chua practical uses k=10 (10-fold CV) on the Pima diabetes dataset.

10-Fold CV in R

library(plyr)

k <- 10
DTaccuracy <- rep(NA, k)

# Create 10 folds
folds <- split(df1, cut(1:nrow(df1), 10))

# Iterate over each fold
for (i in 1:k) {
  test  <- ldply(folds[i],  data.frame)
  train <- ldply(folds[-i], data.frame)
  test$.id  <- NULL
  train$.id <- NULL

  # Train decision tree on training fold
  DTmodel <- rpart(diabetes ~ ., data=train,
                   method='class',
                   parms=list(split='information'))

  # Predict on test fold
  DTpred <- predict(DTmodel, test, type='class')

  # Record accuracy
  DTaccuracy[i] <- confusionMatrix(DTpred, test$diabetes,
                                    positive='pos')$overall['Accuracy']
}

# Mean CV accuracy
mean(DTaccuracy)

Why Cross-Validation?

IssueSingle Splitk-Fold CV
Variance in estimatesHigh — depends on random splitLow — averages over k splits
Use of dataOnly 80% used for trainingEvery sample used in training k−1 times
Computational costLowk × higher
Overfitting detectionLimitedBetter generalisation estimate
Choosing k: k=10 is conventional in ML literature (Kuhn 2008, caret package). k=5 is also common. Leave-one-out CV (k=n) is computationally expensive but has minimal bias.

8.8 Performance Evaluation

Confusion Matrix

A confusion matrix summarises prediction results by cross-tabulating predicted vs actual class labels:

Actual
Positive (Yes) Negative (No)
Predicted Positive True Positive (TP) False Positive (FP)
Type I error
Negative False Negative (FN)
Type II error		 True Negative (TN)

Performance Metrics

\[ \text{Accuracy} = \frac{TP + TN}{TP + FP + FN + TN} \] \[ \text{Precision (PPV)} = \frac{TP}{TP + FP} \] \[ \text{Recall (Sensitivity)} = \frac{TP}{TP + FN} \]
MetricMeasuresWhen to prioritise
Accuracy Overall correct predictions Balanced classes
Precision (PPV) Of those predicted positive, how many truly are? Cost of false positive is high (e.g., unnecessary surgery)
Recall (Sensitivity) Of all actual positives, how many were caught? Cost of false negative is high (e.g., missed disease diagnosis)
F1 Score Harmonic mean of Precision & Recall Imbalanced classes
Specificity TN / (TN + FP) Complement to sensitivity

Confusion Matrix in R (caret)

library(caret)

# Decision tree evaluation
confusionMatrix(DTpred, testData$diabetes, positive='pos')

# Output includes:
# - Confusion matrix table
# - Accuracy with 95% CI
# - Sensitivity (Recall)
# - Specificity
# - Positive Predictive Value (Precision)
# - Negative Predictive Value
Class imbalance warning: When the target class is imbalanced (e.g., rare disease), accuracy alone is misleading. Prefer AUC-ROC, sensitivity/specificity trade-off, or use sampling strategies (SMOTE, upsampling).

ROC Curve and AUC

The Receiver Operating Characteristic (ROC) curve plots Sensitivity (True Positive Rate) against 1−Specificity (False Positive Rate) across all classification thresholds. The Area Under the Curve (AUC) summarises discriminative ability:

  • AUC = 1.0 → perfect classifier
  • AUC = 0.5 → no discriminative ability (random)
  • AUC ≥ 0.70 is generally considered acceptable in clinical prediction
library(pROC)
roc_obj <- roc(testData$diabetes, as.numeric(DTpred))
auc(roc_obj)
plot(roc_obj, main="ROC Curve - Decision Tree")

8.9 R Implementation — Annotated Code & Explanation

Based on Linda Chua (2024) GET502 ML Tutorial — Pima Indian Diabetes Dataset. Each line is decoded below.

# STEP 1 — Install packages (run once only)
install.packages(c("mlbench","plyr","rpart","rpart.plot",
                   "class","neuralnet","caret","pROC"))

# STEP 2 — Load packages into current session
library(mlbench)     # provides PimaIndiansDiabetes dataset
library(plyr)        # ldply() for fold manipulation in CV
library(rpart)       # rpart() — decision tree builder
library(rpart.plot)  # rpart.plot() — tree visualisation
library(class)       # knn() — k-nearest neighbour classifier
library(neuralnet)   # neuralnet() — artificial neural network
library(caret)       # confusionMatrix() — model evaluation
library(pROC)        # roc(), auc() — ROC curve and AUC

# STEP 3 — Load dataset
data(PimaIndiansDiabetes)   # loads built-in dataset from mlbench
df0 <- PimaIndiansDiabetes  # assign to df0 (768 rows, 9 cols)
summary(df0)                 # quick overview of all variables

# STEP 4 — Remove rows with physiologically impossible zero values
# (zero glucose, pressure, triceps, insulin, or BMI = missing, not true zero)
df1 <- df0[df0$glucose  != 0 &
            df0$pressure != 0 &
            df0$triceps  != 0 &
            df0$insulin  != 0 &
            df0$mass     != 0, ]
# df1 now has n = 532 (removed 236 rows with suspected missing values)
cat("Clean n:", nrow(df1), "\n")

# STEP 5 — Train / Test split  (80% train, 20% test)
set.seed(123)                        # fix random seed for reproducibility
sRow      <- floor(nrow(df1) * 0.8) # last row of training set = floor(532*0.8) = 425
trainData <- df1[1:sRow, ]           # rows 1–425   → training set
testData  <- df1[(sRow+1):nrow(df1), ] # rows 426–532 → test set

cat("Train n:", nrow(trainData), "\n")  # expect 425
cat("Test  n:", nrow(testData),  "\n")  # expect 107
Why filter zeros? In the Pima dataset, variables like glucose and BMI cannot be 0 biologically — zeros were used as a missing-value code. Keeping them distorts model training.
Key variables (predictors): pregnant, glucose, pressure, triceps, insulin, mass (BMI), pedigree, age — 8 continuous predictors.
Outcome: diabetes — factor with levels neg / pos. 
# ── DECISION TREE: Build ─────────────────────────────────────
# rpart(formula, data, method, parms)
#   formula:  diabetes ~ .   means predict diabetes using ALL other columns
#   data:     trainData       the 80% training set
#   method:   'class'         classification tree (not regression)
#   parms:    split='information'  use Entropy / Information Gain as split criterion
#             (default is GINI; 'information' = ID3/C4.5 style)

DTmodel <- rpart(diabetes ~ .,
                 data  = trainData,
                 method = 'class',
                 parms  = list(split = 'information'))

# ── Visualise the tree ────────────────────────────────────────
# type=4:   show split labels at each node
# extra=104: show class probability + percent of obs at each leaf
rpart.plot(DTmodel, type = 4, extra = 104,
           main = "Decision Tree — Pima Diabetes")

# ── Print splitting rules as text ─────────────────────────────
print(DTmodel)         # shows each node: variable, threshold, n, loss
printcp(DTmodel)       # complexity parameter table (for pruning)

# ── DECISION TREE: Evaluate on test set ──────────────────────
# predict() with type='class' returns predicted class labels (neg/pos)
DTpred <- predict(DTmodel, testData, type = 'class')

# confusionMatrix(predicted, actual, positive=)
# positive = 'pos' tells caret which class is the "event" for Sens/Spec
confusionMatrix(DTpred, testData$diabetes, positive = 'pos')
# Key outputs: Accuracy, Sensitivity (recall for pos), Specificity, Kappa

# ── ROC curve and AUC ─────────────────────────────────────────
# type='prob' returns probability for each class (two-column matrix)
# [, 'pos']  extracts the probability column for the positive class
DTprob  <- predict(DTmodel, testData, type = 'prob')[, 'pos']
roc_dt  <- roc(testData$diabetes, DTprob) # roc(response, predictor)
auc(roc_dt)                                # Area Under Curve (0.5–1.0)
plot(roc_dt, main = "ROC — Decision Tree")
ArgumentMeaningAlternatives
method='class'Classification tree'anova' for continuous outcome
split='information'Entropy / Info Gain (ID3)'gini' = CART default
type='class' (predict)Return class labels'prob' returns probabilities
extra=104Show % + class prob at leavesextra=2 shows class counts
Reading confusionMatrix output:
Sensitivity = TP/(TP+FN) — how well it detects true diabetics.
Specificity = TN/(TN+FP) — how well it excludes non-diabetics.
Kappa = agreement above chance (>0.6 = good).
AUC >0.70 acceptable; >0.80 good; >0.90 excellent. 
# ── k-NN STEP 1: Normalise features to [0, 1] ────────────────
# k-NN uses Euclidean distance — variables on different scales would
# dominate the distance calculation if not normalised.
# Formula: x' = (x - min) / (max - min)

normalize <- function(x) {
  return ((x - min(x)) / (max(x) - min(x)))
}

# lapply() applies normalize to each column (1:8 = the 8 predictors)
# as.data.frame() converts the list back to a data frame
trainDataN <- as.data.frame(lapply(trainData[, 1:8], normalize))
testDataN  <- as.data.frame(lapply(testData[,  1:8], normalize))

# IMPORTANT: append class labels back (normalization removed them)
trainDataN$diabetes <- trainData$diabetes
testDataN$diabetes  <- testData$diabetes

# ── k-NN STEP 2: Build and predict ───────────────────────────
# knn(train, test, cl, k)
#   train: normalised training predictors only (columns 1:8)
#   test:  normalised test predictors only
#   cl:    class labels for each training row (column 9 = diabetes)
#   k:     number of nearest neighbours to vote (odd number, avoid ties)
KNNpred <- knn(train = trainDataN[, 1:8],
               test  = testDataN[,  1:8],
               cl    = trainDataN[, 9],
               k     = 7)

confusionMatrix(KNNpred, testDataN$diabetes, positive = 'pos')

# ── k-NN STEP 3: Tune k (find optimal k) ─────────────────────
# Try k = 3, 5, 7, 9, 11 — compare accuracy
k_vals <- c(3, 5, 7, 9, 11)

# sapply: apply function to each k and return a vector
acc_k <- sapply(k_vals, function(k) {
  pred <- knn(trainDataN[, 1:8], testDataN[, 1:8],
              trainDataN[, 9], k = k)
  mean(pred == testDataN$diabetes)  # proportion correct = accuracy
})

# Display results
data.frame(k = k_vals, accuracy = round(acc_k, 3))
Why normalise for k-NN?
Glucose (range ~0–200) vs triceps (range ~0–100): without normalisation, glucose dominates the Euclidean distance. Min-max scaling puts all predictors equally in [0, 1].
Choosing k: Small k (k=1) = high variance (overfitting). Large k = high bias (underfit). Rule of thumb: start with k = √n ≈ √425 ≈ 20, then tune down. Odd k prevents tie votes.
kBehaviour
k=1Memorises training data — overfits
k=3–7Common starting range
k very largeApproaches majority-class prediction
 
# ── ANN: Uses NORMALISED data (trainDataN, testDataN from k-NN step) ──
# neuralnet(formula, data, hidden, linear.output)
#   formula:       list all predictor names explicitly (no ~ . shortcut)
#   data:          normalised training data
#   hidden:        number of nodes in each hidden layer
#                  hidden=1      → 1 hidden layer, 1 node
#                  hidden=c(5,3) → 2 hidden layers (5 nodes, then 3)
#   linear.output: FALSE = sigmoid activation → classification
#                  TRUE  = identity (linear) → regression

NNmodel <- neuralnet(
  diabetes ~ pregnant + glucose + pressure + triceps +
             insulin + mass + pedigree + age,
  data          = trainDataN,
  hidden        = 1,          # simplest architecture: 1 hidden node
  linear.output = FALSE       # classification
)

# Visualise network weights
plot(NNmodel)   # nodes = circles; widths = weight magnitudes

# ── ANN: Evaluate ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# predict() returns a matrix: col 1 = P(neg), col 2 = P(pos)
NNprob <- predict(NNmodel, testDataN)

# Convert probabilities to class labels using 0.5 threshold
# ifelse(condition, value_if_TRUE, value_if_FALSE)
NNpred <- ifelse(NNprob[, 2] > 0.5, 'pos', 'neg')

# Convert character vector to factor with same levels as actual
NNpred <- factor(NNpred, levels = c('neg', 'pos'))

confusionMatrix(NNpred, testDataN$diabetes, positive = 'pos')

# ── ANN: Try deeper architecture ──────────────────────────────
# hidden=5 = 1 hidden layer with 5 nodes
NNmodel2 <- neuralnet(
  diabetes ~ pregnant + glucose + pressure + triceps +
             insulin + mass + pedigree + age,
  data          = trainDataN,
  hidden        = 5,
  linear.output = FALSE
)
plot(NNmodel2)
ANN weight update rule (backpropagation):
\[ w_{\text{new}} = w_{\text{old}} + \eta \cdot \delta \cdot x \] where \(\eta\) = learning rate, \(\delta\) = error signal, \(x\) = input.
The neuralnet package uses resilient backpropagation (rprop) by default.
hidden=ArchitectureNotes
18 → 1 → 2Simplest; fast; may underfit complex data
58 → 5 → 2More capacity; watch for overfitting
c(5,3)8 → 5 → 3 → 2Deep network; slow; needs more data
Why linear.output = FALSE? For classification, the output layer needs a sigmoid (logistic) activation to produce probabilities between 0 and 1. Setting TRUE uses a linear output — only appropriate for regression. 
# ── 10-FOLD CROSS-VALIDATION (CV) ─────────────────────────────
# Purpose: more reliable accuracy estimate than a single train/test split.
# Process: split data into 10 equal folds, rotate which fold is test set.

library(plyr)  # for ldply()

k           <- 10            # number of folds
DTaccuracy  <- rep(NA, k)    # empty vector, length 10
KNNaccuracy <- rep(NA, k)    # empty vector, length 10

# split(df1, cut(1:nrow(df1), k)):
#   1:nrow(df1) = row indices 1..532
#   cut(..., k) = divide into k equal intervals (labels 1–10)
#   split()     = divide df1 into 10 sub-data-frames by those labels
folds <- split(df1, cut(1:nrow(df1), k))  # list of 10 data frames

# ── CV Loop ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
for (i in 1:k) {

  # ldply(folds[i], data.frame):  combine fold i into one data frame
  # ldply(folds[-i], data.frame): combine all OTHER folds (9 folds)
  test  <- ldply(folds[i],  data.frame); test$.id  <- NULL  # remove fold-id column
  train <- ldply(folds[-i], data.frame); train$.id <- NULL  # remove fold-id column

  # ── Decision Tree for this fold ──────────────────────────────
  DTmod <- rpart(diabetes ~ ., data = train,
                 method = 'class',
                 parms  = list(split = 'information'))

  DTp <- predict(DTmod, test, type = 'class')  # predict on test fold

  # $overall['Accuracy'] extracts just the accuracy value from confusionMatrix
  DTaccuracy[i] <- confusionMatrix(DTp, test$diabetes,
                                    positive = 'pos')$overall['Accuracy']

  # ── k-NN for this fold (normalise within fold!) ───────────────
  # IMPORTANT: fit normalisation on TRAINING fold, apply to BOTH
  # (avoids data leakage from test fold into normalisation parameters)
  norm_fn  <- function(x) (x - min(x)) / (max(x) - min(x))
  trainN   <- as.data.frame(lapply(train[, 1:8], norm_fn))
  testN    <- as.data.frame(lapply(test[,  1:8], norm_fn))

  KNNp <- knn(trainN, testN, train$diabetes, k = 7)
  KNNaccuracy[i] <- mean(KNNp == test$diabetes)  # direct accuracy
}

# ── Summarise CV results ───────────────────────────────────────
results <- data.frame(
  Fold    = 1:k,
  DT_Acc  = round(DTaccuracy,  3),
  KNN_Acc = round(KNNaccuracy, 3)
)
print(results)

cat("\nMean DT  Accuracy:", round(mean(DTaccuracy),  3), "\n")
cat("Mean KNN Accuracy:", round(mean(KNNaccuracy), 3), "\n")
cat("SD   DT  Accuracy:", round(sd(DTaccuracy),    3), "\n")
cat("SD   KNN Accuracy:", round(sd(KNNaccuracy),   3), "\n")
How 10-fold CV works:
Data split into 10 equal parts. In each iteration, 1 fold = test, 9 folds = train. Repeat 10×, rotating test fold each time. Final accuracy = mean of 10 accuracy values. SD shows stability.
Critical: normalise INSIDE the loop. If you normalise using min/max from the FULL dataset before the loop, information from the test fold leaks into the training process — this is data leakage and inflates accuracy estimates. Always fit preprocessing on train fold, then apply the same transformation to test fold.
FunctionWhat it does
split(df, cut(1:n, k))Divides data frame into k equal folds
ldply(folds[i], data.frame)Extracts fold i as a data frame
ldply(folds[-i], data.frame)Combines all folds EXCEPT i
$overall['Accuracy']Extracts accuracy from confusionMatrix object
rep(NA, k)Creates empty vector of length k to store results

Dataset coronary.csv: events (coronary disease count), agecat, tenure, personyears (cumulative follow-up). Hypothesis: events related to agecat and tenure.

3 marksCorrect IRR interpretation for tenure
2 marksState model equation in log form
Total: ~20 marks
R Code: 
chd <- read_csv("coronary.csv")
model_chd1 <- glm(events ~ agecat + tenure + offset(log(personyears)),
                   data=chd, family=poisson(link="log"))
tbl_regression(model_chd1, exponentiate=TRUE)
Model equation:
log(events/personyear) = −0.924 − 0.07(agecat30–39) − 0.12(agecat40–49) − 0.08(agecat50–59) − 0.01(agecat60–69) − 0.14(agecat≥70) − 0.05(tenureshort)

IRR interpretations (3 marks each — must include all 3 elements per variable):
  • Age 30–39: IRR = 1.08 (95% CI 0.93–1.25, p=0.333) — not significant, adjusting for tenure
  • Age 40–49: IRR = 0.89 (95% CI 0.77–1.03, p=0.128) — not significant
  • Short tenure: IRR = 0.95 (95% CI 0.87–1.04, p=0.243) — not significant, adjusting for age
3-element rule per interpretation (1 mark each): (1) direction + IRR value, (2) 95% CI, (3) p-value + significance statement + "adjusting for [covariates]"
(b) State analysis of choice. Justify. ~10 marks
▶ Marking Scheme
2 marksCorrect identification: Poisson regression — rate model
2 marksOutcome is a non-negative count (number of coronary events) — non-negative integer distributed as Poisson
2 marksSubjects contribute different follow-up times (personyears) → rate model needed, not count model → use offset(log(personyears))
2 marksPoisson assumes mean = variance (equidispersion); allows computation of Incidence Rate Ratios (IRR)
2 marksEnables investigation of relationship between count outcome and multiple categorical/continuous predictors
Total: 10 marks

▶ Marking Scheme

Total: 10 marks

Q3 Parametric vs Cox PH Survival 15 marks
Discuss the advantages and disadvantages of Cox Proportional Hazard analysis and Parametric Survival Analysis.
▶ Marking Scheme — comparison discussion; 1–2 marks per valid point (max 15)
2 marksCox PH — Advantage: Semi-parametric — baseline hazard h₀(t) left unspecified; no distributional assumption required; robust and widely accepted
2 marksCox PH — Disadvantage: Must satisfy PH assumption (HR constant over time); verify with cox.zph(); if violated → stratified Cox or Extended Cox with time-dependent covariates needed
1 markCox PH — Limitation: Cannot predict absolute survival times without baseline hazard estimate
2 marksParametric — Advantage: Fully specifies distribution (Weibull, Exponential, Log-logistic, Log-normal); can predict absolute survival times at any quantile (25th, 50th, 75th percentile)
2 marksParametric — Advantage: AFT parameterisation gives Time Ratio (TR) — directly interpretable as factor by which survival time is accelerated/decelerated; more informative than HR in some contexts
2 marksParametric — Disadvantage: Sensitive to wrong distributional assumption; must verify distribution fit with diagnostics (AIC comparison, eha::check.dist(), WeibullDiag())
2 marksComparison — coefficient interpretation: Cox gives HR; Parametric AFT gives TR; Parametric PH gives HR. TR < 1 = shorter survival time; HR > 1 = higher hazard rate
2 marksWhen to choose parametric: (i) distribution known/verified to fit; (ii) absolute time prediction required; (iii) PH assumption violated; (iv) more efficient estimator if distribution correctly specified
Total: 15 marks (award up to 2 marks per point; max 15)

Q1 Parametric Survival Analysis 30 marks
Perform Exponential and Weibull parametric survival analysis (mastectomy vs BCS breast-conserving surgery dataset).
(a) Results in both tables — Exponential and Weibull. 10 marks
▶ Marking Scheme
2 marksCorrect R code for Exponential model: survreg(Surv(time,status)~mastectomy, dist="exponential") + ConvertWeibull()
2 marksCorrect R code for Weibull model: survreg(Surv(time,status)~mastectomy, dist="weibull") + ConvertWeibull()
3 marksExponential table: correct coefficient, SE, p-value, TR (AF), 95% CI for TR, HR, 95% CI for HR
3 marksWeibull table: same columns as above — correct values
Total: 10 marks
library(survival); library(SurvRegCensCov)
# Exponential
exp.mod <- survreg(Surv(time,status) ~ mastectomy, data=df, dist="exponential")
ConvertWeibull(exp.mod, conf.level=0.95)
# Weibull (AFT)
wei.mod <- survreg(Surv(time,status) ~ mastectomy, data=df, dist="weibull")
ConvertWeibull(wei.mod, conf.level=0.95)
Required table columns: Variable | Coeff | SE | p-value | TR (AF) | 95% CI (TR) | HR | 95% CI (HR)
(b) Interpret results. 10 marks
▶ Marking Scheme — 5 marks per model (Exponential + Weibull)
2 marksTR interpretation (per model): TR = 0.644 (Exp) or 0.701 (Weibull) — "mastectomy patients have survival time 0.644× that of BCS; survival time shortened by 36% (Exp) or 30% (Weibull)"
1 mark95% CI for TR: state confidence interval and what it means (plausible range for the true TR)
2 marksHR interpretation (per model): HR = 1.55 (Exp) / 1.57 (Weibull) — "mastectomy patients are dying at 1.55× the rate of BCS patients (95% CI 1.37–1.75)"
Total: 10 marks (5 per model × 2 models)
Model answers (from PYQ marking scheme):
ExponentialWeibull
TR (mastectomy)0.644 (shortened by 36%)0.701 (shortened by 30%)
HR (mastectomy)1.55 (95% CI 1.37–1.75)1.57 (95% CI 1.39–1.78)
TR < 1 interpretation rule: TR = 0.644 → survival time is 0.644× shorter → event arrives faster → survival shortened by (1−0.644) = 35.6% ≈ 36%.
HR > 1: mastectomy group has 1.55× the instantaneous hazard rate (dying faster) compared to BCS.
(c) Predict survival at 25th, 50th, 75th percentile. 10 marks
▶ Marking Scheme

Must state: "25th failure percentile = 75th survival percentile → 75% of mastectomy patients survive beyond 53.2 days; 50% survive beyond 128.2 days (median); 25% survive beyond 256.4 days" — 1 mark deducted if stated as "25% survive X days" without the complement

Quick Reference for Exam

🎯 Topic–Method–R Function Mapping

OutcomeMethodKey R FunctionTopic
Binary (2 categories)Binary Logistic Regressionglm(family=binomial)Topic 1
Nominal >2 (unordered)Multinomial LRmultinom(), vglm()Topic 2
Ordinal (ordered categories)Ordinal LR (PO model)clm(), polr()Topic 3
Count (no exposure offset)Poisson Count Regressionglm(family=poisson)Topic 4
Count (with personyears)Poisson Rate Regressionglm + offset(log(PY))Topic 4
Correlated / clusteredGEE / GLMMgee(), glmer()Topic 5
Time-to-event (semi-param)Cox PH / Extended Coxcoxph()Topic 6
Time-to-event (parametric)Exponential / Weibull AFTsurvreg(), flexsurvreg()Topic 7
Categorical class (prediction)DT / KNN / ANNrpart(), knn(), neuralnet()Topic 8

🌟 Marks-Earning Formula for Every Interpretation

3-element rule (1 mark each) — applies to ALL regression interpretations:
  1. Direction + magnitude: "X group has IRR/OR/HR/TR = [value] compared to [reference group]"
  2. 95% Confidence Interval: "(95% CI [Lower]–[Upper])"
  3. Significance + adjustment: "(p=[value]; statistically significant/not significant), adjusting for [all other covariates]"
Missing any one element → lose that mark. Missing "adjusting for" / "controlling for" → common mark loss. (H&L uses "controlling for [covariates] in the model"; both accepted in marking.)

⚠ High-Mark-Loss Mistakes

Common errors (1–2 marks lost each)
✗ SurvivalStating "25% of patients survive X days" instead of "75% survive beyond X days" — confusing failure time with survival time (S = 1−F)
✗ PoissonUsing count model when personyears/offset is given — always use offset(log(personyears)) for rate model
✗ MultinomialCalling output "OR" instead of "RRR" (Relative Risk Ratio)
✗ OrdinalForgetting to state "adjusting for [covariate]" in interpretation
✗ PO testStating p>0.05 means "assumption violated" — it means assumption IS met (fail to reject H₀)
✗ Model compareConcluding complex model is always better — if LRT NS → prefer simpler model (parsimony)
✗ TR vs HRTR < 1 = shorter survival (bad); TR > 1 = longer survival (good). Not the same as HR direction.

📋 Standard Justification Phrases (memorise these)

Poisson count: "Outcome is a non-negative integer count; Poisson distribution assumes mean = variance; log link models multiplicative effects; produces IRR."

Poisson rate: "[Above] + subjects contribute different follow-up times (personyears) → rate model with offset(log(personyears)) needed."

Multinomial LR: "Outcome has >2 categories with no natural ordering (nominal); binary LR limited to 2 categories; ordinal LR requires ordering; multinomial LR produces RRR per outcome category vs reference."

Ordinal LR: "Outcome has >2 ordered categories; ordinal structure preserved; proportional odds model uses single set of coefficients across all cut-points."

Parametric over Cox: "Distribution verified to fit data (AIC; diagnostic plots); absolute survival time prediction required; Cox cannot predict survival times without baseline hazard."

Model parsimony: "LRT p > 0.05 → complex model does not significantly improve fit → simpler model preferred on grounds of parsimony (fewer parameters, less overfitting risk)."

📊 Decision Tree — PlayTennis Exact Values

S = 14 (9 Yes, 5 No): Entropy(S) = 0.940
Gain(Outlook) = 0.246 → selected as root
Gain(Humidity) = 0.151  |  Gain(Wind) = 0.048  |  Gain(Temp) = 0.029
Sunny branch (n=5): Gain(Sunny, Humidity) = 0.971 → perfect split (High=No, Normal=Yes)
GET502 Advanced Categorical Analysis · Interpretation Compendium

Interpretation Guide — Templates & Worked Examples

Fill-in-the-blank templates · 3-element rule · Verbatim example sentences

T1 — Multinomial Logistic Regression: Interpretation Guide (RRR)

Applied Logistic Regression Conventions (Hosmer, Lemeshow & Sturdivant, 2013)
  • Always write “estimated odds ratio” — never omit “estimated.”
  • State the comparison explicitly: “… for [Group A] compared to [Reference B], controlling for [covariates] in the model.”
  • For multinomial the exponentiated coefficient is the Relative Risk Ratio (RRR) — odds of being in outcome category X vs reference category REF, for a one-unit change in the predictor.
  • Reference outcome category and reference predictor level must both be declared.
  • Each non-reference outcome level produces a separate set of RRR estimates.
🔷 Categorical Predictor — H&L Template

“The estimated odds of [outcome category X] versus [reference outcome REF] for subjects with [Category A] is Ψ̂ = [RRR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], times the estimated odds for subjects with [Reference Category B], controlling for [covariates] in the model.”

RRR > 1: Subjects in [A] have higher estimated odds of being in [X] than [REF] relative to [B].
RRR < 1: Subjects in [A] have lower estimated odds; express as [1−RRR×100]% reduction.

Categorical Example — Sex on Transport Mode (reference outcome: car)

Output: RRR for female vs male (bus vs car) = 2.34, 95% CI 1.12–4.89, p = 0.024.

H&L-style sentence: “The estimated odds of choosing bus versus car for female subjects is Ψ̂ = 2.34 (95% CI: 1.12–4.89), p = 0.024, times the estimated odds for male subjects, controlling for income in the model. Female subjects thus have 2.34 times the estimated odds of bus over car use compared to males.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor — H&L Template

“The estimated odds ratio of [outcome X vs REF] for a c-unit increase in [predictor X] is exp(c × β̂) = [RRR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates] in the model.”

Choose c to be clinically meaningful (e.g., c = 1,000 for income in RM, c = 5 for BMI in kg/m²).

Numerical Example — Income (c = RM1,000 increase) on Transport Mode

Output: β̂ = −0.198 per RM1 → exp(1000 × −0.000198) = 0.82, 95% CI 0.71–0.95, p = 0.007.

H&L sentence: “The estimated odds ratio of choosing bus versus car for a RM1,000 increase in monthly income is exp(c×β̂) = 0.82 (95% CI: 0.71–0.95), p = 0.007, controlling for sex in the model. A RM1,000 increase in income is associated with an 18% reduction in the estimated odds of bus over car use.”

🔀 Interaction Term — H&L Template

H&L (Chapter 3.4): when an interaction is present, the estimated OR for [A] depends on the level of [B]:

Ψ̂(A | B = b) = exp(β̂A + β̂AB × b)

“Because the interaction term is significant (p = [p]), the estimated odds ratio for [Predictor A] is not constant but depends on the value of [Predictor B]. The estimated OR for [A] at [B = level 1] is [OR₁] (95% CI: …) and at [B = level 2] is [OR₂] (95% CI: …), controlling for [other covariates] in the model. These stratum-specific estimates should be reported rather than a single pooled OR.”

Interaction Example — Sex × Income on Transport Mode (bus vs car)

Output: Interaction RRR (female × income per RM1,000) = 1.23, 95% CI 1.04–1.45, p = 0.015.

H&L sentence: “Because the product term sex × income is significant (p = 0.015), the estimated odds ratio for income on bus versus car choice is not constant across sex. Among females: exp(β̂income + β̂sex×income) = 1.01 (95% CI: 0.94–1.08); among males: exp(β̂income) = 0.82 (95% CI: 0.71–0.95), controlling for age in the model. Stratum-specific ORs are presented as the interaction precludes a single summary estimate.”

R Code — VGAM (Primary) + nnet::multinom (Alternative)
# ── VGAM (primary) ──────────────────────────────────────
library(VGAM)
df$income_k <- df$income / 1000         # c = RM1,000 unit
df$transport <- relevel(factor(df$transport), ref="car")

m1 <- vglm(transport ~ sex + income_k, family=multinomial, data=df)
summary(m1)            # log-RRR, Wald z, p-value per outcome level
exp(coef(m1))          # RRR — rows named "sex:1","sex:2" per outcome
exp(confint(m1))       # 95% Wald CI
lrtest(m1)             # overall LR test vs intercept-only

# Interaction
m2 <- vglm(transport ~ sex * income_k, family=multinomial, data=df)
lrtest(m1, m2)         # LR test for interaction
exp(coef(m2))          # stratum-specific RRR via linear combination

# Stratum-specific ORs (H&L recommendation)
m_female <- vglm(transport ~ income_k, family=multinomial,
                 data=subset(df, sex=="female"))
m_male   <- vglm(transport ~ income_k, family=multinomial,
                 data=subset(df, sex=="male"))
exp(coef(m_female)); exp(coef(m_male))

# ── nnet::multinom (alternative) ────────────────────────
library(nnet)
m_nn <- multinom(transport ~ sex + income_k, data=df, ref="car")
exp(coef(m_nn)); exp(confint(m_nn))
z <- summary(m_nn)$coefficients / summary(m_nn)$standard.errors
p <- (1 - pnorm(abs(z), 0, 1)) * 2

T2 — Ordinal Logistic Regression: Interpretation Guide (Proportional Odds OR)

Applied Logistic Regression Conventions — Chapter 8 (H&L, 2013)
  • The model estimates a single OR that is assumed constant across all cut-points (proportional odds assumption).
  • H&L template: “The estimated OR of [outcome ≥ j] for [Group A] vs [Reference B] is Ψ̂ = [OR] (95% CI: …), controlling for [covariates] in the model.”
  • H&L emphasise the cumulative odds framing: OR refers to the odds of being at or above a given category.
  • Always verify the proportional odds assumption: nominal_test() (clm) or Brant test (polr). Report the test result.
  • If assumption violated for a predictor → use partial proportional odds (nominal = ~predictor in clm); that predictor then has cut-point-specific ORs.
🔷 Categorical Predictor — H&L Template (Chapter 8)

“The estimated odds of [outcome ≥ category j] for subjects with [Category A] is Ψ̂ = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], times the estimated odds for subjects with [Reference Category B], controlling for [covariates] in the model. This estimate is assumed constant across all cut-points j under the proportional odds model.”

OR > 1: [A] have higher estimated odds of being in a higher [outcome] category than [B].
OR < 1: [A] have lower estimated odds; express as [1−OR×100]% reduction in the odds of a more severe category.

Categorical Example — Treatment on Pain Severity (1=mild, 2=moderate, 3=severe)

Output (clm): OR = 0.38, 95% CI 0.19–0.76, p = 0.007. nominal_test: p = 0.43 (PO met).

H&L sentence: “The estimated odds of being in a higher pain severity category (moderate or severe vs mild, or severe vs mild/moderate) for subjects receiving treatment is Ψ̂ = 0.38 (95% CI: 0.19–0.76), p = 0.007, times the estimated odds for placebo subjects, controlling for age and sex in the model. This represents a 62% reduction in the estimated odds of a more severe pain category. The proportional odds assumption was satisfied (nominal test, p = 0.43), supporting a single summary OR across cut-points.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor — H&L Template

“The estimated odds ratio of [outcome ≥ category j] for a c-unit increase in [predictor X] is exp(c × β̂) = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates] in the model.”

Note: in clm(), a positive coefficient means higher [X] is associated with higher odds of a more severe category. In polr() the sign convention is reversed (negative β = higher X → higher category); always check direction against the data.

Numerical Example — Age (per year) on Disability Severity

Output: OR = 1.04 per year (clm), 95% CI 1.02–1.07, p = 0.001. nominal_test: p = 0.38.

H&L sentence: “The estimated odds ratio of being in a higher disability severity category for a one-year increase in age is exp(β̂) = 1.04 (95% CI: 1.02–1.07), p = 0.001, controlling for sex and treatment in the model. The proportional odds assumption for age was satisfied (nominal test, p = 0.38).”

For c = 10 years: exp(10 × β̂) = 1.0410 = 1.48 → “… for a 10-year increase in age the estimated OR is 1.48 (95% CI: …).”

🔀 Interaction Term — H&L Template

H&L (§8.2, Chapter 3.4): when an interaction is significant, the estimated OR depends on the level of the effect modifier:

Ψ̂(A | B = b) = exp(β̂A + β̂AB × b)

“Because the product term [A] × [B] is significant (p = [p]), the estimated odds ratio for [A] is not constant but varies with [B]. At [B = level 1], Ψ̂ = [OR₁] (95% CI: …); at [B = level 2], Ψ̂ = [OR₂] (95% CI: …), controlling for [covariates] in the model. Stratum-specific ORs should be presented.”

Interaction Example — Treatment × Age on Pain Severity

Output: Interaction OR = 1.03 (treatment × age), 95% CI 1.01–1.06, p = 0.012 (clm).

H&L sentence: “Because the product term treatment × age is statistically significant (p = 0.012), the estimated odds ratio of higher pain severity for treatment is not constant across age. At age 30: Ψ̂ = exp(β̂trt + β̂trt×age × 30) = 0.29 (95% CI: …); at age 60: Ψ̂ = 0.68 (95% CI: …), controlling for sex in the model. The protective effect of treatment on severe pain diminishes with age.”

R Code — ordinal::clm (Primary) + MASS::polr (Alternative)
# ── ordinal::clm (primary) ──────────────────────────────
library(ordinal)
df$pain <- ordered(df$pain, levels=c("mild","moderate","severe"))

m1 <- clm(pain ~ treatment + age + sex, link="logit", data=df)
summary(m1)            # coefficients (log-OR); thresholds at top, predictors below
exp(coef(m1))          # OR — subset to predictor rows only (exclude thresholds)
exp(confint(m1))       # 95% profile-likelihood CI

# Test PO assumption (H&L recommendation: report per predictor)
nominal_test(m1)       # p > 0.05 per predictor = proportional odds met
scale_test(m1)         # tests scale/heteroscedasticity effects

# Stratum-specific ORs for interaction (H&L §3.4)
m2 <- clm(pain ~ treatment * age + sex, link="logit", data=df)
anova(m1, m2)          # LR test for interaction term
# Compute stratum-specific OR at age=30 and age=60:
# OR(trt | age=30) = exp(b_trt + b_trt_age * 30)
b <- coef(m2)
exp(b["treatment1"] + b["treatment1:age"] * 30)
exp(b["treatment1"] + b["treatment1:age"] * 60)

# Partial PO if assumption violated for one predictor
m_ppo <- clm(pain ~ age + sex, nominal=~treatment, link="logit", data=df)
summary(m_ppo)         # treatment gets cut-point-specific ORs

# ── MASS::polr (alternative) ────────────────────────────
library(MASS); library(brant)
m_polr <- polr(pain ~ treatment + age + sex, data=df, Hess=TRUE)
exp(coef(m_polr)); exp(confint(m_polr))
ct <- coef(summary(m_polr))
p  <- pnorm(abs(ct[,"t value"]), lower.tail=FALSE)*2
brant(m_polr)          # Brant test: p > 0.05 = PO met

T3 — Conditional Logistic Regression: Interpretation Guide (Matched OR)

Applied Logistic Regression Conventions — Chapter 7 (H&L, 2013)
  • H&L: the conditional logistic likelihood removes the matched-set intercepts — the resulting estimated OR is a within-set comparison of cases vs controls.
  • Template: “The estimated odds of [outcome = case] for subjects with [Exposure A] is Ψ̂ = [OR] (95% CI: …) times the estimated odds for subjects with [Reference B], within matched [pairs / sets], controlling for [additional covariates] in the model.”
  • Always state matching variables (controlled by design, in strata()) and any additional covariates (in the model formula).
  • Concordant pairs contribute no information; only discordant pairs identify the OR — note this in Methods.
🔷 Categorical Predictor — H&L Template (Chapter 7)

“The estimated odds of [outcome = case] for subjects with [Exposure A] is Ψ̂ = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], times the estimated odds for subjects with [Reference B], within matched [pairs / sets], controlling for [additional covariates] in the model.”

OR > 1: Subjects with [A] have greater estimated odds of being a case within matched pairs.
OR < 1: Subjects with [A] have lower estimated odds; express as [1−OR×100]% reduction.

Categorical Example — Smoking on Myocardial Infarction (1:1 age–sex matched)

Output: Conditional OR = 3.12, 95% CI 1.54–6.32, p = 0.002 (clogit).

H&L sentence: “The estimated odds of myocardial infarction for smokers is Ψ̂ = 3.12 (95% CI: 1.54–6.32), p = 0.002, times the estimated odds for non-smokers, within age- and sex-matched pairs, controlling for BMI and hypertension in the model.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor — H&L Template

“The estimated odds ratio of [outcome = case] for a c-unit increase in [predictor X] is exp(c × β̂) = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], within matched [pairs / sets], controlling for [additional covariates] in the model.”

Numerical Example — BMI (c = 5 kg/m²) on Myocardial Infarction

Output: β̂ = 0.051 per 1 kg/m² → exp(5 × 0.051) = 1.28, 95% CI 1.10–1.48, p = 0.001.

H&L sentence: “The estimated odds ratio of myocardial infarction for a 5 kg/m² increase in BMI is exp(5 × β̂) = 1.28 (95% CI: 1.10–1.48), p = 0.001, within age- and sex-matched pairs, controlling for smoking and hypertension in the model.”

🔀 Interaction Term — H&L Template

H&L (§7.4 / §3.4): when the product term is significant, the estimated OR for [A] depends on [B]:

Ψ̂(A | B = b) = exp(β̂A + β̂AB × b)

“Because the product term [A] × [B] is significant (p = [p]), the estimated OR for [A] within matched sets is not constant. At [B = level 1]: Ψ̂ = [OR₁] (95% CI: …); at [B = level 2]: Ψ̂ = [OR₂] (95% CI: …), controlling for [covariates] in the model. Stratum-specific ORs should be reported (H&L §3.4).”

Interaction Example — Smoking × Hypertension on Myocardial Infarction

Output: β̂smoking = 1.14, β̂htn = 0.88, β̂smoking×htn = 0.88. OR interaction = 2.41, p = 0.031.

H&L sentence: “Because the product term smoking × hypertension is significant (p = 0.031), the estimated OR for smoking is not constant across hypertension status within matched pairs. Among hypertensive subjects: Ψ̂ = exp(β̂smoking + β̂smoking×htn) = 6.89 (95% CI: …); among normotensive subjects: Ψ̂ = exp(β̂smoking) = 3.12 (95% CI: 1.54–6.32), controlling for BMI in the model. Stratum-specific ORs are presented as the interaction precludes a single summary estimate.”

R Code
library(survival)
df$bmi5 <- df$bmi / 5   # c = 5 kg/m2 unit

m1 <- clogit(mi ~ smoking + bmi5 + htn + strata(matchID), data=df)
summary(m1)            # exp(coef) = conditional OR, 95% CI, p-value

# Interaction
m2 <- clogit(mi ~ smoking * htn + bmi5 + strata(matchID), data=df)
anova(m1, m2)          # LR test for interaction

# Stratum-specific ORs (H&L recommendation)
b <- coef(m2)
exp(b["smoking"])                          # OR among normotensive (htn=0)
exp(b["smoking"] + b["smoking:htn"])       # OR among hypertensive (htn=1)

# Or fit separately within each stratum
clogit(mi ~ smoking + bmi5 + strata(matchID), data=df[df$htn==1,])
clogit(mi ~ smoking + bmi5 + strata(matchID), data=df[df$htn==0,])

T4 — Poisson Regression (Count): Interpretation Guide (IRR)

3-Element Rule — Dr Wan’s Structure
  1. Direction + IRR value
  2. 95% CI
  3. p-value + “controlling for …”

Check overdispersion (deviance/df). If >1.5 → quasi-Poisson. Same IRR, wider CI. Interpretation sentence is identical.

🔷 Categorical Predictor Template (Dr Wan sentence)

“[Group A] had an incidence rate of [outcome] that was [IRR] times [higher / lower] compared to [Reference Group B] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates].”

Alternative phrasing: “The incidence rate of [outcome] was [IRR−1×100]% [higher / lower] in [A] compared to [B] (IRR = [IRR], 95% CI: …, p = …), controlling for …”

Categorical Example — Age Group on Asthma Hospital Visits

Output: IRR (elderly vs young) = 1.87, 95% CI 1.43–2.44, p < 0.001.

Dr Wan sentence: “The elderly group had an incidence rate of asthma hospital visits that was 1.87 times higher compared to the young group (95% CI: 1.43–2.44, p < 0.001), controlling for sex and comorbidities.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor Template

“For every one unit increase in [continuous predictor], the incidence rate of [outcome] [increased / decreased] by a factor of [IRR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates].”

IRR > 1: “… increased by [IRR−1×100]% per unit increase.”
IRR < 1: “… decreased by [1−IRR×100]% per unit increase.”

Numerical Example — Duration of Exposure (per year) on Asthma Visits

Output: IRR = 1.06, 95% CI 1.03–1.09, p < 0.001.

Interpretation: “For every one-year increase in duration of occupational exposure, the incidence rate of asthma hospital visits increased by 6% (IRR = 1.06, 95% CI: 1.03–1.09, p < 0.001), controlling for age group and sex.”

🔀 Interaction Term Template

“The effect of [Predictor A] on the incidence rate of [outcome] was significantly modified by [Predictor B] (interaction IRR = [value], 95% CI: [lower]–[upper], p = [p]). Among those with [B = level 1], the IRR for [A] was [IRR₁]; among [B = level 2], IRR = [IRR₂].”

Interaction Example — Age Group × Sex on Asthma Visits

Output: Interaction (elderly × female): IRR = 1.34, 95% CI 1.08–1.66, p = 0.008.

Interpretation: “The effect of age group on asthma visit rates was modified by sex (interaction IRR = 1.34, 95% CI: 1.08–1.66, p = 0.008). Among females, elderly individuals had a 1.34-fold higher incidence rate compared to elderly males, suggesting a greater age-related burden in females controlling for comorbidities.”

R Code
m1 <- glm(visits ~ agegroup + duration + sex, family=poisson, data=df)
exp(coef(m1)); exp(confint(m1))
summary(m1)            # check residual deviance/df for overdispersion

# Quasi-Poisson if overdispersed (deviance/df > 1.5)
mq <- glm(visits ~ agegroup + duration + sex, family=quasipoisson, data=df)
exp(coef(mq)); exp(confint(mq))

# Interaction
m2 <- glm(visits ~ agegroup * sex + duration, family=poisson, data=df)
anova(m1, m2, test="Chisq")    # LR test
exp(coef(m2))          # "agegroupelderly:sexfemale" row = interaction IRR

T4R — Poisson Regression (Rate + Offset): Interpretation Guide (IRR)

3-Element Rule — Rate Model
  1. IRR + direction — events per person-time / population
  2. 95% CI
  3. p-value + denominator unit + “controlling for …”

Always state the denominator unit (per 100,000 population, per person-year, etc.) in the sentence.

🔷 Categorical Predictor Template (Rate)

“[Group A] had an incidence rate of [outcome] that was [IRR] times [higher / lower] per [denominator unit] compared to [Reference Group B] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates].”

Categorical Example — Urban vs Rural TB Notification Rate (per 100,000 population)

Output: IRR = 2.31, 95% CI 1.88–2.84, p < 0.001.

Interpretation: “Urban districts had a TB notification rate that was 2.31 times higher per 100,000 population compared to rural districts (95% CI: 1.88–2.84, p < 0.001), controlling for poverty index and health facility density.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor Template (Rate)

“For every one unit increase in [continuous predictor], the incidence rate of [outcome] per [denominator unit] [increased / decreased] by a factor of [IRR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates].”

Numerical Example — Poverty Index (per 10 points) on TB Rate

Output: IRR = 1.18, 95% CI 1.11–1.26, p < 0.001 (per 10-point increase).

Interpretation: “For every 10-point increase in the poverty index, the TB notification rate per 100,000 population increased by 18% (IRR = 1.18, 95% CI: 1.11–1.26, p < 0.001), controlling for urban/rural status and health facility density.”

🔀 Interaction Term Template (Rate)

“The effect of [Predictor A] on the [outcome] rate per [denominator] was modified by [Predictor B] (interaction IRR = [value], 95% CI: [lower]–[upper], p = [p]). Rate IRRs by stratum: [B = level 1]: [IRR₁]; [B = level 2]: [IRR₂].”

Interaction Example — Urban/Rural × Poverty on TB Rate

Output: Interaction (urban × poverty per 10 points): IRR = 1.09, 95% CI 1.01–1.18, p = 0.027.

Interpretation: “The effect of poverty on TB notification rates was significantly modified by urbanisation status (interaction IRR = 1.09, 95% CI: 1.01–1.18, p = 0.027). The impact of poverty on TB rates was greater in urban areas, suggesting urban poverty contributes disproportionately to TB burden per 100,000 population.”

R Code
df$poverty10 <- df$poverty / 10   # scale continuous predictor

# Rate model with offset
m1 <- glm(tb_cases ~ urban + poverty10 + health_density
          + offset(log(population/100000)),
          family=poisson, data=df)
exp(coef(m1)); exp(confint(m1))

# Interaction
m2 <- glm(tb_cases ~ urban * poverty10 + health_density
          + offset(log(population/100000)),
          family=poisson, data=df)
anova(m1, m2, test="Chisq")
exp(coef(m2))          # "urban1:poverty10" row = interaction IRR

T5 — GEE & GLMM: Interpretation Guide

Applied Logistic Regression Conventions — Chapter 9 (H&L, 2013)
ModelOR Type (H&L Ch.9)H&L Sentence Opening
GEEPopulation-averaged OR“The estimated population-averaged OR …”
GLMMCluster-specific (subject-specific) OR“The estimated cluster-specific OR …”
  • H&L (§9.1): GEE estimates the population-averaged effect — the OR averaged over the distribution of random effects in the population.
  • H&L (§9.2): GLMM estimates the cluster-specific (subject-specific) effect — the OR for a given cluster holding its random effect constant.
  • For binary outcomes, the cluster-specific OR (GLMM) is always larger in absolute magnitude than the population-averaged OR (GEE).
  • Always report: working correlation structure (GEE); variance component σ²b and ICC (GLMM).
  • Use “estimated” before OR and “controlling for” before covariates, consistent with H&L style.
🔷 Categorical Predictor — H&L Template (Chapter 9)

GEE (population-averaged): “The estimated population-averaged odds ratio of [outcome] for subjects with [Category A] is Ψ̂PA = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], times the estimated odds for subjects with [Reference B], controlling for [covariates] in the model, using GEE with [exchangeable/AR-1/unstructured] working correlation structure and robust (sandwich) standard errors.”

GLMM (cluster-specific): “The estimated cluster-specific odds ratio of [outcome] for subjects with [Category A] is Ψ̂CS = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], times the estimated odds for subjects with [Reference B], within the same cluster (random effect held constant), controlling for [covariates] in the model.”

Categorical Example — Prior Fracture on Subsequent Fracture (GLOW_RAND)

GEE: Population-averaged OR = 1.89, 95% CI 1.34–2.67, p = 0.001, exchangeable working correlation.

H&L GEE sentence: “The estimated population-averaged odds ratio of subsequent fracture for women with prior fracture is Ψ̂PA = 1.89 (95% CI: 1.34–2.67), p = 0.001, times the estimated odds for women without prior fracture, controlling for age and BMI in the model, using GEE with exchangeable working correlation and robust standard errors.”

GLMM: Cluster-specific OR = 2.24, 95% CI 1.54–3.26, p < 0.001. σ²b = 0.71, ICC = 0.18.

H&L GLMM sentence: “The estimated cluster-specific odds ratio of subsequent fracture for women with prior fracture is Ψ̂CS = 2.24 (95% CI: 1.54–3.26), p < 0.001, times the estimated odds for women without prior fracture within the same clinic (random effect held constant), controlling for age and BMI in the model. The estimated between-clinic variance was σ²b = 0.71 (ICC = 0.18), indicating that 18% of the residual variability in fracture risk is attributable to clinic-level differences.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor — H&L Template

GEE: “The estimated population-averaged odds ratio of [outcome] for a c-unit increase in [predictor X] is exp(c × β̂GEE) = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates] in the model, using GEE with [working correlation] and robust standard errors.”

GLMM: “The estimated cluster-specific odds ratio of [outcome] for a c-unit increase in [predictor X] is exp(c × β̂GLMM) = [OR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], within the same cluster, controlling for [covariates] in the model.”

Numerical Example — BMI (c = 1 kg/m²) on Fracture (GEE)

Output: Population-averaged OR = 0.96, 95% CI 0.93–0.99, p = 0.012.

H&L sentence: “The estimated population-averaged odds ratio of fracture for a 1 kg/m² increase in BMI is exp(β̂GEE) = 0.96 (95% CI: 0.93–0.99), p = 0.012, controlling for prior fracture and age in the model, using GEE with exchangeable working correlation and robust standard errors. A 1 kg/m² increase in BMI is associated with a 4% reduction in the estimated population-averaged odds of fracture.”

🔀 Interaction Term — H&L Template

H&L (§9.3 / §3.4): when an interaction is present, the estimated OR depends on the value of the effect modifier:

Ψ̂(A | B = b) = exp(β̂A + β̂AB × b)

GEE: “Because the product term [A] × [B] is significant (p = [p]), the estimated population-averaged OR for [A] is not constant. At [B = level 1]: Ψ̂PA = [OR₁] (95% CI: …); at [B = level 2]: Ψ̂PA = [OR₂] (95% CI: …), controlling for [covariates] in the model.”

GLMM: “Within the same cluster, the estimated cluster-specific OR for [A] varies with [B] (interaction p = [p]): at [B = level 1]: Ψ̂CS = [OR₁]; at [B = level 2]: Ψ̂CS = [OR₂].”

Interaction Example — Prior Fracture × Age Group on Fracture (GEE)

Output: Interaction OR = 1.48, 95% CI 1.09–2.01, p = 0.012. Stratum OR (elderly): 2.80; (younger): 1.89.

H&L sentence: “Because the product term prior fracture × age group is significant (p = 0.012), the estimated population-averaged OR for prior fracture is not constant across age groups. Among elderly women: Ψ̂PA = exp(β̂fx + β̂fx×age) = 2.80 (95% CI: …); among younger women: Ψ̂PA = exp(β̂fx) = 1.89 (95% CI: 1.34–2.67), controlling for BMI in the model, using GEE with exchangeable working correlation. Stratum-specific ORs are presented as the interaction precludes a single summary estimate (H&L §3.4).”

R Code
library(geepack); library(lme4); library(performance)

# GEE (population-averaged OR)
m_gee <- geeglm(fracture ~ prior_fx + bmi + agegroup,
                id=clinic_id, family=binomial, corstr="exchangeable", data=df)
exp(coef(m_gee)); exp(confint(m_gee))
summary(m_gee)         # robust (sandwich) SE used automatically

# GEE interaction + stratum-specific ORs (H&L §3.4)
m_gee2 <- geeglm(fracture ~ prior_fx * agegroup + bmi,
                 id=clinic_id, family=binomial, corstr="exchangeable", data=df)
anova(m_gee, m_gee2)   # Wald test for interaction
b <- coef(m_gee2)
exp(b["prior_fx1"])                               # OR (younger, ref)
exp(b["prior_fx1"] + b["prior_fx1:agegroupelderly"])  # OR (elderly)

# GLMM (cluster-specific OR)
m_glmm <- glmer(fracture ~ prior_fx + bmi + agegroup + (1|clinic_id),
                family=binomial, nAGQ=20, data=df)
exp(fixef(m_glmm))          # cluster-specific OR
exp(confint(m_glmm, parm="beta_"))
icc(m_glmm)                 # ICC from between-cluster variance

T6 — Stratified Cox Proportional Hazards: Interpretation Guide (HR)

3-Element Rule
  1. HR + direction — instantaneous hazard at any time point
  2. 95% CI
  3. p-value + PH assumption (Schoenfeld) + “controlling for … stratified by …”

HR assumes constant ratio over time. Always report cox.zph() result. If PH violated for a variable → stratify by it.

🔷 Categorical Predictor Template

“[Group A] had a hazard of [event] that was [HR] times [higher / lower] compared to [Reference Group B] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates] and stratifying by [variable]. The proportional hazards assumption was [met / violated] (Schoenfeld residuals, p = [p]).”

HR > 1: “[HR−1×100]% higher hazard at any time point.”
HR < 1: “[1−HR×100]% lower hazard (risk reduction).”

Categorical Example — Treatment on Death (stratified by centre)

Output: HR = 0.62, 95% CI 0.44–0.87, p = 0.006. Schoenfeld global p = 0.41.

Interpretation: “Patients receiving experimental treatment had a 38% lower hazard of death compared to those on standard treatment (HR = 0.62, 95% CI: 0.44–0.87, p = 0.006), controlling for age and disease stage, and stratifying by centre. The proportional hazards assumption was confirmed (Schoenfeld residuals, global p = 0.41).”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor Template

“For every one unit increase in [continuous predictor], the hazard of [event] [increased / decreased] by a factor of [HR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], controlling for [covariates] and stratifying by [variable].”

Tip: scale predictor for clinical relevance (e.g., per 10-year increase in age, per 5 kg/m² BMI).

Numerical Example — Age (per 10 years) on Survival

Output: HR = 1.42, 95% CI 1.28–1.57, p < 0.001. Schoenfeld for age: p = 0.18.

Interpretation: “For every 10-year increase in age, the hazard of death increased by 42% (HR = 1.42, 95% CI: 1.28–1.57, p < 0.001), controlling for treatment and disease stage, and stratifying by centre. The proportional hazards assumption for age was satisfied (p = 0.18).”

🔀 Interaction Term Template

Covariate–covariate: “The effect of [Predictor A] on the hazard of [event] was modified by [Predictor B] (interaction HR = [value], 95% CI, p = [p]). Stratum-specific HRs: [B=1]: HR = [HR₁]; [B=2]: HR = [HR₂].”

Time-varying (PH violated): “[Predictor A] showed a time-varying effect (interaction with log-time: p = [p]). In the early period [0–t months], HR = [HR₁]; after t months, HR = [HR₂].”

Interaction Example — Treatment × Disease Stage on Survival

Output: Interaction HR = 1.68, 95% CI 1.12–2.52, p = 0.012.

Interpretation: “The survival benefit of experimental treatment was modified by disease stage (interaction HR = 1.68, 95% CI: 1.12–2.52, p = 0.012). In early-stage patients, experimental treatment conferred a 51% lower hazard of death (HR = 0.49); in advanced-stage patients the benefit was attenuated (HR = 0.82), suggesting treatment is most effective in early disease.”

R Code
library(survival)
df$age10 <- df$age / 10   # per 10 years

# Categorical + Numerical
m1 <- coxph(Surv(time, event) ~ treatment + age10 + stage + strata(centre), data=df)
summary(m1)            # HR, 95% CI, p-value
cox.zph(m1)            # PH test per covariate + global (want p > 0.05)

# Covariate interaction
m2 <- coxph(Surv(time, event) ~ treatment * stage + age10 + strata(centre), data=df)
anova(m1, m2)          # LR test for interaction

# Time-varying effect (when PH violated for treatment)
m3 <- coxph(Surv(time, event) ~ treatment + tt(treatment) + age10 + strata(centre),
            tt=function(x,t,...) x*log(t+1), data=df)

T7 — Parametric AFT: Interpretation Guide (Time Ratio / TR)

3-Element Rule
  1. TR + direction — ratio of expected survival times (TR > 1 = longer survival = protective)
  2. 95% CI
  3. p-value + distribution + “controlling for …”

TR = exp(β) from survreg. Positive β in AFT = protective (opposite sign convention vs Cox). Choose distribution by lowest AIC.

🔷 Categorical Predictor Template

“[Group A] had a survival time that was [TR] times [longer / shorter] compared to [Reference Group B] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], based on a [Weibull / log-logistic / log-normal] AFT model, controlling for [covariates].”

TR > 1: “[A] experienced [TR−1×100]% longer expected time to [event].”
TR < 1: “[A] experienced [1−TR×100]% shorter expected time (accelerated failure).”

Categorical Example — Intensive Therapy on Relapse-Free Survival

Output: TR = 1.74, 95% CI 1.23–2.46, p = 0.002, Weibull AFT.

Interpretation: “Patients receiving intensive therapy had a relapse-free survival time 1.74 times longer compared to standard therapy (95% CI: 1.23–2.46, p = 0.002), based on a Weibull AFT model, controlling for age and baseline severity. This represents a 74% increase in expected time to relapse.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Predictor Template

“For every one unit increase in [continuous predictor], the expected survival time [increased / decreased] by a factor of [TR] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), p = [p-value], based on a [distribution] AFT model, controlling for [covariates].”

Scale predictor if one unit is clinically trivial (e.g., per 10-unit increase in symptom score).

Numerical Example — Baseline Severity (per 10 points) on Time to Relapse

Output: TR = 0.87, 95% CI 0.79–0.96, p = 0.004, log-logistic AFT.

Interpretation: “For every 10-point increase in baseline severity, the expected time to relapse decreased by 13% (TR = 0.87, 95% CI: 0.79–0.96, p = 0.004), based on a log-logistic AFT model, controlling for treatment and age. Higher baseline severity was associated with accelerated time to relapse.”

🔀 Interaction Term Template

“The effect of [Predictor A] on expected survival time was modified by [Predictor B] (interaction TR = [value], 95% CI: [lower]–[upper], p = [p-value]). Among those with [B = level 1], TR for [A] = [TR₁]; among [B = level 2], TR = [TR₂], based on a [distribution] AFT model.”

Interaction Example — Therapy × Sex on Relapse Time

Output: Interaction TR = 1.31, 95% CI 1.06–1.62, p = 0.013, Weibull AFT.

Interpretation: “The effect of intensive therapy on relapse-free survival was significantly modified by sex (interaction TR = 1.31, 95% CI: 1.06–1.62, p = 0.013). Among females, intensive therapy extended expected relapse-free time by 128% (TR = 2.28) compared to 43% in males (TR = 1.43), based on a Weibull AFT model, suggesting greater treatment benefit in females.”

R Code
library(survival); library(flexsurv)
df$severity10 <- df$severity / 10   # scale predictor

# Distribution selection — pick lowest AIC
mw  <- flexsurvreg(Surv(time,event) ~ therapy + severity10 + age, data=df, dist="weibull")
mll <- flexsurvreg(Surv(time,event) ~ therapy + severity10 + age, data=df, dist="llogis")
mln <- flexsurvreg(Surv(time,event) ~ therapy + severity10 + age, data=df, dist="lnorm")
AIC(mw, mll, mln)

# Fit chosen model (e.g., Weibull) + interaction
m1 <- survreg(Surv(time,event) ~ therapy + severity10 + age + sex, data=df, dist="weibull")
m2 <- survreg(Surv(time,event) ~ therapy * sex + severity10 + age, data=df, dist="weibull")
anova(m1, m2)          # LR test
exp(coef(m1))          # TR (time ratios); exp(coef(m2)) includes interaction TR

T8 — ML Classification: Interpretation Guide (Performance + Feature Importance)

Core Metrics — Quick Reference
MetricFormulaMeaning
Accuracy(TP+TN)/NOverall correct classification rate
SensitivityTP/(TP+FN)Cases correctly found
SpecificityTN/(TN+FP)Non-cases correctly excluded
AUC-ROCArea under ROCDiscrimination: 0.5=chance, 1=perfect

AUC benchmarks: <0.7=poor; 0.7–0.8=fair; 0.8–0.9=good; >0.9=excellent. For imbalanced data, also report sensitivity and AUC — accuracy alone is misleading.

🔷 Categorical Feature Interpretation (Variable Importance)

“The categorical variable [Feature X] was among the [top N / most important] predictors in the [algorithm] model (variable importance score = [value]). [Level A] was associated with [higher / lower] predicted probability of [outcome] compared to [Level B], as indicated by [partial dependence plots / SHAP values].”

Decision tree: “The first split in the classification tree was on [Feature X] at [level/threshold], with [Level A] branching toward [outcome = 1] (n = [N], probability = [p]) and [Level B] toward [outcome = 0].”

Categorical Example — Sex in Random Forest for Disease Classification

Output: MDA importance for sex = 12.4 (ranked 3rd of 10 features).

Interpretation: “Sex was the third most important predictor in the Random Forest model (mean decrease in accuracy = 12.4). Partial dependence plots indicated that female sex was associated with higher predicted probability of disease classification, consistent with known biological risk differences.”

🔢 Numerical (Continuous) Feature Interpretation

“The continuous variable [Feature X] ranked [N]th in variable importance (score = [value]). The partial dependence plot revealed a [linear / non-linear / threshold] relationship: predicted probability of [outcome] [increased / decreased] with [higher] values of [Feature X], with the most notable inflection around [threshold value].”

For logistic regression used as ML baseline: apply standard OR numerical template. Report OR per unit or per meaningful increment.

Numerical Example — Age in Random Forest for Diabetes

Output: Gini importance = 0.089 (ranked 1st). Partial dependence plot: monotone increase above age 45.

Interpretation: “Age was the most influential predictor (Gini importance = 0.089). The partial dependence plot demonstrated a monotonically increasing relationship between age and predicted diabetes probability, with the steepest increase observed between 40 and 55 years, suggesting 45 years as a clinically meaningful threshold.”

🔀 Interaction Detection Template

“An interaction between [Feature A] and [Feature B] was identified through [SHAP interaction values / H-statistic / 2D partial dependence plot]. The joint effect of [A] and [B] on predicted [outcome] was [greater / smaller] than the sum of their individual effects, suggesting [synergism / antagonism].”

For tree-based models: “Co-occurrence of [Feature A = value 1] and [Feature B = value 2] in the same terminal node across [N] trees indicates a learned interaction, with [outcome = 1] rate of [Y]% in that leaf.”

Interaction Example — Age × BMI in Random Forest for Diabetes

Output: SHAP interaction value (age × BMI) = 0.031 (highest pairwise interaction).

Interpretation: “SHAP interaction analysis identified age and BMI as the most influential interacting pair (SHAP interaction value = 0.031). Among individuals aged >50 years with BMI ≥30 kg/m², predicted diabetes probability was markedly higher than predicted by either feature alone, suggesting a synergistic effect of older age and obesity that the model captured through joint node splits.”

Model Comparison Paragraph Template

“Five classification algorithms were evaluated: [list]. The [Best Algorithm] achieved the highest AUC-ROC of [value] (95% CI: [lower]–[upper]), with sensitivity [X]%, specificity [X]%, PPV [X]%, and NPV [X]% on the holdout test set, outperforming [other algorithms] (AUC range: [range]). Ten-fold cross-validation confirmed stability (mean AUC = [value], SD = [SD]). Therefore, [Best Algorithm] was selected as the final model.”

R Code — Feature Importance & Interaction
library(randomForest); library(caret); library(pROC); library(pdp)

# Train + variable importance
m_rf <- randomForest(outcome ~ ., data=train_df, ntree=500, importance=TRUE)
varImpPlot(m_rf)           # MDA and Gini

# Partial dependence (continuous feature)
pd_age <- partial(m_rf, pred.var="age", prob=TRUE)
plotPartial(pd_age)

# 2D PD plot (interaction)
pd_2d <- partial(m_rf, pred.var=c("age","bmi"), prob=TRUE, chull=TRUE)
plotPartial(pd_2d, levelplot=TRUE)

# Performance metrics
cm <- confusionMatrix(predict(m_rf, test_df), test_df$outcome, positive="1")
cm$overall["Accuracy"]
cm$byClass[c("Sensitivity","Specificity","Pos Pred Value","Neg Pred Value")]
roc_obj <- roc(as.numeric(test_df$outcome)-1,
               predict(m_rf, test_df, type="prob")[,"1"])
auc(roc_obj); ci.auc(roc_obj)
